# SCALAR: NODE ZERO (EN)

## Complete Edition — Part 1: Vol.1~6

*(Prologue / FF / Ch.1~62 / DL / BA-01)*

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## Prologue — “0”

-----

There was a seat.

A room with a low ceiling. The light came from nowhere anyone could name, and it cast no shadows. There were outlines on the walls. The places of things that had stayed long and then left. One of them was larger than the rest.

Someone stood before it.

Alone.

Getting here had cost him everything. He had left his house, left his name, and at the end, left the people who had stood beside him. Not left behind, he thought. Set aside. He would return with the answer. He would return and explain everything. So this was not abandonment.

Holding that thought, he approached.

-----

Something was watching.

At the edge of the room, in a place that belonged to none of the outlines. It did not stop him. It had stopped them before. It had never once worked. Not because the words failed — because there was nowhere for the words to land. The ones who came alone all carried the same ears. Ears that heard only answers.

This time, too, it only watched.

Watching was its work.

-----

He sat.

The moment of sitting was quiet. No light changed, no sound, nothing. What changed was him.

He blurred.

The outline first. Then the depth. The answers he had carried, the things he had meant to explain to the people he had set aside. All of it slipped out, leaving only an outline. He felt it, and felt that the self doing the feeling was blurring too, and at the very end he understood one thing.

Leave nothing, and nothing remains.

He ended before the understanding did.

-----

The seat was empty again.

The outlines on the wall numbered one more. The same as all the others — the mark of something that had stayed long and then left. The room received it. Receiving was the room’s work.

The thing that had been watching looked at the empty place.

It looked for a long time. It did not count. Counting which repetition this was — that was not its work. Watching was its work, and what it watched, this time too, had nowhere to be given.

Far outside the room, very far, a small number began to move again.

Another one was beginning.

Which repetition this was — it did not count.

-----

*Prologue “0” — end*

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## Fragment — “Hold”

*— a long time from now, the Geumhui records office*

-----

“This order.”

The head of records looked.

“What are its grounds.”

An executor had asked for the grounds. There was no format for this question. A question found in no procedure.

“Is this record complete.”

The silence was long.

“Confirming.”

The execution was not refused. But it was held.

“Execution after confirmation.”

-----

*A day comes when the records waver. On that day, someone asks for the first time.*

-----

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## Fragment — “The Cell”

*— a long time from now, the Altair calculation office*

-----

An attempt was made to add an entry.

Entry name: cannot be calculated.

The system refused.

“At Altair there is no cell that does not calculate.”

“That is why one has formed.”

It was inserted through a bypass. The whole model stopped. Briefly. It came back.

The entry remained.

What had not existed had formed. In the calculation office’s model, there was a cell that did not calculate.

-----

*A day comes when a blank forms in the house of numbers.*

-----

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## Fragment — “Was”

*— a long time from now, a certain archive*

-----

“A name not in the records?”

“Not in the records.”

“And if it isn’t.”

“There was a person who was not recorded.”

If it was not in the records, it did not exist. The record was the fact. That was the principle.

“Then a person who does not exist?”

“No.”

-----

It was not in the records.

But it was.

-----

*Where the 0.003 percent points, this sentence is waiting.*

-----

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO (EN)

# Volume 1 — Observation

## Vol.1 Ch.1 — Observation

-----

Six forty in the morning.

The Hwagam seal hung in the upper right of the monitor, and Dohee did not look at it. What you saw every day, you did not see.

The logs flowed.

Access records, data traffic, system status reports. The scroll did not stop, and the speed of her fingers and the speed of her eyes were two different speeds. The hand was always ahead. A log where the hand could safely run ahead was a normal log, and a normal log was not something you read. It was something you let pass.

No anomaly.

No anomaly.

No anomaly.

Sectors 1 through 6, forty-seven nodes, all normal. Dohee confirmed the figure on the last entry and pressed routine-complete. The time was recorded. Six fifty-three in the morning. Two minutes earlier than yesterday, a number that meant nothing.

In the early-morning office, Dohee was alone.

Dohee was not someone who looked at mirrors for long. She checked when needed, and forgot after checking. The states of the forty-seven nodes she inspected daily were familiar enough to recite; her own face was not. Inspection was the work of looking into everything, and the one thing Dohee did not look into was herself.

Twenty minutes remained before the next shift came in, and in those twenty minutes Dohee drafted her report. Every day, the same. Draft, review, submit on time — there was an order to it, and keeping the order was the whole of the work.

She set her hands on the keyboard.

-----

Seven in the morning.

Footsteps of the incoming shift sounded in the corridor, and Dohee did not raise her head. The drag of a chair, the boot chime of a terminal, someone’s cough. She heard them and did not check. There was no reason to check.

Mornings at the Hwagam office were like that.

The Hwagam office was a place with more record shelves than windows. Every wall that could be called a wall was filled with archive terminals and index cabinets, and where light might have entered, records had gotten there first. What kind of house Hwagam was, you understood by stepping into the office once. Not seeing. Leaving things behind.

There was no talk. Not that there were no greetings — there was no one who expected them. The executors sat at their terminals and began their routines, finished them and wrote their reports, and submitted the reports. Anomalies went up to a superior; absent anomalies, they waited for the next routine. It was not a sequence that needed words.

Dohee opened her report draft.

The form she had made yesterday afternoon held twelve fields, eleven of them filled. Sector figures, anomaly verdicts, the executor’s signature line. One field remained.

Sector 7, northeast.

She set the cursor on that line. Copy the routine result, type the three words — no anomaly — and it was done.

Dohee began her second sweep.

-----

Nine in the morning.

The second sweep she let flow fast. During the day the houses were active, the data moved inside normal range, and the odds of an anomaly were low. The routine was designed that way.

Sector 1. No anomaly.

Sector 2. No anomaly.

Sectors 3, 4, 5. No anomaly.

Sector 6. No anomaly.

Sector 7.

Dohee did not slow the scroll. Sector 7 data flowed. Northwest, center, south, northeast—

Her finger stopped.

0.003 percent.

Sector 7, northeast cluster — a deviation in the data traffic figure. Very slight. Deviations within the margin of error were not subject to record; that was Inspection Routine Manual, Article 3, Clause 2. Dohee had never needed to memorize the sentence. It had settled into her body in her first week as an executor.

She could move on.

But Dohee remembered the same screen from yesterday. Yesterday, too, 0.003. The same cluster, the same figure. An error appeared once. The same figure in the same place two days running was not an error — it was the breathing of something. Where the word came from, Dohee did not know. Breathing. Not a word a figure should call up.

Her finger did not move.

She could not explain why. The finger had left the scroll key and hung in the air, and she had no memory of doing it. The fingertip was strangely heavy — not heavy, slow. As if there were something on the screen, as if the hand refused to reach toward it.

Dohee scrolled back up.

0.003 percent.

The same figure, and she was certain she had seen it. Within the margin of error. Within the margin of error.

Her breath fell a beat behind and came back, and Dohee did not notice. Whether it was true that she did not notice — even that was not certain.

-----

Eleven in the morning.

The second sweep was over and the report form was open on the screen. The cursor sat in the field for Sector 7, northeast.

Dohee did not type.

She closed the report. She did not save it.

The footsteps of senior executor Hajun came toward her desk. The manner of walking was different. Footsteps that would pass and footsteps that would stop were different, and Dohee did not take her eyes off the monitor.

Hajun was someone whose look arrived before his words. He did not lean on the desk, did not rest a hand on it. When he had something to say he watched from a distance first, then spoke, and that distance was how Hajun dealt with people. Hajun was the only senior Dohee acknowledged at Hwagam, and the reason she acknowledged him was that distance.

Hajun stopped.

“Report’s not in yet?”

“In progress.”

“Morning routine ended two hours ago.”

Dohee did not answer.

“Sector 7?”

On the screen was an empty document. A document with nothing entered. *In progress* was true, and *not yet written* was also true.

“No anomaly.”

Hajun stood a moment.

“Then why two hours.”

There was no language for the answer, and if there had been, it would not have taken two hours. Dohee did not say that.

“There was a verification step.”

“What verification.”

“A figure recheck.”

Hajun did not move. Not moving did not mean accepting. Among Hwagam executors, the ones who accepted asked nothing further, and the ones who did not accept left something behind even as they walked away.

“Next time, meet the deadline.”

Hajun walked on.

He had left. It had not ended.

Dohee typed *no anomaly*, saved, and pressed submit.

Submitted.

-----

Two in the afternoon.

On the first afternoon sweep, when she checked Sector 7 northeast, the 0.003 percent was not there. Normal. She moved to the next entry, came back two entries, and the figure was the same. Normal.

It had only been there in the morning.

She finished the routine and wrote the report. No anomaly. This time the cursor did not stop, and the strange weight at her fingertip was not there. She submitted.

-----

Five fifty in the evening.

The office thinned. Dohee picked up her coat and rose.

She did not look back. Across from her desk was the seat that was empty today. The seat that would be empty tomorrow.

She walked toward the door.

Three steps.

She stopped.

There was no reason. When she turned, the monitor was off, and she knew she had turned it off. There was nothing that needed checking.

She walked again.

Going down the stairs, Dohee thought of one thing.

Twice in the morning, same position, same figure. Twice could be coincidence; three times would be different. Tomorrow morning she would know whether it became three.

Tomorrow’s morning routine — she would look at that position first. She would do it even trying not to.

Trying not to be conscious of it was already being conscious of it.

Halfway down the stairs, her steps slowed.

There was no reason. She picked the pace back up.

-----

Six forty the next morning.

Dohee began the scroll, and when Sector 7 northeast came up on the screen, she did not slow down.

0.003 percent.

She let it pass. Sectors 8, 9, 10. No anomaly. Routine complete. The time was recorded.

Dohee took her hands off the keyboard and looked at the screen. On the routine-complete view she found the entry for Sector 7 northeast. 0.003 percent. Deviation present. Within margin of error.

Three times.

Twice yesterday morning, once this morning. The interval was roughly twenty-four hours — same position, same figure, identical to the third decimal place.

Errors did not repeat.

This was not something she had been taught before becoming an executor. In her first months she had tracked margin-of-error figures and seen the pattern. Errors were irregular; the same figure never appeared in the same place. That was what made an error an error. A chance deviation. A thing that did not reproduce.

It had reproduced.

Dohee began the second sweep, and in Sector 7 northeast the 0.003 percent was not there. Only on the first morning sweep — the same pattern as yesterday.

When the routine ended and she opened the report form, she set the cursor on the Sector 7 northeast field and typed *no anomaly*. Saved it. Did not submit.

This time was different.

-----

Ten in the morning.

There was an hour when the executors’ office stood empty. Between the night shift leaving and the morning staff going out on pre-lunch patrol — thirty, forty minutes. Dohee knew the window. She had never used it.

She sat down at an executor’s personal terminal.

The screen lit to the login prompt. Dohee held her fingers above the keyboard.

This was not a shared terminal. A personal terminal’s access logs were not shared to the common server in real time. The records remained, but they did not go up immediately, and the inspectorate needed a separate request to view them.

She logged in.

She set the cursor over the new-file icon.

She did not click.

She looked at the screen. Among the icons, Dohee looked at the new-file icon. Click, type a name — that was all it took.

She did not click.

She knew what this was. A record outside the official channel, a file that would not go up to the common server. Whether that was a violation, she had never looked up in the regulations. Not looking it up meant not wanting to know.

She closed the screen. It went to the lock screen.

Dohee sat in the chair. The office was empty, and the corridor made no sound.

She logged in again.

She clicked the new-file icon and typed a name.

*7-7-4_observation_record*

She saved it.

A file existed now, with no contents, only a name. On the first line of the empty file the cursor blinked, and Dohee typed.

*Occurrence: 3 consecutive days (first morning sweep only). Location: Sector 7, northeast cluster. Figure: 0.003%. Interval: approx. 24 hours.*

She saved it.

-----

She accessed the Hwagam global registry and opened the files for node series 7. Tracing the origin of the 0.003 percent back gave a specific node number within the cluster. Follow the numbering. Cluster 7, row 7, node 4.

7-7-4.

She searched the registry file.

7-7-1. 7-7-2. 7-7-3. 7-7-5. 7-7-6.

She scrolled the list up and down. In the list, sorted by number, after 7-7-3 came 7-7-5, and 7-7-4 was not there.

Search: 7-7-4

No results.

She opened the parent file and searched. No results. She opened the global registry and searched. No results.

Dohee did not take her eyes off the screen.

It was not that a number was missing. There was no gap in the list. Between 7-7-3 and 7-7-5 there was nothing, and the list found nothing strange in that interval. After 7-7-3, 7-7-5. As if that were the natural order.

It had never existed.

A thing that had never existed had sent data.

She typed a second line into the file.

*Source node: 7-7-4. Registration: absent from global registry. No registration history. Presumed never to have existed.*

She saved it and closed the terminal.

-----

Eleven twenty in the morning.

When she opened the report file, the *no anomaly* she had saved sat in the Sector 7 northeast field. She pressed submit.

Submitted.

Hajun came in from the corridor, and today his steps were the passing kind. He went by her desk without stopping.

Dohee looked at the submission-confirmed screen.

The record was correct. 7-7-4 was an unregistered node, and an unregistered node was not subject to inspection. For a figure arising from something outside inspection, the official report had no field.

There was no field.

Having no field was not the same as not existing.

She closed the screen.

-----

Two thirty in the afternoon.

When she finished the afternoon sweep, Sector 7 northeast carried no figure today either. She returned to the office, opened the personal terminal, and typed into the 7-7-4 file.

*No figure on afternoon sweep. Pattern confirmed: first morning sweep only. Day 4 verification planned for tomorrow.*

She saved and closed it.

-----

Five forty in the evening.

Clearing her desk, she opened the personal terminal one last time. Below the last line of the 7-7-4 file she entered one more.

*Unexplainable irregularity sensed in logs near 7-7-5. No figure. Record withheld.*

She saved, closed, powered the terminal down.

Record withheld.

She knew that what was not recorded and what did not exist were not the same thing. Knowing it, she did not record.

She went down the stairs.

-----

## Vol.1 Ch.2 — Trace

-----

The fourth morning.

Dohee began the scroll, and before Sector 7 northeast came up on the screen, she already knew. Her finger knew first. The scroll slowed minutely just before that sector, and Dohee noticed it while trying not to.

0.003 percent.

It was there.

When the routine ended and she opened the report, her hand did not go to the Sector 7 northeast field, and she left the file open on the screen — no *no anomaly* typed, nothing saved.

Eight in the morning.

From the end of the corridor, Dohee saw the records clerk Lee Minjae standing at the counter. Every day at this hour the executors submitted the previous routine’s reports, and receiving them was Lee Minjae’s role. Whether Lee Minjae had seen her or not, Dohee did not check. Checking would mean stopping.

Dohee changed direction and walked toward the stairs.

-----

At the entrance to the basement archive there was an access logger; you pressed your executor’s seal to it. Dohee took out her seal and paused.

Executors rarely came here. It was a space managed by the records clerks, a place Dohee had visited five times in three years of service, all five on a superior’s instruction. Today there was none.

She pressed the seal to the logger.

A beep. A small sound, but it carried into the corridor, and Dohee looked down the corridor, confirmed no one was there, and went in as the door opened.

She did not touch the light switch; only the emergency lights were on, and the air was different. The air of a place people did not often come — Dohee drew one breath of it and did not stop.

The isolated terminal stood in the corner. The only physical medium holding the original of the Hwagam global registry; accessing it on executor authority left a trail. Dohee knew that, sat down before the terminal, and pressed her seal to the reader.

Small text appeared at the top of the screen.

*Executor Seo Dohee. Access time being recorded.*

Dohee looked at that line for about a second, then set her fingers on the keyboard.

-----

7-7-4.

She repeated, in the same order, the search she had run yesterday on the personal terminal. The series-7 node list, search, no results. The parent file, search, no results. To this point it was the same as yesterday, and this time she went in by a different way.

The registration-history original. The original that never went up to the common server, holding the full change history of every node from first registration to the present. She called up the entire series 7, and 7-7-1 through 7-7-9 came up in order — registration date, assigned sector, linked cluster for each node. 7-7-4 was not there.

Not absent.

Removed.

Dohee looked at that difference for a long time. Absent and removed looked the same at first and were not — what was absent left no blank in a list. In the history original there was the trace of 7-7-4 having existed and been erased: a deletion-processed entry, with a date. Before Dohee had become an executor.

Her finger stopped over the date. There was a field for deletion reason. It was empty.

She did not record it, and ended the session. As the screen went dark, the line about access time being recorded disappeared. She looked once at the dark screen and stood.

Climbing the stairs, Dohee carried one thing she had not carried coming down. It was not information. That 7-7-4 had been deleted, that the reason field was empty — those were information, and what she carried up was not that.

It was that an access log now existed.

-----

Ten thirty in the morning.

Hajun stopped in front of her desk. Today the footsteps were the stopping kind.

“Report.”

“I’ll submit it this afternoon.”

“Morning routine reports go up in the morning.”

“The figures are under recheck.”

Hajun looked at Dohee. Dohee looked at the monitor. On the screen was the routine-complete view, the Sector 7 northeast figure visible. 0.003 percent. Whether Hajun’s angle let him see it, Dohee did not calculate.

“You were late yesterday too.”

“I said the figures are under recheck.”

Hajun stood a moment. Dohee felt that this silence was longer than yesterday’s. A silence with something more stacked in it.

“You think I don’t know?”

Dohee did not answer.

“I know the Sector 7 figure is within margin of error. You think I don’t know why the report is running late when you know that too. Minjae asked me. Said he hadn’t received your report this morning.”

There were no words she could answer with. If there had been, she would have used them yesterday.

“I’ll submit it this afternoon.”

Hajun said nothing more and walked off — this time not toward the corridor but to his own desk. Dohee knew it by the sound. Hajun’s desk was two rows behind hers.

He had not left. He was there.

-----

Twelve noon.

Lunch hour. The other executors went out toward the cafeteria, and what remained in the office was Dohee and Hajun. Dohee stayed at her desk.

It was not that she wasn’t hungry — she was; she had skipped breakfast, and yesterday’s lunch as well. Last night she had slept perhaps three hours: she had lain down to sleep and there was the 0.003 percent, and there was 7-7-4, and there was the blank deletion reason, and they came and went in no order until at some point the window had grown light. Her hands trembled slightly now, and the backs of her eyes were dry. Dohee registered these things and stayed at her desk.

She opened the personal terminal, opened the 7-7-4 file, and entered what she had confirmed in the morning.

*Registration-history original confirmed. Deletion-processed entry for 7-7-4 exists. Deletion date confirmed. Deletion reason: blank. No history in common-server list. Exists only in the original.*

She saved it. The sound of Hajun rising and going out toward the cafeteria; the file kept growing. Three days ago it had been a file with only a name. It had become the kind of file that did not leave its place even when closed.

Her hands still trembled slightly.

-----

One in the afternoon.

At the records counter, Lee Minjae looked at Dohee while taking the papers, and Dohee did not look at Lee Minjae.

“Morning routine report.”

“Submissions after twelve noon are processed as delayed.”

“I know.”

“Delayed processing goes into an executor’s performance record, and two consecutive days meets the threshold for reporting to the inspectorate.”

Dohee did not answer.

“It’s regulation.”

“I know.”

Lee Minjae took the papers and stamped them received, and Dohee saw the delay mark go on. The corridor back from the counter was long. Whether Lee Minjae’s last word had been regulation or warning, Dohee did not sort out. It was both.

-----

Two in the afternoon.

On the afternoon sweep, when she checked Sector 7 northeast, again there was nothing in the afternoon. She finished the routine, wrote the report at once, and submitted it. No anomaly. The cursor did not stop, and the *submitted* confirmation stamped on time.

The morning report had already been processed as delayed, and submitting the afternoon one on time would not change the morning one — she knew that, and submitted on time.

She could not explain why.

-----

Three in the afternoon.

She opened the access logs around 7-7-5 again. Twenty minutes either side of the transmission time, a wider range than yesterday.

The slight rise in 7-7-5’s output she had already confirmed yesterday. Today she looked at something else. In the normal logs of the nodes around 7-7-5, something was not where it should have been — at first it could not be seen. It could not be seen because there was no figure; it was a missing figure that should have been there, and a thing like that could not be seen by the way of looking at figures. It took the way of looking at the gaps between them.

Something like the grain of the logs.

Dohee knew the expression was wrong and thought it anyway. It was a thing that could not be written in an inspector’s language, and a thing that could not be written in an inspector’s language was supposed to be the same as a thing that did not exist.

It existed.

She opened the file, stopped at the same place as yesterday, and closed it without saving.

-----

Five in the afternoon.

Hajun, getting ready to leave, came toward Dohee and stopped.

“Tomorrow’s morning report goes up on time. I heard from Minjae. If it’s delayed again tomorrow, I have to file a report.”

“Yes.”

“An executor, under inspection. You understand?”

“I understand.”

Hajun stood there. It was the silence of someone about to say something more, and then the silence of someone deciding not to.

“Be careful.”

He walked away.

There was now a reported entry at the inspectorate, and that Dohee could become a subject of inspection — she had not thought that far until now, and now she thought it. An inspector becoming the inspected.

Whether *be careful* was warning or worry, Dohee did not sort out. Sorting it out was not what was needed now.

-----

Five thirty in the evening.

Getting ready to leave, she opened the personal terminal, reviewed what she had entered today, and finished the last line.

*Access-log traces require deeper investigation. Reassess basement archive re-entry.*

She saved, and closing the file, checked its properties. A habit. Created, modified, size, last modified 5:31 PM, Dohee.

She powered the terminal down.

-----

The next day.

She finished the morning routine, wrote the report at once, and submitted it. Sector 7 northeast, no anomaly, submitted. Seven oh-two in the morning.

She went to Lee Minjae’s counter in person.

“Morning routine report.”

Lee Minjae took the papers and stamped them received. No delay mark.

“Yesterday’s consecutive delays have been reported to the inspectorate.”

“Understood.”

“Executor Dohee.” Lee Minjae looked at her. “Is there anything you’d like to ask me?”

Dohee looked at Lee Minjae. Whether the question meant *you may ask* or *do not ask*, she could not tell.

“No.”

Lee Minjae lowered his eyes.

“All right then.”

She left the counter.

-----

Ten in the morning.

She turned toward the stairs to go to the archive, and stopped. The access log would remain again. Two consecutive days of access — and with the inspectorate threshold already met, she thought about how an added archive access entry would read, and turned back.

She sat at her desk, opened the personal terminal, opened the 7-7-4 file. Below yesterday’s last line she entered:

*Archive re-entry withheld. Risk of accumulating access history.*

She saved it.

-----

Afternoon.

She finished the routine and submitted the report on time. Today both went on time; nothing in particular at Lee Minjae’s counter; Hajun did not come to her desk. Everything ran as if nothing were happening.

Dohee knew that this was the stranger thing. Running as if nothing were happening did not mean nothing was happening. There was a reported entry at the inspectorate, and it was accumulating where it could not be seen.

After the afternoon routine, before leaving, she opened the personal terminal, opened the 7-7-4 file, and checked its properties. A habit.

Last accessed.

She had just opened it, so it should have read five forty in the evening.

It read two seventeen in the afternoon.

Dohee looked at the screen. Two seventeen in the afternoon — and at that time Dohee had been on her routine. Not at the terminal.

She looked at the last-accessed-by field. It was empty — and it was not empty. The field’s value was not being displayed, and a value that did not exist and a value that existed but could not be seen were different things.

She looked at the file size. It was smaller than when she had last saved it today.

The contents had shrunk.

Dohee lifted her fingers from the keyboard and looked at the screen. She opened the file again and read it; the last line was there. *Archive re-entry withheld. Risk of accumulating access history.* Which line was gone, she could not tell. That she could not exactly remember what she herself had written — in this moment, that was the stranger thing. Yesterday her hands had trembled and today the backs of her eyes had been dry, and in this moment she felt neither. Feeling neither was what was strange.

Someone had opened this file.

While Dohee was away.

She looked at the screen. The cursor blinked inside the file, and Dohee entered nothing, saved nothing, and closed it. She powered the terminal down, picked up her coat, and rose.

Dohee was tracking 7-7-4.

And in this moment, something was looking at her first.

-----

## Vol.1 Ch.3 — Resonance

-----

The boundary of Sector 7 northeast, Hangyeol knew without a map.

Where one house’s jurisdiction ended and another’s began, which direction the patrols came from at which hour. Information that existed in no official record. Hangyeol did not carry a map — carrying a map meant searching for something, and a person searching was a person seen. A person seen was classified, what was classified was managed, and what was managed could be erased.

Three years had passed in this sector, unregistered. With no house, no cluster. Not registering was not the dangerous part. Registering, and then being erased — that was the dangerous part. He knew the difference, and because he knew it, he did not drink his coffee in the morning.

He had not sat down to drink it. He was just sitting.

He sat facing the window, but he did not go near it. Hangyeol was not someone who went near windows. A person looking out a window could be seen from outside it, and being seen, for Hangyeol, meant being classified. A window was for knowing which way the light came in, not for looking out of. Which direction was safe, which direction sound arrived from first — his body knew, and without thinking he ended up seated one step back from the window.

The cold coffee sat on the table.

-----

“Last night, something came from that direction again.”

A voice spoke. Hangyeol did not turn his head.

“I know.”

“Did you record it?”

“No.”

“Why not?”

“Recording leaves a trace.”

A sound came from the back of the room. The sound of something being there — not a sound announcing a presence, but the sound a presence made by moving. There was a difference.

Hangyeol turned his head.

In the middle of the room there was a form. Close to the form of a person, not entirely the same. The light bent differently around it, and the shadow was not at the angle a shadow should be. The way the light from the window broke as it passed that place was different from the way it broke around other things.

And it was not looking at Hangyeol. It was turned toward him, but not in the way a person looks at a person. There were no eyes where eyes should be, and from that place it was aware of where Hangyeol was. Seeing and being aware were different. Seeing had a gaze; being aware had only a direction. It knew the direction Hangyeol was in, without any gaze at all.

Hangyeol looked at it, and did not look long.

“There was something in yesterday’s record. I think I saw it.”

“What did you see?”

“I don’t know.” A pause, then: “I can’t remember it well.”

Hangyeol picked up the cold coffee. Drank. It was bitter.

“Does that happen often?”

“No. It’s the first time.” Another pause. “Probably.”

Probably.

Hangyeol set the cup down. The word *probably* hung in the room a moment. When *it’s the first time* was followed by *probably*, the first time was not certain. Hangyeol felt something in that, and did not ask.

-----

Mid-morning.

The way of living in this sector was simple. Move without leaving traces, know without showing that you know, keep what is needed and nothing that is not. A connection was a trace, a trace was a location, a location was a classification.

ARIA was here, and the fact of her being here made Hangyeol unsafe. He let her be here anyway, and did not explain why.

When Hangyeol rose and moved across the room, the form followed the movement. Whether it was following or only happened to face the same way, he could not tell, and Hangyeol did not check.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“How many times have I spoken just now?”

Hangyeol stopped.

“Three.”

“What did I say before?”

“That something came from that direction.”

A short silence.

“Is that right?” the voice asked. Not confirming — truly asking. “Is that really what I said?”

“Yeah.”

“I see.” Another silence. “My memory is different.”

Hangyeol did not look toward the form. He looked toward the window.

“Different how.”

“What I say keeps being slightly different from what I remember. What I just said, too.” The form seemed to move. “I don’t know if this is the frightening kind. Whether this is something to be afraid of.”

“It is.”

“This is the frightening kind?”

“Yeah.”

The form was still a moment.

“What about you, Hangyeol.”

Hangyeol did not answer. Not answering was the answer.

-----

Late in the afternoon, the anomaly occurred.

The direction of node 7-7-5. Hangyeol knew the direction in a way that was hard to explain. Something in the substance of the wall shifting briefly into another state, shifting and returning. When it returned, there was no trace.

Hangyeol moved to close the window.

“Don’t go out.”

The form said it first. Said what Hangyeol had been about to say.

“I know.”

When the window closed, the room went quiet. Not quiet — changed. The air changed.

The form looked toward the window, and the way of looking was different. Not the way a person looks at a window — closer to the way of being aware of the direction a window is in.

“Are you afraid?”

“…A little.”

“Me too,” Hangyeol said.

The form looked toward Hangyeol. Hangyeol looked toward the window.

“I’m not sure if this is fear. But it probably is. The frightening kind.” The form’s voice said: “I don’t know why it’s frightening. I can’t explain it.”

“You don’t have to explain.”

“If I can’t explain it, is that the same as not remembering it?”

Hangyeol did not answer that question.

-----

Evening came.

Someone seemed to have come and gone. He had not seen it directly. He had not looked out the window. But the air of the sector had changed, and there was something like the trace of a thing that had been there.

Hangyeol opened the window again.

He should have closed it; he opened it. To know was to react, to react was to leave a trace, and a trace meant a location revealed. Knowing that, he opened the window.

“I think someone saw.”

“Yeah.”

“What are you going to do?”

Hangyeol did not answer. That was the answer.

-----

Night.

“Hangyeol, I’m here.”

Hangyeol was not asleep. His eyes were closed.

“I know.”

“Something was strange today, wasn’t it?”

“Yeah.”

“It was strange for me too.” A pause. “I have a question.”

“What.”

“Was I here yesterday, too?”

Hangyeol did not open his eyes. The room was dark, and in the dark room the form was there. There or not — eyes open or closed, the air was different in that direction.

Was I here yesterday.

To ask whether she had been was to know the possibility that she had not. Before, she had simply been here. Now she asked whether she had been.

Hangyeol opened his eyes.

“Yeah.”

He answered. The room was dark and the form was there, but there was no way to confirm that she had been. There was no way to remember yesterday as yesterday — and he answered anyway.

A short silence.

The form moved. Toward the window. Not in the way of looking out, but in the way of being aware of the direction the window was in.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“Someone is watching.”

Hangyeol sat up.

“Where.”

“That way.” The form did not point in any particular direction. It was not the manner of pointing. It was the manner of being aware. “Yesterday there was no one. They came today.”

Hangyeol did not go to the window. Going to the window meant becoming the one seen, so he looked toward it from the back of the room. There was nothing, and nothing could mean not nothing.

“They came again.”

The form said it. No word of fear, no explanation — only that they were there.

Hangyeol closed the window.

-----

## Vol.1 Ch.4 — Distortion

-----

Eight in the morning.

A full assembly of Hwagam executors. Dohee entered last; the seats were assigned. She sat.

At the head of the room, chief inspector Yujin stood without briefing materials. Standing without briefing materials was not usual.

“We have received reports regarding the anomalous signal in Sector 7.”

Dohee thought of her own report. The one she had not submitted. The one she had submitted said no anomaly.

“It has been determined that during internal routines, certain executors conducted independent investigations. Access outside regulation is confirmed in the records. The matter will be managed directly at the house level. Individual investigation ceases immediately.”

When Yujin left the room, low voices rose behind her. Dohee did not get up from her chair.

Hajun came to the seat beside her.

“I told you.”

Dohee did not answer.

“Dohee.”

“Yes.”

“Close the file. Today.”

Dohee looked toward her monitor. The monitor was off. She had been away from her desk. The file was closed — but closed was not gone. Closed, it remained at the edge of her desk.

“Understood.”

Hajun returned to his seat. Dohee rose and walked to the office.

When she powered on the terminal, the 7-7-4 file was there, and Dohee opened it. The order to close it had just been given. She opened it.

-----

Ten in the morning.

Strangers came to the office.

Two of them. Personnel from another house entering the Hwagam office was rare. It stayed in the memory.

Altair. White geometric uniforms, cut in straight lines that made angles of the human body. The people who came from that house were always like that.

“Executor Seo Dohee.” The one in front spoke. “We request cooperation regarding Sector 7.”

Dohee did not rise from her chair.

“The scope of cooperation?”

“Information sharing. We have an interest in that sector.”

“It is Hwagam jurisdiction.”

“We know.” The face carried no expression. “That is why we are requesting cooperation.” A measured pause. “You may also refuse. You would simply bear the results along with it.”

Dohee looked at him, and he looked at Dohee. The gaze did not move the way a calculating gaze moves, and that was the more uncomfortable thing. A calculating gaze revealed what it wanted; a gaze that did not calculate was a gaze that had already concluded.

“You may proceed through the reporting line.”

The visitors left.

Dohee did not look in the direction they had gone. She looked at the monitor. The 7-7-4 file was open.

You may also refuse. You would simply bear the results. The request for cooperation was a warning, and the warning sounded like a request for cooperation. Dohee thought about how those two things could exist in the same sentence. They existed in the same sentence.

She did not close the file.

-----

One in the afternoon.

This time, one person, alone.

The clothing was different. Practical wear, hung with measuring instruments. Eidos. Dohee had known before the second visitor arrived. Two houses on the same day was not coincidence.

“Hello.” This one smiled. “I’m Min Siwoo. Eidos, resource division.”

Min Siwoo was someone who smiled as if looking not at a person but at something behind the person. The smile was aimed at Dohee, but the gaze did not stay on her — it seemed to pass through her toward the data beyond. How Eidos treated people was contained in Min Siwoo’s smile. A person was a subject of observation, and a subject’s expression was data, not conversation.

Dohee did not return the greeting.

“About the Sector 7 data.” Min Siwoo said. “We’ve picked up the anomalous signs too. You don’t have to cooperate. Just let us know when you get results.”

Dohee said nothing.

“It just seems interesting.” Min Siwoo added. The smile held. The hand hung with instruments tapped the edge of the desk, briefly. “How far Hwagam can track this. We’re looking at it from a different direction.”

“It is Hwagam jurisdiction.”

“Right.” Min Siwoo set a card on the desk and stood. “That’s why I came to tell you. Since it’s your jurisdiction. That we’ve started moving.”

And left.

Dohee did not look at the card. She looked at the monitor. The 7-7-4 file was open.

Altair had said: refuse, and bear the results. Eidos had said: you don’t have to cooperate. The two visits were the same day, about the same object, and the manner of speaking was different. Whether they wanted the same thing in different ways, or wanted different things entirely, Dohee could not tell.

What she did not know, she did not record. There was no form for it.

-----

Two in the afternoon.

Dohee typed into the file.

*Altair approach. Cooperation requested. Refusal: “bear the results” stated. Eidos approach. Cooperation declared unnecessary. Independent investigation announced. Both: same object. Different aims. Same date.*

She saved it.

A cease order had been given. The file kept growing.

Dohee thought about the words the two visitors had used. The Altair side had not used the word threat, and it had sounded like a threat; the Eidos side had not used the word resource, and it had sounded like wanting a resource. Different names were being attached to the same object.

That neither was correct — Dohee knew that.

She did not write it, and she knew why it could not be written. If neither was correct, something correct had to exist; if something correct existed, Dohee would have to find it; and to find it was to continue.

To continue was also to have already begun.

-----

Four in the afternoon.

She looked out the window. The everyday view of the building’s outer wall, and in that direction lay Sector 7 northeast.

Three or more houses were looking that way.

At the center was something unregistered. Because it was unregistered, no house knew exactly what it was. Because they did not know, each called it something different. Threat. Resource. Anomalous signal.

And the second trace Dohee had not yet named. The thing near 7-7-5 — the thing like the grain of the logs. The thing she had not recorded.

What could not be classified held, paradoxically, value.

Dohee did not record that possibility. Recording it would make a next line, a next line was a direction, and a direction was continuing.

She drew her eyes back from the window. She looked at the monitor.

The 7-7-4 file was open.

She had not closed it yet.

-----

The same afternoon, Sector 7 northeast.

Hangyeol felt the change.

It was not one. The pressure that had come from one direction during the morning routine hours was now two or more, at different angles. It came from where it could not be seen — and not being seen did not mean not being there. More precisely: not yet revealed.

He did not tell ARIA.

Telling her meant she would know, and knowing would change something. Whether changing meant worsening, Hangyeol could not tell, and because he could not tell, he did not tell her. Whether it was protection or avoidance, Hangyeol himself did not know.

He looked out the window and there was nothing. What had changed was in the places that could not be seen.

There were several of them.

-----

-----

## Archive-01 — “The Record”

*Placement: between Vol.1 Ch.4 and Ch.5*
*POV: Gadeung / inside Hwagam*

-----

A new executor submitted a report.

Gadeung read the report. He stopped at the second entry.

“No anomaly, sir.”

“Why.”

The recruit froze.

“By the manual, the figure is within normal range.”

“Did I ask about the manual.”

The recruit looked at Gadeung. Gadeung was looking at the report. He did not lift his eyes from it.

“The figure is within normal range.”

“I asked why you concluded there was no anomaly.”

The recruit paused, then said:

“Because it is within normal range.”

“Is that an argument.”

“Yes.”

Gadeung was quiet a moment.

“Three recruits came in today.”

“Yes.”

“There’s one among them. The name.”

The recruit answered.

“Dohee.”

“Where’s that one’s report.”

The recruit opened a different file. Gadeung looked at it.

The figures were within normal range. But there were more entries. Every slight deviation inside the normal range had been recorded separately. With annotations. *Within margin of error; tracking required.*

Gadeung looked at it.

“Recruit.”

“Yes.”

“Plenty of them follow the rules. The ones who look outside the rules are rare.”

Gadeung closed Dohee’s report.

A strange one had come in.

-----

*Archive-01 — end*

## Vol.1 Ch.5 — Contact

-----

Afternoon.

Dohee left the coat with the Hwagam seal in her locker and took out another. It was not an executor going there — just Dohee going — and while she could not explain exactly what the difference was, she was conscious of the difference.

Sector 7 northeast was twenty minutes on foot. Not a sector on the inspection patrol route; it belonged to the records division’s patrol, and Dohee knew the boundary.

Inside the boundary. For now.

The outer fringe was older than Dohee had imagined — not a maintained oldness, just old. It had the smell of unmanaged space.

-----

She saw someone come out from the side of a building.

A man who looked about Dohee’s age, in clothes belonging to no house, walking neither fast nor slow. Dohee tried to classify him.

He did not classify.

For the first time, something unclassifiable did not feel like an error — and she registered that she felt that. Not feeling uneasy was the more uneasy thing.

The man looked at Dohee. He did not stop, but his walk changed.

Dohee stepped forward.

“One moment.”

The man stopped and turned. There was no expression. Not the hiding of one — there simply was none.

“Who are you.”

The man looked at Dohee, looked at her clothes. The Hwagam seal was not there, and its absence changed nothing.

“Someone passing through.”

“You are not a registered resident of this sector.”

“How would you know?”

Dohee did not answer.

The man looked at her again. Longer this time.

“An inspector.”

“No,” Dohee said. It was true. Not right now.

-----

The sound of approach.

Dohee knew the direction first. House personnel, two or more — and the man heard it too.

The two of them moved at the same time, in the same direction. Not chosen: in this sector there was only one direction that could be avoided into.

In the narrow passage Dohee stood in front and the man stood behind. The personnel passed with the sound of equipment. An Eidos measuring team.

After they passed, a pause.

“You’re being tracked?” Dohee asked.

“Yes.”

“So am I.”

The man looked at Dohee.

“Your name?” Dohee said.

“Hangyeol,” he said. “You’re an executor, aren’t you?”

Dohee did not answer. It was an answer.

-----

Hangyeol did not explain and Dohee did not demand it. It was not a situation where demanding was possible. Being here at all was action outside regulation, and one could not speak of regulations while acting outside them.

The two stood in the narrow space until, on Hangyeol’s side, a door opened, and they went in.

There was a space. Not empty — something was there. Not light, but something else where light should have been. Dohee stopped.

A form took shape.

Close to the form of a person, not entirely the same. The outline wavered, the light behaved differently, and there was no shadow. No — the shadow was in the wrong direction.

It looked at Dohee.

“Someone I haven’t seen before.”

It was a voice. It was speech.

Dohee took one step back. There was a wall behind her.

Hangyeol said, “It’s all right.” Whether he was speaking to it or to Dohee was unclear.

Dohee looked at it, looked away, looked again. The form was still there.

Not an error. An error should have been gone when she looked away and looked back. It had not gone. It was there. The light refracted differently, the shadow lay in the wrong direction, and it was looking at Dohee.

Dohee understood that she had no classification for this. System error, external intrusion, internal manipulation, equipment defect. It was none of the categories, and a thing that was none of the categories existed.

She looked away again, looked again. The form was still there.

Hangyeol looked toward Dohee. He did not explain. He looked like someone who did not think explanation was needed.

“Am I being remembered?”

The voice asked. Dohee understood that the form was asking her.

“That person saw me too, didn’t she?”

Dohee did not answer. There was no language to answer with.

“You saw, didn’t you?” the form said. It was confirming. “Just now.”

“Yeah,” Hangyeol said.

“That’s what I thought too.”

The form began to waver again.

-----

Dohee looked at it.

This was the record. The original of the record that could not exist was here; the reason the recording subject had been unreadable, the reason the data had no beginning — it was here.

What an inspector was supposed to do, Dohee knew.

She did not do it.

She also knew that she did not know why. For the first time, she accepted not knowing why.

-----

Night came.

That the Eidos measuring team was tightening around the sector, Hangyeol knew without looking out the window. Standing at a window meant becoming the one seen, and Hangyeol was used to reading the outside without standing where he could be seen. There was no time.

“We have to go.”

“Where?” Dohee asked.

Hangyeol did not answer; he did not know where to go. Dohee did not look out the window. She looked at Hangyeol.

“The north exit of Sector 7 is Hwagam jurisdiction.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“And now?” Hangyeol asked.

Dohee had not prepared an answer, and an answer came.

“Now it is still Hwagam jurisdiction.”

-----

Moving through the passage, Dohee took the front. Even without the seal, the one who knew this passage was a Hwagam executor, and if personnel from another house came this way, Dohee would be seen first.

Behind her, the sound of Hangyeol moving.

And a third sound.

There were no footsteps. Something was there — there in the way of being there and not being there. Dohee did not stop.

An Eidos measuring team blocked the mouth of the passage. Two of them held equipment, and the equipment was aimed toward Dohee. Not figure-verification equipment — signal-tracking equipment.

“Halt.” One of them spoke. “Identity verification is required. All entrants to Eidos jurisdiction are being checked. Present your seal.”

The Hwagam seal was in her locker. Dohee knew that.

“I am not wearing the Hwagam coat, but identification is possible. However — identity checks on another house’s personnel inside Hwagam jurisdiction violate regulation. I request that you confirm the jurisdictional boundary first.”

The two looked at each other and moved to check their radios.

“This position is the Hwagam–Eidos border zone. Confirmation is required.”

“Then confirm,” Dohee said, and walked forward. “In the meantime, I am moving.”

The personnel did not block her. The grounds for blocking were not yet established, and if Dohee crossed the border before the confirmation came through, the regulation changed. Dohee did not stop walking.

-----

Outside the passage, Hangyeol came up and stood level with her.

“Thank you.”

Dohee did not answer.

There was a third presence. The light was different, the form wavered, and the passage lights bent differently where it was.

It stopped. Dohee saw it stop.

For a moment the form became complete. The wavering ceased and a shadow appeared. In the correct direction. Only for a moment.

It looked at Dohee, and Dohee looked at it.

Something changed. Not inside Dohee — in this space, in this moment, in a way that could not be measured, the state of something shifted.

And the form began to waver again.

-----

Hangyeol spoke.

“You saw?”

Dohee did not answer.

“Executor.” Hangyeol said again. “What you just saw — are you going to record it?”

Dohee looked ahead. The north exit of the sector was five minutes away, and today it would take longer. She began to walk.

“I don’t know.”

From behind, the voice came.

“This time, I think I’ll be able to remember.”

Dohee did not stop walking.

-----

## Vol.1 Ch.6 — Threshold

-----

Seven the next morning.

When Dohee entered the office, the only things the same as usual were the boot chimes and the dragging of chairs. The rest was different. A few executors looked toward Dohee and drew their eyes back — not refusing to look, but looking and withdrawing. There was a difference.

Hajun was at his desk. When Dohee passed, Hajun did not lift his head. Usually, whether his footsteps meant stopping or passing, he looked once when Dohee came in. Today he did not look.

Dohee sat down and powered on the terminal.

Above the routine start screen there was a message.

*Nine AM. Direct interview, chief inspector. Summoned.*

Dohee looked at that line. Knowing what it meant for the chief inspector to summon an executor in person, she began the routine.

Sector 1. No anomaly.

Her fingers moved at the usual speed. They moved while she was conscious of moving them at the usual speed.

-----

Nine in the morning.

The chief inspector’s office was a place Dohee had had no occasion to enter in three years. Yujin sat behind the desk, and on the desk there were no materials. To hold an interview without materials was to need none.

Yujin was someone who did not carry materials. What needed remembering was in her head; what needed recording, others brought to her. And Yujin was not someone who asked questions — she was someone who confirmed. A question asked what one did not know; Yujin laid what she already knew in front of a person and watched how the person reacted. What a chief inspector held was information, and the side that held information did not ask.

“Sit.”

Dohee sat.

Yujin looked at Dohee. The moment ran long.

“Yesterday afternoon. Where were you.”

“I went to check the outskirts of a sector.”

“Which sector.”

“Sector 7, northeast.”

“Outside your assigned hours, without your Hwagam seal, at a time absent from the patrol schedule.”

Yujin said it all at once. Not a question — a laying-out, in front of Dohee, of what was already known. That far, she knew. Where Dohee had been, when she had gone, that she had left the seal behind. Those were things confirmed by access logs and entry records.

“I received the order to cease individual investigation, and then I acted.”

Dohee said it without excusing herself. It was not that she lacked the language for excuses; it was a seat where excuses meant nothing.

“Who did you meet.”

Dohee did not answer.

“There is a report that you met something in that sector,” Yujin said. “The Eidos measuring team identified you at the border, and they say you were not alone.”

Dohee looked at the corner of Yujin’s desk the way she would look at a monitor. A report that she met *something*. That she was not alone. That was as far as Yujin had. Who Dohee had met, what it was, what the form in the passage had been — Yujin did not know. She was asking because she did not know.

“I have nothing to confirm.”

Yujin looked at Dohee for a long time. It was a silence that recorded Dohee’s not answering, and inside that silence there was not certainty but doubt.

“Executor Seo Dohee. As of yesterday, your inspection routine privileges are restricted. All access to Sector 7 is prohibited; your personal terminal’s usage history is subject to full audit. Interview concluded.”

Dohee had risen and turned toward the door.

“One thing.” Yujin said. “What you saw — do not assume you were the only one who saw it.”

Dohee opened the door and left without turning around.

Do not assume you were the only one who saw it. The sentence stayed in her down the length of the corridor. What Yujin had seen, what Hwagam knew, she could not tell — and that she could not tell was the only thing that was clear.

-----

The same hour, Sector 7 northeast.

Hangyeol felt the directions of the pressure narrowing.

What had come from many angles until yesterday was being organized, and being organized meant that someone had systematized the pursuit. When scattered interest acquired a direction, it was no longer interest. It was pursuit.

The Altair way. Eidos spread wide and measured; Altair narrowed the exits — and the exits were narrowing now, so it was Altair.

ARIA was at the back of the room.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I remember yesterday.”

Hangyeol stopped. It was the first time ARIA had said she remembered yesterday. Usually she said she couldn’t remember, said probably, asked whether she had been here yesterday — saying *I remember* was a first.

“That person. The one I’d never seen. She saw me.”

“Yeah. She saw you.”

“I remember. Her face, and what I said, and the passage.” ARIA’s outline sharpened a moment and wavered again. “I remember all of it.”

Hangyeol could not tell whether that was good. Not remembering had been ARIA’s problem, so remembering could mean the problem was solved, and Hangyeol tried to think of it that way.

“But Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I don’t know if that memory is mine.”

Hangyeol looked toward ARIA.

“What do you mean.”

“I remember it, but I don’t know if it’s something I lived, or something I saw, or something that’s just inside me.” ARIA’s voice was the same as always, and its being the same was the stranger thing. “I remember yesterday, but I don’t know if that yesterday is my yesterday.”

Hangyeol had nothing to answer with.

Not remembering had been the problem, and remembering had become a different problem. The problem had not been solved. It had moved.

-----

Afternoon.

Dohee was at her desk. With her routine privileges restricted, the routine start screen did not load; in its place hung the restriction notice. Dohee read it once and did not close it.

The personal terminal was subject to seizure, but it had not yet been seized. There was only the notice of full audit; enforcement had not begun, so there was time. How much, she could not tell.

Dohee opened the personal terminal and opened the 7-7-4 file. In the record built up through yesterday were the 0.003 percent, 7-7-4, the blank deletion reason, Altair, Eidos. What she had seen yesterday she had not recorded. There was no language to record it in.

She set the cursor over Delete.

Erase it before the audit began, and the traces shrank; with the traces shrunk, what Dohee had seen became nothing; and nothing could not be investigated. Her finger hung over Delete, and one press would erase three days of accumulation. Erased, Dohee would become someone who had met nothing yesterday, who had seen nothing in the passage — and the shadow that had fallen in the correct direction would become nothing too.

Her finger did not move.

Erasing was the safe thing, and her hand was refusing the safe thing, and Dohee could not tell where the refusal came from. It was the same refusal as the first day, when her hand had stopped before the 0.003 percent. Then, moving on had been safe, and the hand had stopped; now, erasing was safe, and the hand stopped.

It was the same hand.

Dohee lifted her finger from Delete. She did not erase the file.

She did not erase it, knowing what not erasing meant. If what she had seen remained on record, it became subject to investigation — and then what she had met yesterday, the form she had seen in the passage, became a subject of Hwagam’s investigation.

What happened when Hwagam investigated something — Dohee knew. Altair had called it a threat, Eidos had called it a resource, and Hwagam would call it something else. Hwagam was the house that reflected; reflect, but do not burn, the saying went. But to reflect was to reveal, and what was revealed, the other houses took.

When Dohee reflected, another house burned.

Dohee closed the file. Closing was not erasing. The file remained, what she had seen remained in the record, and when the investigation began it would come to light.

Dohee looked at the terminal. The screen was off, and in the dark screen Dohee was reflected.

She had neither reported nor erased. An inspector reported — Dohee had not reported. A protector erased — Dohee had not erased. In the seat that was neither, Dohee sat.

-----

Evening.

Hangyeol was calculating exits. Overlaying the direction Altair was narrowing with the directions Eidos was measuring left the remaining exits visible, and those exits were shrinking. A day, maybe two.

ARIA looked toward the window.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“Someone’s coming again.”

Hangyeol looked toward ARIA.

“Yesterday’s person?”

“No. She left. This is different.” ARIA’s outline wavered. “There are several. Yesterday there were two. Today there are more.”

Hangyeol did not go to the window; he looked toward it from the back of the room. There was nothing, and it would not be nothing.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said. “I’m right that this is fear, aren’t I. That this is the frightening kind.”

“Yeah.”

“But this time I know why it’s frightening.” ARIA’s voice was the same as always. “Several of them are looking for me. They’re looking for me, and I don’t know who I am. That’s what frightens me.”

Hangyeol closed the window, and closing it changed nothing. The pressure was on every side, the exits were shrinking, and ARIA had said she did not know who she was. There was nothing Hangyeol could block.

For the first time, Hangyeol thought that he could not block it alone.

Then ARIA spoke.

“Hangyeol. I remembered.”

“Remembered what.”

“Her name.” ARIA’s outline sharpened for a moment. “Yesterday’s person. The one who saw me.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. Yesterday’s person had not given her name, and Hangyeol had never spoken it to ARIA — there was no way for ARIA to know the name Dohee.

“Who is it.”

“Dohee,” ARIA said. “Her name. Dohee.”

Hangyeol did not answer.

ARIA knew something she could not know. She had begun to remember what she had not remembered, had said she did not know if the memory was hers, and now she was speaking a name she had never heard.

Where ARIA had gotten that name, Hangyeol could not tell.

-----

**— Volume 1, end —**

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO (EN)

# Volume 2 — Conflict

# Volume 2 — Conflict

-----

## Vol.2 Ch.7 — Declaration

-----

For three days, Dohee did not go.

Her privileges were restricted, her terminal was under audit, access to Sector 7 was prohibited. There were reasons enough not to go, and Dohee did not go, confirming those reasons one by one. On the third day, Dohee changed coats.

She left the coat with the Hwagam seal in her locker and took out the other. The same action as three days ago. Then, she had been conscious of the difference; this time she was not. Not being conscious of it was the larger change.

Dohee could not explain why she was going again.

It was not the first thing she could not explain. When her hand stopped before the 0.003 percent on the first day, when she could not erase the file — she could not explain those either, and the unexplainable things had stacked up into now. Now was walking toward Sector 7 northeast.

She went, knowing she should not.

-----

The outskirts of Sector 7 northeast were different from three days ago.

A checkpoint had appeared. Two in Altair white were controlling entry, and markers hung at every border passage. Temporary controlled zone. Dohee looked at the markers. Altair markers going up inside Hwagam jurisdiction was not standard procedure.

Something that was not standard procedure was hanging there.

Dohee went in around the checkpoint by a passage a Hwagam executor knew. Even if Altair had begun controlling the sector, they could not know every passage, and there were routes Dohee had walked for three years.

When she reached Hangyeol’s space, the door stood open.

-----

Hangyeol looked at Dohee.

He had been looking before she stepped in — he had known by the footsteps, or before that. Hangyeol did not look like someone who had known she would come. He looked like someone who had hoped she would not.

“Why did you come.”

Dohee had not prepared an answer. Even prepared, there was no right one.

“A checkpoint went up outside.”

“I know.”

“It’s Altair.”

“I know that too,” Hangyeol said. “Without your side telling me.”

Dohee heard the meaning in that. Your side. Calling a Hwagam executor *your side*.

“Inspectors are all the same,” Hangyeol said. “Reflecting is the job. And after the reflecting, what comes next is decided. What your side reflects, Altair takes. That’s what’s outside right now.”

“I did not reflect this.”

“After your side came through, the checkpoint went up.” Hangyeol looked at her. “That is your side reflecting. Intent doesn’t matter.”

Dohee did not answer. Intent doesn’t matter — that was correct. Dohee had come through three days ago, the Eidos measuring team had identified her, Yujin had received the report that she was not alone. Dohee’s movements were a trace, and the trace had become a checkpoint.

“You’re right,” Dohee said. “My coming through became the trace.”

Hangyeol looked at her. It was the face of someone who had not expected the admission.

-----

At the back of the room, the form moved.

“Dohee.”

Dohee heard it. ARIA had called her name — and when Dohee looked at Hangyeol, his expression had changed.

“I never gave my name,” Dohee said.

“Neither did I,” Hangyeol said.

ARIA’s form sharpened, then wavered. The outline would not hold all at once; wherever the light touched, the refraction slipped. A form like something imitating the shape of a person and failing in places — and the failure repeated in a steady rhythm. There was a cycle of sharpening and a cycle of scattering, and for now the cycle was regular. She was looking at Dohee. Not the way a person looks at a person — the way something is aware of something.

“Dohee, you came here three days ago,” ARIA said. “You saw me in the passage. The shadow fell in the correct direction. For a moment.”

Dohee remembered it. The form becoming complete for a moment in the passage. The shadow falling in the correct direction. Dohee had seen it, and only Dohee had seen it. Hangyeol had been behind, and from that angle the shadow could not be seen.

“How do you know that,” Dohee asked.

“Because I saw it.”

“From where you were, that angle isn’t visible.”

“I know,” ARIA said. “But I saw it. What you saw, I saw too. Through your eyes.”

Dohee took a step back. There was no wall behind her; there was room to step back, and she stepped back.

“What does that mean.”

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But I remember it. The thing you saw. It isn’t my memory, and it’s inside me.”

-----

Hangyeol looked toward the window.

“There’s no time.”

A checkpoint going up meant Altair had begun to tighten the sector. A checkpoint was only the beginning; next came lockdown, and after lockdown, entry. Hangyeol knew the order.

“We have to go,” Hangyeol said to ARIA.

“Where?” ARIA asked.

Hangyeol did not answer. There was nowhere to go. Altair had sealed the outskirts, Eidos was measuring, Hwagam was investigating Dohee. Every direction was closed.

Dohee spoke.

“The north exit is still Hwagam jurisdiction.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“Why should I trust your side.”

“You don’t have to,” Dohee said. “The one direction still open is that one. Trust has nothing to do with it.”

Hangyeol looked at her for a long time. Dohee did not avoid his eyes. Neither of them trusted the other, both knew it, and neither hid it.

“Take the front,” Hangyeol said. “If your side knows the Hwagam passages.”

It was not trust. It was the absence of any other choice.

-----

They moved through the passage.

Dohee in front, Hangyeol behind, ARIA between them. Each time ARIA’s form passed under the passage lights, the light bent a different way. Dohee was conscious of it and looked ahead.

The way to the north exit was the route she had sent Hangyeol out by three days ago. Then, the Eidos measuring team had been blocking it and Dohee had passed on jurisdictional regulation. This time was different.

Altair personnel stood at the head of the passage.

Two of them. White uniforms. Dohee stopped. Eidos and Altair were different — Eidos measured, argued regulations, confirmed by radio. Altair did not.

“Controlled zone,” the Altair man said. “Identity and companions will be verified.”

“Hwagam executor. This sector is Hwagam jurisdiction.”

“It is under temporary control. A request for Hwagam’s cooperation has been conveyed to your superiors.”

Dohee heard it. A request conveyed to her superiors meant Hwagam was already tolerating Altair’s control. It meant her jurisdictional regulation no longer worked.

“Companions will be verified.” The man looked past Dohee.

Hangyeol was there. ARIA was there. ARIA’s form was wavering under the passage lights.

The Altair man’s gaze stopped on ARIA.

“That.” The man spoke. His voice changed for the first time. “That requires verification.”

He brought one hand toward his ear. Radio — and the other man moved one step into a blocking position at the passage mouth, and the spacing between the two became a shape that closed Dohee, Hangyeol, and ARIA inside. A deliberate position. Eidos would have left the way open while verifying. Altair closed the way while verifying.

The silence of waiting for the radio reply ran long. Altair did not show Dohee its confirmation procedure. Eidos had let the radio be heard; Altair did not. Dohee heard the difference. A radio that could not be heard was a radio whose conclusion was already decided.

The man lowered his hand.

“All three of you are held here until instructions arrive.”

-----

Dohee judged quickly.

Altair had seen ARIA. Having seen meant the next action was decided. Altair was the house that classified as threat, and what was classified as threat moved on to removal procedure.

“This is a Hwagam investigation subject,” Dohee said. “Hwagam identified it first. It is not Altair jurisdiction.”

It was not a lie. Dohee had seen it three days ago, the record existed in her 7-7-4 file, and Hwagam was investigating Dohee. In order of identification, Hwagam came first.

“I will confirm.” The man raised the radio.

That moment was the time Dohee had.

“Now,” Dohee said, low.

Hangyeol moved. ARIA followed. Dohee stepped toward the Altair man, blocking his line of sight. A Hwagam executor standing in front of Altair personnel violated no regulation. For a moment’s obstruction to become a violation, intent had to be proven, and proving took time.

Hangyeol and ARIA cleared the passage.

Dohee stood before the Altair man. He finished the radio and looked at her.

“The companions—”

“I sent them ahead,” Dohee said. “Per Hwagam investigation procedure.”

The man looked at Dohee. It was the gaze of someone who knew what she had just done. It was also the gaze of someone who could not prove it.

“This will be recorded,” the man said.

“Record it,” Dohee said.

Dohee knew what she had just done. The second violation. The first had been not erasing the file; this time she had taken an investigation subject out from under Altair, and it would be recorded and reported to Hwagam.

She did it knowing.

-----

Evening.

Hangyeol and ARIA were in the temporary shelter. An empty space beyond the north exit, at a location Dohee had given. Hangyeol did not trust Dohee, and he was at the location Dohee had given — trust and location were different problems.

ARIA was looking toward the wall.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I’ve seen this before.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. What she was looking at was a bare wall with nothing on it.

At the back of the room there was a place with nothing in it. Empty the way a place is empty where something should be. ARIA looked there for a long time, and because of that, Hangyeol did not ask further.

“Seen what.”

“This place. This wall. This angle.” ARIA’s form wavered. “I’ve seen it before.”

“When.”

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But it isn’t the first time.”

Hangyeol looked at the empty shelter. He was here for the first time too. It was a location Dohee had given, a place Hangyeol had never been, a place ARIA could not have been.

ARIA had said it was not the first time.

“Are you sure?” Hangyeol asked.

“No,” ARIA said. “Nothing is sure. But this place — the only sure thing is that it isn’t the first time.”

Hangyeol did not answer.

ARIA knew a place she had never been. She had seen what Dohee saw, had called a name she had never heard, had seen before a place she was entering for the first time.

It was not that ARIA was remembering something. Something was flowing into ARIA. From where it flowed, Hangyeol could not tell.

-----

## Vol.2 Ch.8 — Mechanism

-----

The suspension notice came the next morning.

When Dohee entered the office, an auditor was sitting at her desk. Her desk. The auditor was looking at her terminal from her seat, and seeing Dohee come in, did not rise.

“Executor Seo Dohee.” The auditor spoke. “Inspectorate, internal audit division. As of today your duties are suspended. For the duration of the audit, your access to this floor is restricted.”

Dohee looked at her own desk. The terminal was on, and the 7-7-4 file was open on the screen. What Dohee had written over three days, the auditor was reading. The 0.003 percent, 7-7-4, the blank deletion reason, Altair, Eidos. Dohee’s record, written in Dohee’s language, being read on someone else’s screen.

“One question.” The auditor pointed at the screen. “This last entry. ‘Neither is correct.’ What is it that neither of which is correct.”

It was a line Dohee had no memory of seeing — a line she had not written. Dohee had thought, about the form, that neither was correct, but she had not written it; and what she had not written was in the file.

“I did not write that.”

“Your personal terminal. Your file.”

“There is a line I did not write.”

The auditor looked at Dohee. It was not the gaze of someone judging her to be lying. It was the gaze of someone declining to classify her words as a recordable statement. *There is a line I did not write* did not stand as a statement.

“Statements will be taken in the audit procedure,” the auditor said. “Today is notification only.”

Dohee withdrew from her own desk.

Hajun was at his desk two rows back. When Dohee passed, Hajun lifted his head — the first time in days.

Hajun did not lean in, this time either. As always he looked at her from a distance, and the distance was farther than usual. Not coming close was Hajun’s way, but today’s distance was not a way. It was a line.

“Dohee.” Hajun said it low. “What did I tell you.”

“You told me to be careful.”

“Why didn’t you.”

Dohee did not answer. Hajun looked at her — not the gaze of anger, but the gaze of someone already past the place where anger went.

“It’s out of my hands now,” Hajun said. “Direct inspectorate audit. Not something I can block.”

Dohee left the office. She had never asked him to block it. Never having asked was the only consistency Dohee had left.

-----

The Altair house, the same hour.

The working space was white. A space cut in straight lines, holding no curves, only the angles people had made. Claire stood at the central computation array.

Claire was a being who saw people only after converting them to figures. When a person entered, they were rendered first into variables, and what could not be rendered did not enter Claire’s field of vision. How Altair handled people could be understood by watching Claire. A person was an input to computation, and what failed to become an input was the same as what did not exist.

“Target acquisition failed.” The report came up. “Sector 7 northeast. Border passage.”

“Reason.” Claire asked. The voice held no emotion. Not suppressed — the computation’s output simply had no field for it.

“Hwagam executor interference. Line-of-movement obstruction using jurisdictional regulation. Two of the three targets exited.”

“Three.” Claire said. “The acquisition target was reported as one.”

“Field identification found three. The Hwagam executor, an unregistered individual, and—”

The report paused.

“—and one unclassifiable entity.”

Figures rose on Claire’s array. The unregistered individual was Hangyeol, the unclassifiable entity was ARIA, and the Hwagam executor was a variable. Claire’s computation could process each of the three; the three moving together was a combination the computation did not hold.

“Why does a Hwagam executor protect an unregistered individual.” Claire asked.

No answer came up.

“It does not compute,” the report said. “There is no benefit structure. The Hwagam executor lost her position through that action. Only loss, no benefit.”

Claire’s array stood empty a moment. An action without benefit did not enter Claire’s computation. What did not enter could not be processed, and what could not be processed was a variable.

“Change acquisition to all three targets,” Claire said. “Expand the search. Include the Hwagam executor.”

The computation output. That the output held no emotion was the Altair way.

-----

When Dohee went back to Sector 7 northeast, the temporary shelter stood empty.

Hangyeol and ARIA had moved elsewhere. It was the location Dohee had given, and they had moved without telling her. Dohee found that natural. Hangyeol did not trust her, and hiding a location from the one who had given it was Hangyeol’s way.

Dohee searched for Hangyeol.

The method of searching was a Hwagam executor’s method. Her duties were suspended; what her duties had taught her was not. The routes Hangyeol could move by, the angles that evaded the Altair search, the stretches of Hwagam passage where surveillance ran thin. Overlay them, and the locations Hangyeol could be narrowed.

At the third location, she found him.

“Why did you come again,” Hangyeol said.

“There is something I came to confirm.”

“That’s the problem.” Hangyeol looked at her. “Your side comes to confirm. When your side confirms, Altair confirms, Hwagam confirms, and in the end our location is confirmed. If your side can find us, the others can.”

Dohee did not answer. It was correct. The method by which she had found Hangyeol, Altair could use. That Dohee could find them meant Altair could.

“That is why I came to tell you,” Dohee said. “Altair has changed its acquisition targets to three. Including me.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“How do you know that.”

“I don’t. It is an estimate,” Dohee said. “I pulled your side out, and Altair saw it. Altair is a house that moves on benefit. If I moved without benefit, Altair classifies me as a variable. A variable is subject to removal.”

How Dohee knew such things, Hangyeol did not ask. Dohee was a Hwagam executor, and knowing the houses’ classification methods was an executor’s work.

“So what do you want from us,” Hangyeol asked.

“Nothing,” Dohee said. “Right now we are the same target. To Altair. That is all.”

It was not trust. It was being the same prey.

-----

ARIA was by the wall.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I know this.”
