“Know what.”

“I don’t know.” ARIA’s form wavered. “But I know it. Something white. There are no curves. Someone is there.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“They’re counting. Counting something.”

“Counting what,” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But the number went up. Just now. It became one more.”

Hangyeol stopped. Dohee stopped too.

What Dohee had just said was that Altair had changed its acquisition targets from two to three. She had said it as an estimate. She had never seen the Altair working space; she had not directly known that the number Altair was counting had grown — she had inferred it from classification method.

ARIA was speaking that grown number.

“ARIA.” Dohee spoke to ARIA directly for the first time. “Are you seeing that space right now.”

“No. It isn’t seeing,” ARIA said. “It’s knowing. As if I were there. But I’m here. Not in two places — I’m here, and I know there.”

“Whose memory is it,” Dohee asked.

“It isn’t memory,” ARIA said. “Memory is what has passed. This is now. Someone is counting now. When I try to know it, it goes away. When I don’t try, it shows.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol, and Hangyeol looked at Dohee. Both were thinking the same thing. A number Dohee had spoken only as an estimate, ARIA had seen in the same moment. If it were memory, it should have been something passed — and ARIA had said *now*.

What was flowing into ARIA, where it came from, whether it was passed or now — neither of them could tell.

-----

Night.

The Altair search team tightened around the sector. Dohee knew first. Her duties were suspended; reading search patterns was not. The movement of white uniforms was drawing a grid, and the grid was shrinking toward Hangyeol’s position.

“We have to move,” Dohee said.

“Where,” Hangyeol asked.

“Northeast is sealed. South is the Eidos measuring zone. The west is empty.”

“Why is the west empty,” Hangyeol asked. “When everywhere else is sealed and one place is empty, it isn’t empty. It’s been left empty.”

Dohee stopped. Hangyeol was right. Altair was a house that moved on benefit, and leaving one direction open was for driving things toward it. Dohee had read the search pattern — and the pattern could have been designed to be read.

“Your side reads the Hwagam way,” Hangyeol said. “Altair knows your side reads the Hwagam way. So they made the west look empty to a Hwagam reading.”

Dohee did not answer.

For the first time, Hangyeol had seen the danger before Dohee. Dohee knew the houses’ ways of classifying; Hangyeol was someone who had lived on the side that escaped classification. The eyes of the classifier and the eyes of the one who evades classification saw different things.

“Then where do we go,” Dohee asked.

“Nowhere,” Hangyeol said. “Move, and the grid catches us. Right now, not moving is moving.”

The search team’s footsteps neared, receded, and the grid passed over the empty shelter. The three did not move, and ARIA’s form did not waver. For the first time, ARIA was completely still.

The footsteps faded.

-----

After the search team passed, Dohee sat in the empty shelter.

Her duties suspended, her terminal seized, the 7-7-4 file in the inspectorate’s hands, herself now an Altair acquisition target. Three days ago Dohee had been a Hwagam executor; now she was one of the three Altair hunted. The one who watched had become the prey.

Dohee did not regret the change. She registered that she did not regret it — and that she had already passed the place where regret went.

There were things left behind at Hwagam. The suspension notice, the terminal the auditor had been reading, the 7-7-4 file with the line Dohee had not written.

The file had left Dohee, but it had not ceased to exist. It was somewhere. In a place where Dohee was not.

The line Dohee had not written.

She thought about it again. The last line the auditor had pointed to. *Neither is correct.* Something Dohee had thought and not written. On Dohee’s terminal, in Dohee’s file, there was a line Dohee had not written.

It meant someone had accessed the file.

Before, the file’s contents had shrunk; this time they had grown. Shrinking and growing were the same thing. While Dohee did not know, someone was reading and writing in her file.

With her suspension, the terminal had been seized. It was in the inspectorate’s hands. What could access that file now was the inspectorate.

Dohee thought of the seized terminal. The inspectorate would have checked the file’s last access record. Last accessed by. What name was recorded there, Dohee could not tell. It would not be her own. She had not written.

The last time Dohee had checked, the file’s final access location had been Sector 7 northeast.

Where Dohee was now.

And in that access record, no last accessor remained. No trace of reading, no trace of writing, belonging to no one. Someone had read and written in Dohee’s file, and the one who read and wrote was not in the record.

-----

-----

-----

## Observation-01 — “The Signal”

*Placement: between Vol.2 Ch.8 and Ch.9*
*POV: Tiko / the Eidos observation network*

-----

The signal was strange.

An Eidos agent reported to Tiko.

“Unclassifiable. Shall I reprocess it.”

“No.”

“Shall I archive it.”

“It is under observation.”

The agent looked at Tiko. Tiko was looking at the screen. The signal wavered on the screen. Not a person, not a machine, not a facility.

“There is no handling directive.”

“It is under observation.”

The agent paused, then said:

“You mean to only observe.”

“Yes.”

“And the report.”

“When the observation yields a result, I report.”

“When does the result come.”

Tiko did not take his eyes off the screen.

“When the observation ends.”

The agent said no more. On the screen, the signal kept wavering. Tiko looked at it. Looking was everything. Looking was the work.

This signal is strange.

He recorded only that.

-----

*Observation-01 — end*

## Vol.2 Ch.9 — Divergence

-----

Min Siwoo came first.

Before Altair. Altair tightened in a drawn grid; Min Siwoo came without one. Alone at the entrance of the empty shelter where the three were hiding, carrying no measuring equipment. That was the more uncomfortable thing. Eidos arriving without measurement meant the measuring was already done.

“Found you.” Min Siwoo said. Smiling — and the smile was not a smile that looked at the three. It looked through them, at the signal the three made, at the data behind them. The same smile as the first time at the Hwagam office. Looking not at a person but behind a person.

Hangyeol looked at Dohee. This was not a location Dohee had given, nor one Dohee knew. Hangyeol had moved here alone; it should have been a place only Hangyeol knew.

“How did you find us,” Dohee asked.

“You find what you set out to find,” Min Siwoo said. “Altair searches by grid. That takes time. We search differently. When the three of you are together, the signal changes. There’s a pattern the three of you make. You just follow the pattern.”

Dohee heard it. The pattern the three made. Dohee and Hangyeol and ARIA being together was itself a signal. A signal that weakened when scattered and strengthened when gathered. The way Dohee had found Hangyeol, the way Altair was tightening, the way Min Siwoo had followed — all of it was that signal.

“Did you come to take us,” Dohee asked.

“No,” Min Siwoo said. “I came to help.”

The smile held, and Dohee tried to read the intent in it, and it could not be read. Altair’s expressionlessness was a face with the conclusion already decided; Min Siwoo’s smile — whether it was a face hiding a conclusion or a face with none, she could not tell.

“What does helping mean,” Hangyeol asked.

“Do you know what happens if Altair acquires you?” Min Siwoo said. “They disassemble. Because you’re a threat. A threat has to be made to stop functioning, and to make it stop, you disassemble it. That’s how Altair does it.”

Min Siwoo looked toward ARIA. ARIA’s form wavered.

“We don’t,” Min Siwoo said. “We observe. We observe it functioning. Disassembled things don’t produce data. So we keep things alive.”

Keep things alive. Dohee heard the words. Altair disassembled, Eidos kept alive — and keeping alive was not saving. It was for observation.

“I’ll come again tomorrow.” Min Siwoo said. No card was set down. Three days ago a card had been left; this time, none. “If you’re still here then, I’ll take it as refusal. And if you refuse, we’ll have to look at you differently too. Until now we’ve only observed — but refusal means an observation subject declining cooperation, and from there the classification changes.”

“How does the classification change,” Dohee asked.

“That gets decided tomorrow, so there’s nothing I can tell you now,” Min Siwoo said. The smile held. “See you tomorrow.”

Min Siwoo left.

Dohee thought about the card not being set down. Three days ago a choice had been left; this time, none. No card meant no way for Dohee to make contact first. Contact was possible only by Min Siwoo coming tomorrow, and until then Dohee was the waiting side. Eidos spoke as if giving choices while holding the clock of choosing itself.

-----

Dohee attempted contact with Hwagam.

Her duties were suspended, but her contact privileges had not been cut. Dohee sent Hajun a message. Sector 7 situation report. Altair control expanding. She waited for a reply.

No reply came.

An hour later it still had not, and Dohee sent again. Short, one line. I need an answer. To that too, no reply.

It was the first time Hajun did not answer. For three years, Hajun had answered Dohee’s reports, even late. Answering late and not answering were different. Not answering meant Dohee had been cut from the reporting line.

Dohee checked the inspectorate’s general channel. Part of her access privileges remained alive, and a notice about Sector 7 was posted.

*Sector 7 northeast: transferred to direct inspectorate management. All Hwagam executor access prohibited. Information concerning the unregistered entity is to be reported directly to the inspectorate.*

Dohee read the notice. All Hwagam executor access prohibited. Dohee was a suspended Hwagam executor, and that suspended executor was inside the prohibited sector.

There was nowhere to return to.

Dohee thought it for the first time. When her duties were suspended, a place to return to had remained; when the audit ended she could be reinstated, and reinstated, she would be an executor again. Now it was different — Hwagam had closed the sector Dohee was in. For Dohee to return, Dohee would have to open the door Hwagam had closed, and it was a door that did not open.

Dohee closed the message window. Hajun’s last reply was the single line from three days ago — *Be careful* — and that had been the last.

-----

They had to move.

The Altair grid was tightening toward this shelter. Dohee read it, and told Hangyeol the pattern she had read.

“If we go north—”

“Left,” ARIA said.

Dohee stopped. ARIA was looking at the left of the passage, and the north Dohee had named was to the right.

“The north is closed by Hwagam,” Dohee said. “The left is inside the Altair grid.”

“We have to go left,” ARIA said.

“Why,” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But it’s left. We can’t go right. The right—” ARIA’s form wavered. “Someone is already waiting on the right.”

Dohee looked at the right passage. It stood empty. The direction Hwagam had closed — closed, so it should be empty — and the Altair grid lay to the left. Read by classification, the right was safe and the left was dangerous.

ARIA was saying the opposite.

“Someone is in the direction Hwagam closed?” Dohee asked.

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But they’re waiting. Knowing we’ll come that way.”

Dohee had to judge. ARIA’s intuition and Dohee’s analysis were colliding. Dohee had lived three years on analysis. ARIA was speaking what could not be explained. To follow ARIA was to set down the analysis, and to set down the analysis was to set down the only tool Dohee had.

“Go left and we’re caught in the Altair grid,” Dohee said.

“Go right and something else is waiting,” Hangyeol said.

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol believed what ARIA said.

“Why do you believe that,” Dohee asked.

“In three years, ARIA has never been wrong,” Hangyeol said. “She can’t explain it. But she’s never been wrong. Your side lived on analysis. I lived on ARIA.”

-----

They went left.

The inside of the Altair grid was, by Dohee’s analysis, a suicide route. But there was an empty stretch inside the grid. The grid was not uniform, and the direction ARIA headed was that empty stretch — not a place Altair had left empty, but a place it had not yet managed to fill.

ARIA had known that stretch.

Passing through it, Dohee thought about that. Where the Altair grid was empty was Altair’s internal information. Which places it had filled and which it had not — only Altair knew. ARIA had known it. As if ARIA were watching Claire’s computation.

They cleared the grid.

A sound came from the right passage. The direction Dohee had analyzed as safe, the direction Hwagam had closed. Personnel were coming out of it — not Hwagam uniforms, uniforms Dohee did not know, what looked like the inspectorate’s own. If Dohee had gone right, they would have met those personnel.

ARIA had been right. Dohee had been wrong.

But where they emerged from the empty stretch, there were traces. Eidos measuring markers. Three small markers stamped into the floor at regular intervals, their direction following the route the three had come by. The empty stretch ARIA had guided them through was a way past the Altair grid — and it was also a way Eidos had laid markers along in advance. The place that escaped Altair was inside Eidos’s range of observation.

ARIA had read the empty place in Altair’s grid — and that empty place might have been one Eidos had left empty. ARIA’s intuition had not been wrong. Only, whose empty place she was reading — even ARIA did not know that far.

-----

When they reached a safe stretch, Hangyeol looked at Dohee.

“We’re being hunted because of you.”

Dohee did not answer.

“At first it was only Altair and Eidos. Before your side came. Your side came through and the checkpoint went up; your side pulled us out and Altair started hunting three; your side got reported to Hwagam and the inspectorate closed the sector.” Hangyeol said. “Every time your side moves, our space gets smaller.”

“None of it was my intent,” Dohee said.

“You said yourself at the start — intent doesn’t matter,” Hangyeol said. “Whatever your intent, the result is the same. Since your side attached to us, the pursuit has grown. That’s the fact.”

Dohee searched for grounds to refute it. If she had not pulled them out, Altair would have disassembled ARIA; she had read the pattern and they had escaped the search. If Dohee had not been there—

If Dohee had not been there.

She could not finish the sentence. If Dohee had not been there, Hangyeol and ARIA would be in the state before she came — the state where Altair and Eidos held interest but did not move. Dohee had moved, and they had moved. Hangyeol was right.

“You’re right,” Dohee said.

Hangyeol looked at her. Again the face of someone who had not expected the admission.

“Since I attached, the pursuit has grown. That is fact,” Dohee said. “But if I separate now, the pattern the three of us make shrinks — and ARIA’s reading of the empty places in the grid goes with it. The left, just now, was that. Whether my leaving is to your benefit or not, I don’t know.”

Hangyeol did not answer, and Dohee said no more. Neither of them knew the answer. Together, the pursuit grew; apart, they lost ARIA’s intuition — and which was more dangerous did not compute.

It was not that trust had formed. It was that separating had become impossible.

-----

Night came.

Dohee read the patterns again. The Altair grid was shrinking, Eidos was observing the signal the three made, the inspectorate had closed the sector. Three directions were contracting at once, and the contraction did not stop.

The places to run to were shrinking. The speed of the shrinking was increasing.

“Hangyeol,” ARIA said.

“Yeah.”

“We can’t stay here.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. ARIA was looking toward the wall. This was a stretch they had entered judging it safe — outside the Altair grid, not the direction Hwagam had closed, not the Eidos measuring zone.

“Why,” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But something’s coming soon.”

“Who,” Dohee asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA’s form wavered. “Not Altair, not Eidos, not Hwagam. Something else. Something that hasn’t come until now. But it’s coming soon. Here.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol, and Hangyeol looked at Dohee. Three directions were contracting, and ARIA was speaking of a fourth. Something that had not come until now, something Dohee could not analyze, something Dohee did not know — it was coming.

-----

## Vol.2 Ch.10 — Fracture

-----

“They’re here,” ARIA said.

Dohee stood up. ARIA had said something was coming soon last night, and Dohee had not been able to analyze it and so had not been able to prepare. What could not be prepared for, ARIA was saying again. They’re here. Present tense.

“Where,” Hangyeol asked.

“All of them,” ARIA said. “The right, and the left, and from above. Three at once.”

Dohee looked at the passage. Sound was starting from the three directions ARIA had named — not footsteps, but movement with the footsteps suppressed. Altair would have come slowly, drawing the grid; this was fast, and it was not a grid but straight lines. Someone knew exactly where the three were and was coming in straight lines.

“Our location is exposed,” Dohee said.

“How,” Hangyeol asked.

Dohee knew one thing. In the empty stretch they had escaped through yesterday, there had been Eidos markers. It was Eidos that had observed their route, and if someone knew their location exactly, it would have begun from that observation. But observing and handing over were different problems. Whether Eidos had handed it over directly, whether the observation had leaked, whether it was some other route — Dohee could not determine.

“Who knows our location is the side that observed,” Dohee said. “The only one who observed our route yesterday was Eidos. It started there — that much is clear. Who passed it on, and how, I don’t know.”

-----

What came in straight lines was the inspectorate’s own.

Uniforms that were not Hwagam uniforms. The uniforms Dohee had seen in the right passage yesterday. Personnel under chief inspector Yujin directly, and unlike Hwagam executors, unconstrained by house regulation. The inspectorate stood above the houses.

One of them stepped forward.

“Suspended executor Seo Dohee. Unregistered individual Hangyeol. Unclassifiable entity.” The man looked at the three in turn. “You are subjects for direct inspectorate custody. Do not resist.”

Dohee stepped forward.

“This sector is—”

“Not Hwagam jurisdiction,” the man said. “As of yesterday it is under direct inspectorate management. Your jurisdictional regulation does not operate here, Executor.”

Dohee stopped. The tool Dohee had was jurisdictional regulation — passing the Eidos measuring team, standing before the Altair personnel, all of it had been jurisdictional regulation. Before the inspectorate it did not operate, because the inspectorate stood above the houses.

“Step aside,” Dohee said.

“You are a suspended executor,” the man said. “Not someone to step aside for. Someone to take into custody.”

Dohee had nothing. No authority, no jurisdiction, no position — Dohee faced it head-on for the first time. A place where everything that had made Dohee herself for three years did not operate.

-----

Hangyeol moved.

While Dohee stood facing the man, Hangyeol took ARIA and slipped into the side passage. While Dohee was blocking the line of sight. The same structure as when Dohee had pulled Hangyeol out three days ago. This time Hangyeol did it.

But Hangyeol did not slip away.

He sent ARIA deeper into the side passage, and came back toward Dohee.

Dohee was being surrounded by three inspectorate personnel. One had her arm; Dohee twisted to pull free, but the hand on her wrist was a trained hand, precisely placed. Hwagam executors did inspection work; they were not trained in restraint. The inspectorate’s own were different. It was a grip Dohee could not break.

Another moved around to her other side. Held from both sides, Dohee would not be able to move.

In that moment ARIA’s voice came from the side passage.

“The one on the right. The knee.”

Hangyeol heard it. The right man’s knee. Hangyeol did not ask why; he moved. The right man was leaning in to take Dohee’s wrist, and in that posture the knee was carrying his weight. Hangyeol cut in toward the knee and broke the man’s balance.

The man lost Dohee’s wrist. The other one holding her looked toward his stumbling colleague. In that brief moment Dohee pulled her wrist free.

Hangyeol stepped into the man’s path.

“What do you think you’re doing,” the man said.

Hangyeol did not answer. He had no words to answer with, and no words to explain why he had come back. Send ARIA off and slip away alone — that was all it took; Dohee was the person who had put him in danger; if Dohee were taken, the pursuit might even shrink. By calculation, he should not have come back.

He came back.

“We go together,” Hangyeol said to Dohee.

“Why,” Dohee asked.

“I don’t know,” Hangyeol said. “Same as what you said before. I don’t know if separating is to our benefit or not. So we don’t separate.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. He was handing her own logic back to her. Not knowing whether separating was to their benefit. The thing she had said yesterday.

-----

What got them out was ARIA.

The ARIA sent into the side passage had not stopped there. She came back out from inside the passage, looking toward the space behind the inspectorate personnel.

“There’s an empty place there,” ARIA said.

The personnel had the three surrounded, and the formation had an empty place in it. Concentrating on Dohee and Hangyeol, they had left open the flank ARIA had slipped out by. ARIA saw the empty place. The same as seeing the empty place in the Altair grid yesterday.

“The left end,” ARIA said. “Now.”

Dohee moved without judging. Yesterday ARIA’s intuition and Dohee’s analysis had collided; today they did not. It was not that Dohee had begun to trust ARIA — Dohee had no resources left for analysis. In a place where jurisdictional regulation did not operate, Dohee’s analysis meant nothing, and where it meant nothing, what remained was ARIA’s intuition.

The three slipped out by the left end.

The inspectorate personnel turned too late. The three passed through before the empty place closed. The empty place ARIA had seen was an empty place in the act of closing, and ARIA had seen the last moment before it closed.

-----

Where they came out, Dohee stopped.

One inspectorate man had followed to the end of the passage. The others had fallen behind re-forming, and one had pursued alone. The distance between Dohee and the man was short.

The man threw something at Dohee. Not a weapon — a small marker, and it fastened onto her coat. A tracking marker.

Hangyeol saw it.

Dohee tried to pull it off and it did not come off. The kind that bonded to the fibers; removing it meant removing the coat.

“The coat,” Hangyeol said.

Dohee took off the coat. The coat without the seal — the one she had taken out three days ago, leaving the sealed one in her locker. She shed it and dropped it, and the marker stayed on the floor with the coat.

Dohee had nothing left. No Hwagam seal, no position, no coat. Having dropped even the coat the marker had fastened to, Dohee felt as if she wore nothing at all. In fact she had clothes on underneath — but the things that had made Dohee herself were all on the floor.

The pursuing man stopped. With the marker still on the coat, the tracker showed Dohee at that spot, and by the time the man looked down at the coat on the floor, the three were already clearing the passage.

-----

Night came.

Dohee checked the inspectorate’s general channel one last time. Part of her access remained. A new notice was posted.

*Executor Seo Dohee: dismissed. Refusal of inspectorate custody; aiding the flight of an unregistered individual. Report on sight.*

Not suspension — dismissal. Dohee was no longer a Hwagam executor. Suspension had left room for return; dismissal left none, and three years of being a Hwagam executor ended in one line of notice.

Dohee did not look at the notice long. She read it once and closed the channel.

Regret did not come. That she had passed the place where regret went, Dohee had registered the day her terminal was seized, and before the dismissal notice she confirmed it again. Hwagam executor Seo Dohee had ended — and in the place where it ended, something remained. Hangyeol and ARIA. In the place of the things Dohee had lost were the things Dohee had chosen.

In the dark terminal screen, Dohee’s face was reflected. Dohee was not someone who looked at mirrors for long; her own face was something to check and forget. But now she looked a moment at the reflected face, and the face that was not a Hwagam executor was there. The one thing Dohee had not looked into for three years was herself, and only after the title that defined that self was gone did Dohee look, for the first time, a moment, at her own face. And then, as always, having checked, she drew her eyes away.

The weight of what was lost and the weight of what was chosen — Dohee did not compare them. They could not be compared. They were two faces of the same event.

-----

ARIA was by the wall.

“Hangyeol,” ARIA said.

Hangyeol looked at her. ARIA’s form was wavering more than usual, the outline refusing to hold. When they had first met, ARIA’s wavering had carried regular cycles — a sharpening and a scattering. Now the regularity had broken. The sharpening grew shorter and the scattering longer. A brief sharpening, then a long scattering, and through the long scattering it was hard to gauge by outline where ARIA was.

“There are too many memories,” ARIA said.

“What do you mean,” Hangyeol asked.

“In one day, too much came in,” ARIA said. “The inspectorate people’s memories, where they came from, how the marker works. All of it came in. It comes in even when I try not to look. There’s so much I can’t tell where mine is.”

ARIA’s form scattered wide once, and gathered again.

“But Hangyeol,” ARIA said.

“Yeah.”

“Now I know.”

Hangyeol stopped.

“Know what,” he asked.

“Why they’re all looking for me,” ARIA said. “Now I know.”

Hangyeol waited for the next words.

“But I can’t say it,” ARIA said.

“Why,” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA’s form wavered. “I only know that I can’t say it. It came in together with the knowing. Why they’re looking for me, and that I can’t say it. Both together.”

Hangyeol could not ask. It was not that asking frightened him — it was that if ARIA said it, it would begin. ARIA had begun to know what no one had known, and if ARIA spoke, Dohee and Hangyeol would know too. And knowing, there would be no going back.

Who ARIA’s *can’t say it* was for, Hangyeol could not tell. For ARIA, for the three of them — or for whatever it was that had let it flow into her.

ARIA did not say it, and Hangyeol did not ask.

-----

## Vol.2 Ch.11 — Independence

-----

The next day, ARIA asked the same question four times.

“What time is it?”

Hangyeol answered. ARIA paused a moment and asked again.

“What time is it?”

The same question. Hangyeol answered again, ARIA paused again, asked again, and on the fourth time Hangyeol did not answer and looked at ARIA. ARIA’s form was not wavering the usual way — wavering, then stopping, then wavering again. A broken wavering.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol said. “Are you all right?”

“Yes.” ARIA said. “What time is it?”

ARIA had not stopped last night. As far as Hangyeol had seen, ARIA’s form had stayed awake through the night. Whether the concept of sleep existed for ARIA, Hangyeol did not know, but until now ARIA went quiet at night. Last night she had not gone quiet. All night something had been flowing into ARIA, and it had not stopped.

“The memories won’t stop.” ARIA said. For the first time, a sentence that was not a question. “When I try to sleep, more comes in. When I try not to sleep, it comes in. I don’t know how to make it stop.”

ARIA looked toward Hangyeol.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said. Then paused. “You are Hangyeol, right?”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. ARIA was confirming his name. In three years she had never once called him by another name or confused it.

“That’s right.” Hangyeol said.

“Yes.” ARIA said. “It’s right, but for a moment another name came up. Another name layered over yours. I don’t know whose name it is. But that name also felt like someone I know.” ARIA’s form wavered. “There are too many people I know now. They layer over you.”

Dohee was watching it. ARIA was not growing stronger — she was collapsing. The more she knew, the less of ARIA herself there was.

-----

In the afternoon, ARIA stood up.

“We have to go.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

“Where.”

“I don’t know.” ARIA said. “But we have to go. I think it will stop there.”

“What will stop.” Dohee asked.

“This.” ARIA looked at her own form. At the wavering outline. “What’s coming in. I think it stops there. I don’t know why.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol looked at Dohee. ARIA was naming a place and saying she did not know where it was. She said it would stop there, and did not know why it would stop. What ARIA knew was one thing: that they had to go.

“Do you know the direction?” Hangyeol asked.

“Yes.” ARIA looked in one direction. “That way.”

The direction ARIA looked was the way out of Sector 7. Outside the sector the three had stayed in until now. A sector Dohee had never once patrolled, a place outside Hwagam jurisdiction.

-----

“There are no grounds.” Dohee said.

Hangyeol looked at Dohee.

“ARIA says we have to go, and ARIA doesn’t know why we have to go.” Dohee said. “She says it stops there, and doesn’t know why it stops. There is a direction and there are no grounds.”

“No grounds, and she’s been right.” Hangyeol said. “The left yesterday, all of it — no grounds. All of it right.”

“There is no guarantee she is right this time.” Dohee said.

For the first time, Dohee opposed ARIA’s intuition. Until yesterday, Dohee had followed ARIA because she had no resources to analyze with. Today was different. The place ARIA pointed to was outside the sector, and outside the sector was a place Dohee had never once analyzed — and going to an unanalyzed place on ARIA’s intuition alone was a gamble.

“ARIA is collapsing.” Dohee said. “She said the memories won’t stop. In that state, the guarantee that the direction ARIA points to is accurate shrinks. ARIA’s intuition could be ARIA’s confusion.”

Hangyeol stopped. He had no grounds to refute it. That ARIA was collapsing, Hangyeol had seen too, and whether the intuition of a collapsing ARIA was still right, Hangyeol could not be sure either.

“We still have to go.” ARIA said. She had been listening to the two of them. “Whether I’m right or wrong, I don’t know either. But if we don’t go, I stay like this. It doesn’t stop. If going is a gamble, not going is a certainty.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. For the first time, ARIA had spoken with her own state as grounds. That going was a gamble and not going was a certainty. That was not intuition. That was judgment.

-----

At the sector boundary, Min Siwoo was waiting.

“I knew you’d find it.” Min Siwoo said. He had kept yesterday’s promise to come again — but not at the shelter where the three had been. He was waiting at the sector boundary. He had known the three would come this way.

“Move aside.” Hangyeol said.

“I will.” Min Siwoo said. “One thing first. The direction you’re heading — Altair is watching it, and so is the inspectorate. The three of them have started looking at the same place. Until yesterday they chased you. From today, they watch where you’re going.”

Dohee stopped.

“What does that mean.” Dohee asked.

“It means everyone has started to know where you’re going.” Min Siwoo said. “Where that thing beside you is trying to go. Altair knows, the inspectorate knows. We know too.”

“Do you know where it’s going.” Dohee asked.

“We do.” Min Siwoo smiled. “But what that place is — we don’t know that either. Everyone knows where it is; no one knows what it is. That’s why everyone is following. Because the thing beside you knows that place.”

Min Siwoo stepped aside.

“If you get there first, let us know. What it is.” Min Siwoo said. “We’ll just follow. You do the observing first.”

Dohee thought about Min Siwoo stepping aside. He could have blocked them and did not. Eidos wanted the three of them to reach that place. Not to catch the three — for the three to open that place. To Eidos, the three were an instrument of observation, and the place was the subject.

-----

The place was not far from Sector 7.

An hour on foot. ARIA held the direction, Dohee read the route, Hangyeol watched for pursuit. Neither Altair nor the inspectorate nor Eidos blocked them directly — as Min Siwoo had said, they were not blocking. They were following.

Midway along the route, Dohee stopped.

There was a mark on the floor. A white Altair mark — the trace of someone who had passed this route before the three arrived. The direction of the marks matched the direction the three were going. Altair had gone down this path before them, or had known they would come and laid the marks in advance.

A little further on, another trace. An inspectorate seal. Sealing tape on one side of the passage — torn. The inspectorate had sealed this place, and someone had torn the seal and gone in. Whether the inspectorate had sealed it and the inspectorate had torn it, or the one who tore it was someone else, Dohee could not tell.

“Something came before us.” Dohee said. “We are not first.”

“I know.” ARIA said. “That’s why I told you. I’m not the first.”

Dohee heard it. ARIA saying she was not the first had not been about ARIA alone. Things had come to this place before. Whether they had anything to do with ARIA, Dohee could not tell.

The place they reached was a decommissioned node facility.

Abandoned long ago. The node number plate remained on the wall, the number effaced. Not deleted like 7-7-4 — physically scratched out, erased by someone’s hand. Unmanaged time lay thick over the whole facility, and there was no trace of controlled entry; it looked like a place abandoned as not worth controlling.

And three houses had followed them to the abandoned facility.

Dohee looked at the contradiction. A place that looked not worth controlling, and Altair and the inspectorate and Eidos were watching it at once. Looking abandoned and being watched stood in the same place. One of the two was false.

“It’s here.” ARIA said.

ARIA’s form wavered before the facility entrance. The wavering that had not stopped all night slowed here, slightly. It did not stop, but it slowed — and ARIA’s words, that something would slow if they came here, were right.

-----

ARIA looked into the entrance.

“I’ve been here before.”

Hangyeol stopped.

“When.” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA said. Her form wavered. “But it’s not the first time. This entrance, the inside, the scratched-out number. I’ve seen them before.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. It was not the first time ARIA had claimed to know a place she had never been. At the temporary shelter, too, she had said it was not the first time. But that had been a place Dohee told them about, and ARIA might have known it through Dohee’s memory.

This place was different. It was Dohee’s first time here too. A place Dohee had never patrolled, outside Hwagam jurisdiction, absent from Dohee’s memory. Not a place ARIA could know through Dohee’s memory.

ARIA said she had been here before.

It was not a memory that had flowed into her. Not Dohee’s memory, not Altair’s interior, not any house’s information. It was a place ARIA herself had been. Hangyeol had been with ARIA for three years, and in those three years ARIA had never come here.

It was before the three years.

The ARIA from before Hangyeol knew her had been to this place.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said. Her voice was small. “I think I didn’t want to come here.”

“You just said we should go.” Hangyeol said.

“I said we had to. I didn’t say I wanted to.” ARIA’s form had stopped before the entrance. Not going in. “Having to go and wanting to come are different. I had to come here, and coming here frightens me. The last time I came, I don’t think it ended well.”

ARIA said it and fell silent. Looking into the entrance, not entering, silent.

-----

-----

Night.

Hangyeol cooked. Not because he thought Dohee had to eat. Because when there was food, it had to be made. He worked with what there was. It was not enough. He made it anyway.

ARIA watched it.

“Why do you do it?”

“Because we have to eat.”

“You could eat alone.”

“I don’t know yet.” Hangyeol said. “Whether ARIA needs to eat.”

ARIA heard it.

“You make it without knowing?”

“If you don’t know, better to have it ready.”

-----

## Vol.2 Ch.12 — Tremor

-----

ARIA went in first.

The moment they crossed the entrance, the air changed. Cooler than outside, and the sound was dead. Even footsteps seemed to vanish before they reached the walls.

ARIA, who had stood silent before the entrance without going in, after a long while went in first. Hangyeol followed, and Dohee was last. Hangyeol did not ask why ARIA had hesitated. If the one who hesitated went in first, you followed. That the ARIA who had said she did not want to come had gone in first — Dohee was conscious of it. ARIA had said that having to go and wanting to come were different, and going in first while afraid was different again.

The inside of the facility was dark.

A darkness without power — and in that darkness, small indicator lights were on. Point-like lights blinking at regular intervals along the wall panels, and the blinking had a period. There was no power, and the indicators were running.

Dohee looked at it. Indicator lights on in a facility cut from power meant one of two things. A separate power source, or the indicators drawing from somewhere other than the facility’s power. Either way, this facility was not entirely dead. It had been made to look dead.

“It didn’t stop.” ARIA said.

“What didn’t.” Hangyeol asked.

“This. All of it.” ARIA looked at the indicators in the dark. “It isn’t abandoned. It’s pretending to be stopped. Like me.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. *Like me*, ARIA had said. ARIA was a thing that looked abandoned and had not stopped, and the facility was the same — and ARIA recognized the facility as a thing like herself.

-----

Dust lay thick.

On the floor, on the panels, in the corners of the passage. Long-settled dust. Dust accumulated to the measure of the time no one had walked here. And above that dust, the indicators blinked. The dust said time had stopped; the indicators said time was flowing. The two were in the same space.

ARIA walked along the passage. Walked without hesitation.

“I know this place.” ARIA said.

“Know what.” Hangyeol asked.

“This passage. It bends left, and then there are stairs.” ARIA said. “I don’t know how. But I know. Bend left, and it’s the stairs.”

The passage bent left. The stairs appeared.

Hangyeol stopped. ARIA claiming to know places she had never seen had happened many times, but this was different. Until now it had been things flowing in; this time ARIA knew the way herself. Not flowed-in — as if memorized. As if walked.

“Did you live here once?” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA said. “But after the stairs there’s a large room. In that room—” ARIA stopped. “What’s in that room, I don’t know. It doesn’t come up that far.”

-----

Dohee examined a panel.

An administrative terminal — dead, but physically dead. When Dohee opened the panel, there was a storage medium inside. An isolated medium, cut from external access, the same kind as the isolated terminal in the basement archive.

When Dohee activated it, a separate power source was alive. The same power that kept the indicators on.

Records came up. Most of them erased.

The same way 7-7-4 had been erased. Data deleted, the deletion reason blank, the deleting party unrecorded. Not just one 7-7-4 — the records of this entire facility had been erased the same way. Node numbers, entry records, operation logs, all deleted, all with blank reasons.

Someone had erased this facility deliberately.

Dohee held that certainty for the first time. 7-7-4 could have been an accidental deletion, but an entire facility erased the same way was not accident. Someone had made this place into nothing — and left the indicators on. Recorded it as dead, and kept it alive.

Among the erased records, one remained. A deletion left unfinished. An entry record, half erased.

There was a date. Before Dohee became an executor — close to the date 7-7-4 had been deleted.

-----

Hangyeol looked at something else.

While Dohee read the records, Hangyeol read the space. The width of the passages, the layout of the rooms, the lines people had moved along. Things learned from running. Which spaces people had lived in, which spaces were only passed through — Hangyeol could tell them apart.

“People lived here.” Hangyeol said.

“How do you know.” Dohee asked.

“You learn it running.” Hangyeol said. “A lived-in space grows lines of movement. The paths people take often — the floor is different. Look here.” Hangyeol pointed at the passage floor. Dust had settled, and there was a single strip where the dust lay thin. “People walked here. Every day. For a long time.”

Dohee looked at the strip. As Hangyeol said, the dust had not settled evenly; the path people had walked held thinner dust, and the path led toward the large room by the stairs.

“They erased the records.” Dohee said. “They couldn’t erase the lines.”

“You can erase a record. A trace doesn’t erase.” Hangyeol said. “A record is a thing that was made. A trace is a thing that remained. What was made can be erased. What remained can’t.”

Dohee heard it. Dohee read records and Hangyeol read traces. The eraser had erased the records and failed to erase the traces. With Dohee and Hangyeol together, what the eraser had missed could be seen.

-----

They reached the large room.

The large room past the stairs, the room ARIA had said would not come up that far. The room was empty. No furniture, no equipment — walls and floor only.

There was a mark on the wall.

The place where something had hung. The trace of a panel or a nameplate removed. The wall’s color was different only there. Removed long enough ago that the difference had faded toward the surrounding wall, but the shape remained.

Dohee looked at the mark. The place a nameplate had hung. The nameplate had been taken down, and what had been written on it could not be known.

But below the place the nameplate had been, there were scratch marks on the wall.

Dohee went closer. The marks of someone scraping the wall by hand to erase something. The same as the node number at the facility entrance, scratched out. It had been letters. Scraped almost beyond reading, but part of a letter remained.

One letter of the alphabet was visible.

An A. What came before and after was scraped beyond reading, and a single letter confirmed nothing. The things that began with A or contained A were beyond counting.

Dohee tried not to read it. Trying to read something from one letter was conjecture, and conjecture was not confirmation. But apart from Dohee’s trying not to read it, beside her, something was reading that letter.

ARIA’s form had stopped in front of the letter.

-----

ARIA walked further into the room.

At the end of the large room, one door stood closed. The other doors in the facility were open or removed; this door alone was closed, and intact. An indicator blinked beside it — the brightest indicator in the facility.

ARIA stood before the door.

“It’s here.” ARIA said.

“What’s here.” Hangyeol asked.

“The place I said we had to go. The place where I thought it would stop.” ARIA looked at the door. “It’s here. Inside this door.”

Dohee looked at the door. It had a lock — everywhere else erased and stripped, and this door alone was locked. The eraser had erased everything else and left this door locked. Erasing and locking were different. What was erased had been meant to cease; what was locked had been meant to remain, and stay closed.

Hangyeol moved toward the door.

“Don’t open it.” Dohee said.

Hangyeol stopped.

“Three houses followed us to this facility.” Dohee said. “What they want may be inside this door. If we open it, we open what they want for them.”

“We don’t have to open it.” ARIA said.

Hangyeol and Dohee looked at ARIA.

“We don’t have to open it.” ARIA said again. Without taking her eyes off the door. “What’s inside — I know it.”

ARIA did not say what it was; only that she knew without opening the door. ARIA’s form wavered before the door. The wavering had slowed since entering the facility, and before this door it quickened again. The wavering of the ARIA who had said it would stop here was at its worst at the very threshold of the place where it would stop.

-----

**— Volume 2, end —**

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## DL-01 “Never Asked” — Hangyeol·Dohee

*Placement: between Vol.2 and Vol.3*

-----

It was morning.

Dohee came into the common space. The door that had creaked until yesterday opened without a sound.

Dohee looked at the door. Opened and closed it once more. There was no sound.

Hangyeol was eating something inside.

-----

“When did you fix that?”

“Yesterday.”

“Why didn’t you say?”

Hangyeol thought. He did not think long.

“You never asked.”

-----

Dohee paused a moment.

“That’s true, but.”

Dohee sat down. She considered writing it and did not. A door repair was not an event for recording.

But strangely, it stayed in memory.

-----

*DL-01 “Never Asked” — end*

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO (EN)

# Volume 3 — Anomalous

# Volume 3 — Anomalous

-----

-----

Dawn.

Inside the facility. Dohee and ARIA were sleeping. Hangyeol was not. He had woken early. He always did.

He found something to do. There was a thing broken. A latticed window. The hinge had gone weak. There were no parts. He used what there was. There was wire. He bound it. It was not firm. It still did not open.

Hangyeol looked at it.

Enough. For now.

When Dohee woke, the window was fixed. She did not ask. Hangyeol did not explain.

If it is needed, you do it. That was all.

-----

============================================================

## ◆ Daily Log — DL-01 “Never Asked”

============================================================

## DL-01 “Never Asked” — Hangyeol, Dohee

*Placement: between Vol.2 and Vol.3*

-----

Morning.

Dohee came into the common room. The door that had creaked until yesterday opened without a sound.

Dohee looked at the door. Opened it once more, closed it. No sound.

Hangyeol was eating something at the back.

-----

“When did you fix that?”

“Yesterday.”

“Why didn’t you say?”

Hangyeol thought. Not for long.

“You never asked.”

-----

Dohee was quiet a moment.

“That’s true.”

Dohee sat down. She considered writing it down and did not. A door repair was not an event for the record.

But strangely, it stayed.

-----

*DL-01 “Never Asked” — end*

-----

## Vol.3 Ch.13 — Reflection

-----

The door did not open.

The air before the door was heavy. Breathing, something cold reached the inside of the lungs.

The three stood before the door, and no one opened it. Dohee had said not to open it, Hangyeol had stopped, ARIA had said they did not have to. The door stayed closed, and the indicator blinked beside it.

ARIA’s form wavered before the door.

The wavering did not stop. What had slowed since they entered the facility had quickened again before this door, and after quickening it did not return. The cycle of sharpening was gone; only the time of scattering remained. As far as Hangyeol could see, ARIA’s outline barely held.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol said.

No answer.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol said again.

“Yes.” ARIA said. A moment later. “Yes. Hangyeol.”

Hangyeol heard the gap. It had taken ARIA time to answer to her own name. Not Hangyeol’s name — her own. It was taking ARIA time to respond to being called ARIA.

-----

“Who is Dohee?”

ARIA asked.

Dohee looked at ARIA. Hangyeol looked at ARIA. ARIA’s form was turned toward Dohee, and turned toward her, it was asking.

“Me.” Dohee said.

“Ah.” ARIA said. “I know. I knew. For a moment I didn’t.”

Dohee heard it. For a moment she didn’t. ARIA had forgotten Dohee for a moment. The ARIA who had first spoken Dohee’s name in the passage, the ARIA who had known a name she had never heard — had forgotten Dohee for a moment.

“I know Hangyeol.” ARIA said. “Hangyeol I don’t forget. But other things keep changing places. I knew who Dohee was, and for a moment there was a different Dohee. There are several Dohees. Inside me.”

“What do you mean.” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA said. “I know there’s one Dohee, but inside me there are several. I don’t know which is the real Dohee. The Dohee in front of me now feels real, but I can’t be sure.”

ARIA’s form scattered and gathered. The gathering took longer than before.

Dohee thought about what it meant. Memories were flowing into ARIA, and inside the flowed-in memories there were several Dohees. Dohees who were not Dohee. ARIA was receiving other Dohees from somewhere, and could not tell those Dohees from the Dohee in front of her.

-----

The indicator changed.

Dohee saw it first. The blink period of the indicator by the door had quickened — a period that had held steady since they entered. That period was quickening at the same rate as ARIA’s wavering.

There was change in the wall panels too. Lights had come on in several panels that had been dark — and in a facility without power, that meant the separate source keeping the indicators alive was spreading wider. The facility that had looked dead was waking.

“This is not something we did.” Dohee said.

Hangyeol looked at the panels. Neither Hangyeol nor Dohee had touched anything. None of the three had laid a hand on the facility, and the facility was responding.

ARIA was here.

Dohee looked at it. The speed of the facility’s waking matched ARIA’s wavering. When ARIA scattered, the indicators quickened; when ARIA gathered, the panels steadied. The facility was responding to ARIA. No one had opened a door — the facility was responding to the fact that ARIA was here.

“ARIA.” Dohee said. “The facility is responding to you.”

“I know.” ARIA said. “This place knows me. It wasn’t that I knew this place — this place knew me. When I said I wasn’t the first, it meant this place remembers me.”

-----

Dohee went back to the facility’s storage media.

As the facility woke, several dead terminals came alive, and Dohee opened them. Among the erased records, things had revived — not all of it, fragments where the deletion had not finished.

There were entry records. Many dates, the same identifier repeating; the identifier was erased, but the pattern of entry remained. Someone had come to this facility regularly — in, out, in again — and after the last entry there was only an entry and no exit. After coming in, no record of going out.

In another fragment there was a record of something’s state, measured at intervals. Figures in sequence — what had been measured, erased. The place where the name of the measured subject should have been was scratched out, the same way as the scratched letters on the wall of the large room.

Dohee looked at the figures. The measurements changed over time — unstable at first, stabilizing in the middle, and in the final record unstable again. The shape of the graph was familiar, and what it resembled, Dohee did not say even to herself.

She did not tell Hangyeol. There was nothing yet to tell. That a graph felt familiar was an impression, and an impression was not a record. The name of the measured subject was erased, and guessing at what was erased was not Dohee’s way. Dohee recorded the fragment only, and did not record the feeling of familiarity. The unrecorded things were accumulating. Starting from the file left behind at Hwagam. The things that were closed but not gone — they were here too.

But not concluding and not knowing were different things.

-----

Hangyeol was watching ARIA.

ARIA scattering and gathering, forgetting Dohee and remembering her, the facility responding to ARIA — Hangyeol was watching it. For three years there had been an ARIA Hangyeol knew. The ARIA who could not remember, the ARIA who asked questions, the ARIA who was afraid. That ARIA, Hangyeol knew.

The ARIA now was different.

Not different — the parts Hangyeol did not know were growing. Over the ARIA Hangyeol knew, an ARIA Hangyeol did not know was layering. The way ARIA saw several Dohees, Hangyeol was beginning to see several ARIAs. Whether the ARIA he had protected and the ARIA the facility responded to were the same thing, Hangyeol could not be sure.

For the first time, Hangyeol was afraid of ARIA.

Admitting the fear felt like betraying her. In three years, Hangyeol had never been afraid of ARIA. There had been times ARIA was in danger, but ARIA herself had never been frightening. Now ARIA herself was frightening. There was something inside ARIA that Hangyeol did not know, and it was waking.

Hangyeol did not look toward a window. This facility had no windows, and in a space without windows, for the first time, Hangyeol did not know where to look. Not standing where he could be seen was Hangyeol’s way, and here he could not tell whether every place was a place he could be seen from or not. The facility was watching, through ARIA.

-----

The indicator quickened once more.

And the whole facility hummed, very faintly. Less a sound than a vibration — a low, even trembling that came from the floor and the walls at once. The dead facility had not merely lit its indicators; somewhere deep, something had begun to turn. When Hangyeol set his hand on the floor, the trembling reached his fingertips. The facility was alive, and in the middle of coming alive.

ARIA’s wavering reached its peak. The form nearly lost the shape of a person. The outline loosened, and the way light passed through made ARIA look not like a form but like a stain.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said. The voice was clearer than the form. “I’m afraid.”

“I know.” Hangyeol said.

“But I don’t know if what’s frightening is me, or if the one afraid of me is me.” ARIA said. “This place recognizes me, and I don’t know if being recognized is welcome or dangerous. Whether the me that was here is the same as the me now — I don’t know that either.”

And a sound came from the door.

A mechanical sound. The sound of the lock operating — none of the three had touched the door, and the door operated on its own. The indicator blinked once, long.

Text appeared on the panel. Dohee read it.

*Authentication complete.*

The three looked at the door. The sound of the lock releasing came, but the door did not open. It had only released. Someone, or something, had been authenticated, and the door was now in a state where it could be opened.

Whose authentication it was, Dohee could not tell. Whether Dohee had been authenticated, or Hangyeol, or ARIA. What the facility had checked and what it had passed, the panel did not say. Authentication complete — only those words.

ARIA’s form, in that moment, briefly stopped. The wavering ceased. Whether the ceasing was stability, or something else, Dohee could not tell.

“I think it’s me.” ARIA said. “The one authenticated. I think it recognized me.”

How ARIA knew that, whether ARIA had truly been authenticated, whether it was good or bad — no one could ask.

-----

-----

## Calculation-01 — “The Error”

*Placement: between Vol.3 Ch.13 and Ch.14*
*POV: Julius / the Altair calculation hall*

-----

91%.

There was a number on the screen. Someone looked at the number. A research assistant.

“Executor Claire’s calculation result.”

From across the room, someone spoke.

“It is 94%.”

The assistant looked at the screen. It was 91%.

“It is 91%.”

“My calculation is 94%.”

The assistant looked again. It was 91%.

“Why do they differ.”

“I don’t know. They should be the same, and they differ.”

“Which is correct.”

There was a silence.

“That is the problem.”

The assistant dug further into the data. There was a figure from a few days earlier. 89%. The day before that, another figure.

The same subject, calculated by the same method, with different results.

“This subject is.”

“7-7-4.”

The assistant looked at the screen. The figure kept changing. There was an error. It was within the margin of error — and for the same subject, variation within this range was abnormal.

“Shall I report it.”

“Still calculating.”

The assistant turned to leave, then stopped.

“Have you eaten.”

“I don’t know.”

“…Yes.”

He left.

A figure rose on the screen. 92%. Different again.

-----

*Calculation-01 — end*

## Vol.3 Ch.14 — Echo

-----

The door opened by hand.

Even after the lock released, the door did not open on its own; someone had to push it. Hangyeol pushed. Things like this were always Hangyeol’s. Dohee had not yet considered whether that was natural or not. She had not said don’t open it — and Dohee also knew that the place for saying don’t open it had already passed.

Inside the door was dark. The ceiling was high. In the dark, the outlines of pipes were visible. There was the smell of old metal. A place where machines had breathed longer than people.

Indicator light leaked through the gap of the door and the room’s outline emerged. Circular. A room of walls joined in a curve — the first curve they had seen in this facility. The entrance passage, the large room, all of it had been straight lines; only this room was round.

In the middle stood a single chair.

Only the chair. No desk, no bed, no other furniture — one chair at the center of a circular room, set not toward any wall but toward the whole of the room. A seat for observing. A seat for seeing something, or a seat for being seen by something.

On the walls were dead monitors. Monitors ringed the circular wall, all dark. Below them, the traces of recording equipment — some removed, some remaining, and the remaining ones thick with dust.

Only the chair was different. Everything else was old, and the chair alone looked as if it were not. There was no dust. The whole room was a thing steeped in centuries, and the chair alone seemed to stand outside that time.

-----

ARIA went into the room.

The moment she entered, the room woke.

The monitors on the wall came on at once. Light entered the dead screens, and indicators lit in sequence along the circular wall. The facility-wide vibration was strongest in this room. The floor trembled, and Hangyeol felt the trembling through the soles of his feet.

ARIA stopped.

“ARIA?” Hangyeol said.

“This.” ARIA said. Her form wavered. “I didn’t do this. This is something I don’t know.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. He had thought ARIA responded to the facility — and now ARIA was startled by the facility’s response. ARIA had not come knowing this room. She had come where her feet led, and the room she arrived at had recognized her and woken, and ARIA had not expected it.

“This place knows me.” ARIA said. “And it’s my first time here. That it knows me frightens me. The me I don’t know — this place knows that me.”

ARIA’s form moved toward the center of the circular room, as if drawn. Toward the chair. The chair was set facing ARIA, and ARIA was moving toward it. Not going by choosing to go — being pulled.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol said. “Don’t go there.”

ARIA stopped. In front of the chair.

The floor in front of the chair was different from everywhere else. Worn. The floor coating was shallowly hollowed, as if something had stood in that spot for a very long time, very often. Not the mark of sitting. The mark of standing. The marks of things that had stood before the chair, without sitting.

“Why?” ARIA asked.

Hangyeol could not answer. He could not explain why he had said don’t go, and there were no grounds that the chair was dangerous. But there was a feeling that if ARIA sat in that seat, something would end. A feeling was not grounds, and Hangyeol had spoken without grounds.

-----

Dohee was looking at the monitors.

Records rose on the lit screens. As the facility woke, the dead data was being restored — not all of it. Restorations that stopped partway, broken characters, screens that loaded by half. Among them were things that could be read.

State records.

The same kind as the fragments from the large room, but more had survived here. Measurements in time order, and the measured items carried names. Stabilization index. Memory accumulation. Self-consistency. Dohee looked at the items.

Stabilization failed. One line of the record said so.

The next line: memory accumulation past threshold. The line after: self-consistency collapse.

Dohee looked at the three lines. Stabilization failure, memory accumulation, self collapse. The three lines stood in order, and the order was the same as what Dohee had watched these past days. ARIA had said the memories would not stop, had seen several Dohees, had taken time to respond to her own name. Stabilization failure, memory accumulation, self collapse.

She did not tell Hangyeol. There was nothing yet to tell, and there was no proof that the subject of this record was ARIA. That the same phenomena had occurred in the same order was an impression, and an impression was not a record. Dohee did not record the impression.

But she did not stop looking at the monitor either.

-----

Hangyeol was looking at a different monitor.

A different screen from the records Dohee read. Hangyeol was not someone who read records — he was someone who saw structure before letters. Something like a list stood on the screen, the same format repeating, and Hangyeol looked at the repetition.

The same field, in many rows. Each row held the same items with only the values different — Dohee, reading them, would have known what the values were, but Hangyeol did not read values. He saw only that the same place repeated.

That the place had been filled many times.

Hangyeol saw that. There was a place for something, and the place had been filled not once but many times — filled, emptied, filled again, emptied again. Into the same place, different things had gone in and out.

It looked like ARIA’s place.

Hangyeol had the thought. There were no grounds, and no proof that the list on the screen concerned ARIA. But the place looked like ARIA’s place, and the place did not look like a first. It looked as if someone had been beside ARIA before, as if something not-ARIA had been in ARIA’s place first.

The three years might not have been the first.

It was not about ARIA. That someone had been beside ARIA, that the place had been filled — that might not have been the first, and who that person had been was not important. It was not important, and the thought kept returning.

Hangyeol turned his eyes from the screen.

Why he turned them, Hangyeol could not explain. There was nothing dangerous on the screen; he simply did not want to look longer. Looking longer, he would come to know something, and that something would make him small. He did not want to be small, and why he did not want it, he could not explain.

-----

ARIA did not sit in the chair.

She stood before it. She had come this far as if drawn, and she did not sit. ARIA, too, seemed to know that sitting would begin something.

“Something sat here.” ARIA said.

“What.” Dohee asked.

“I don’t know.” ARIA said. “But someone sat in this chair. For a long time. Many times. Not one. It sat many times, and each time it was different. In the same chair, different things.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol looked at Dohee. What ARIA said was the same as the repetition Hangyeol had seen on the screen. The same place, different things. ARIA had not looked at the screen, and she was saying the same thing.

“Do you know who it was?” Hangyeol asked.

“No.” ARIA said. “But it was like me. I think it wavered like me. I think it was afraid like me.” ARIA’s form scattered before the chair. “But it isn’t me. I’m the first. I’m the first, and it feels like I’m not the first. I don’t know what that means either.”

ARIA did not understand what she herself had said. First, and not the first. ARIA spoke it without knowing it, and speaking it, did not know what it meant. The ones who knew were the monitors, and Dohee, and Hangyeol. Only ARIA did not know.

-----

The innermost panel was the last to restore.

Dohee went toward it. The deepest place in the circular room, the wall directly facing the chair. A record rose on that panel — later than the other monitors, and clearer. The last data the facility brought back.

A list.

Items arranged vertically. Dohee read.

*Cycle 1 — Observer: Human*
*Cycle 2 — Observer: Human*
*Cycle 3 — Observer: Human*
*Cycle 4 — [DATA CORRUPTED]*
*Cycle 5 — [DATA CORRUPTED]*
*Cycle 6 — [DATA CORRUPTED]*

Below, there was room for more lines. The place where Cycle 7 should have been — there, not even an item rose. Not a restoration stopped partway; a place not yet written. Cycles 1 through 3 were Human, 4 through 6 were corrupted, and 7 was empty.

Dohee looked at the list.

Observer. The one who watches. It was the first time Dohee had seen the word — a word that existed in no record of Hwagam, of the inspectorate, of any house. Each Cycle had an Observer, and the Observers of Cycles 1, 2, 3 had been Human. Human beings. After that, corrupted.

Dohee tried not to think about what the list meant. To think about the meaning was to conjecture, and conjecture was not confirmation. What a Cycle was, what an Observer was, why they had been human, why what came after was erased — Dohee knew none of it. What she knew was that three lines said Human.

Three lines said Human.

Dohee did not look beyond that. What the erased entries had been filled with, what was standing before the chair in this room right now — she did not connect them. Connecting them would make a line, and that line was one Dohee could not record.

Behind her, ARIA stood before the chair.

The room was awake toward ARIA, the monitors were lit upon ARIA, the chair was set facing ARIA. Cycles 1 through 3 were Human, 4 through 6 were corrupted, and 7 was empty. What the empty place was waiting for, Dohee did not know.

ARIA could not see the list. The monitors were lit upon ARIA, and ARIA could not see what was written on the screens that lit her. What she herself was looking at — only ARIA did not know.

-----

## Vol.3 Ch.15 — Signal

-----

Dohee noticed that the facility was sending a signal.

Since the monitors in the circular room woke, one panel had been working differently from the rest. The other panels were restoring records; that panel was sending something out. Its output indicator pulsed at a steady period. Not a signal the facility received — a signal it sent.

“This is going outside.” Dohee said.

“Where.” Hangyeol asked.

“I don’t know.” Dohee said. “That a signal is going out is certain; where it goes does not show on this panel. But at this output, it crosses at least a sector.”

Dohee thought about what it meant. A facility dead for centuries had woken when ARIA entered, and the woken facility was sending a signal. If the signal crossed sectors, there was a receiving side. Who received it, Dohee did not know — but she thought of the ones who had followed when the three found the facility. Altair, Eidos, the inspectorate. If they had followed the facility, they would know it had woken.

“It is announcing that we’re here.” Dohee said. “Not us. ARIA.”

Dohee looked once more at the facility status panel. One line stood on it.

*Facility Status: Observer Chamber — Active*

Observer Chamber. Dohee recorded the words, and did not write what they meant. The words were all that could be written.

-----

Altair, the same hour.

A signal rose on Claire’s calculation array.

“Signal detected from an unidentified facility.” The report came up. “Outskirts of Sector 7. A facility inactive for centuries. Activation confirmed.”

“Contents.” Claire asked.

“Undecodable. The signal format differs from current house standards. An old format.”

The signal pattern rose on Claire’s array, and Claire tried to read it, and it could not be read. It was not house standard, not a format entered into Claire’s calculations. What was not entered could not be processed, and what could not be processed was a variable.

“The facility’s name.” Claire asked.

“Not in the records. This facility is registered in no archive of any house. A signal is coming from a facility that does not exist.”

A facility that does not exist. Claire’s array went briefly empty. A thing that did not exist sending a signal was a contradiction, and contradictions did not enter Claire’s calculations.

“Do we proceed with a securing operation.” the report asked.

“No.” Claire said.

The report paused. Claire’s answer was not the expected one. Since the targets had been changed to the three, Altair’s procedure was securing. Finding the source of the signal, the procedure was to secure it.

“We observe.” Claire said.

“I ask the reason.”

“Before understanding, we do not remove.” Claire said. “A threat, we remove. But what is undefined is not a threat. What Altair removes is threats. A threat must be known to be a threat. If we remove this facility before knowing what it is, what signal it sends, why a facility that does not exist exists — then we have removed what we do not know. Removing what one does not know is not order. It is the making of a blank. Altair does not make blanks.”

The calculation output, and the output held no feeling. But the output was observation, not securing, and Claire had deferred a securing for the first time.

“Decode the signal. Observe the facility. The three targets are inside it. Know the facility, and we know the targets. The order is reversed.”

-----

Eidos, the same hour.

The signal rose on Min Siwoo’s terminal as well.

“Observer facility activation confirmed.” A subordinate reported. “Outskirts of Sector 7. Unregistered facility. The signal format is being decoded.”

“Observer.” Min Siwoo said. Smiling. “That word is in the signal?”

“It’s in the facility status display. The name Observer Chamber is included in the signal.”

Min Siwoo heard the word. The one who watches. Observation was what Eidos did, and that facility carried the name of the watcher. Before Eidos, something had existed that held observation as its name.

“Do we approach.” the subordinate asked.

“Why?” Min Siwoo asked.

“We should preempt. Altair will have received the same signal. The inspectorate too. Securing the facility first gives us the information advantage.”

“Preempt it and the observation ends.” Min Siwoo said. The smile held. “Secure the facility and the facility stops. A stopped facility gives no data. Right now that facility is alive. Alive and sending a signal. Observing what is alive is our work. Catch it and that ends.”

The subordinate paused.

“Then what do we do.”

“Wait.” Min Siwoo said. “The three are inside. The three woke the facility. Watch what the three do with the facility, and we learn what the facility is. Catch the three and the three stop and the facility stops. Don’t catch them, and the three keep waking it. All we have to do is watch.”

Min Siwoo looked at the terminal. The signal kept coming. The three were doing something inside the facility, and it was becoming a signal and coming out.

“Tell me if Altair moves.” Min Siwoo said. “If Altair goes in to take them, then we move too. Not because we want to take them — because if Altair tries to end our observation, we have to stop it.”

-----

Inside the facility, ARIA.

ARIA could not step back from the chair.

She tried to step back and could not. It was not her feet being pulled — something was holding ARIA. Her form tilted toward the chair, returned, tilted again.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said.

“Yeah.”

“Someone is looking at me.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. In the room were only Hangyeol and Dohee and ARIA, no one else.

“It’s just us here.” Hangyeol said.

“No.” ARIA said. “It’s not memory. Until now, memories came in. Things that had passed. But this isn’t something passed. Someone is looking at me now. Right now. From far away. Several of them.”

Dohee heard it. The facility was sending a signal, and ARIA feeling watched and the facility sending were the same hour. If the facility sent a signal, the receiving side was looking at ARIA — and ARIA was feeling that gaze.

“I don’t know who’s looking at me.” ARIA said. “But they know me. The people looking at me — I think they know me. People I don’t know, know me.” ARIA’s form scattered wide. “I don’t know why they know me. To know me, you’d have to know what I am, and I don’t know what I am — and there are people who know me. That’s what’s strange.”

People ARIA had never seen were looking at ARIA. The ARIA that ARIA did not know — someone outside knew it. The facility made it possible. The woken facility was shining ARIA outward.

-----

Hangyeol was looking at the chair.

The chair ARIA could not step back from. A chair something had sat in — a place filled not once but many times — and Hangyeol tried not to look at it. The more he tried, the more he saw it.

There had been things that sat in the chair. Things Hangyeol did not know. Before ARIA came, before Hangyeol knew ARIA, something had sat in that chair. Many times. Differently each time.

He hated the thought.

Why he hated it, Hangyeol could not explain. What had sat in the chair had done him no harm. It was from before he met ARIA, a thing that had nothing to do with him. Nothing to do with him — and looking at the chair, something in him turned. Hangyeol tried to take his eyes off the chair, and could not.

ARIA stood before that chair. The ARIA Hangyeol had protected, standing before a chair Hangyeol did not know. The three years in which Hangyeol had thought he knew ARIA were growing small before that chair. Hangyeol might not have been the first. Hangyeol might not have been the first to come to ARIA.

The chair was set like a place where something was meant to be. That it was not ARIA’s place — Hangyeol knew that. And ARIA stood before it.

Hangyeol pushed the thought away. He pushed it away and it came back.

-----

Outside the facility, far away.

Altair and Eidos and the inspectorate were not the only ones who received the signal.

Far from the sector, in a place under no house’s jurisdiction, one person received it. That person did not try to decode it like Altair, did not try to observe it like Eidos. The moment the signal arrived, the person knew what it was.

The person sat before an old terminal. Not a house-standard terminal. A terminal that used the same format as the facility’s signal. The signal rose clearly on that terminal, and the person could read it.

On the screen: Observer Chamber Active.

Below it, one more line rose. A line that rose on no one else’s terminal — a line only that terminal could receive.

*Cycle 7 — Observer: Detected*

The person looked at that line for a long time.

“Sooner than expected.”

-----

## Vol.3 Ch.16 — Resonance

-----

Dohee kept looking at the panels.

The more the facility came alive, the more data restored. Not all of it. The boundary between what restored and what did not was uneven. Some data came back and some stayed broken to the end, and what made the difference, Dohee could not tell. Whether the facility was choosing what to revive, or whether what remained was surfacing on its own.

On a newly restored panel there was a list. Dohee read.

*Observer Candidate — Status: Stable*
*Observer Candidate — Status: Failed*
*Observer Candidate — Status: Failed*

Three lines. There was more below the three, broken. Observer Candidate. Stable, Failed — one stable, two failed.

It was the record of the same thing attempted many times. Attempted and failed, attempted again and failed, and then stabilized. What had been attempted, what had stabilized, Dohee could not tell. The word ARIA was not there, nor any name. Candidate, status. That was all.

Dohee recorded the list. What it meant, she did not write.

-----

Hangyeol did not want to look at the chair.

But the chair kept entering his sight. Turn his eyes where he would — the room was circular, and from any direction the chair hung at the edge of his vision. A circular room left no direction to escape into. Hangyeol set his back against the wall farthest from the chair.

ARIA was not moving from before it.

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. She had said she would not sit, and not sitting, she stood before it. The chair seemed to be holding ARIA.

Something had sat in that chair.

Hangyeol tried not to think it, and the more he tried, the clearer it became. Before ARIA, there had been someone. It had sat in the chair, many times, different each time. What it had been, he did not know — and in the place where it had been, ARIA now stood, and Hangyeol, who had spent three years with ARIA, stood in the corner with his back to the wall.

For the first time, Hangyeol thought that he could lose ARIA.

Not in the way of Altair taking her. Until now the danger had come from outside — Altair, Eidos, the inspectorate — and when they tried to take ARIA, Hangyeol had only to block the outside danger.

This danger was coming from inside. From inside ARIA. The memories flowing in, the forgetting of Dohee, the facility recognizing her, ARIA unable to step back from the chair. It was not a danger Hangyeol could block. He knew how to block the outside. How to block the inside, Hangyeol did not know.

Hangyeol took his back off the wall. And set it back. He moved because he did not know where to go, and stopped because there was nowhere to go.

-----

ARIA looked at the chair.

She had tried not to look long, and looked. The moment she looked at the chair, she saw an unfamiliar hand.

A hand resting on the armrest. The shape of a human hand — whose, ARIA did not know. Not her own. ARIA had a form and could make the shape of a hand, but that hand was not ARIA’s. Someone else’s hand.

And it was familiar.

ARIA had never seen the hand, and no memory of it had flowed in. And it was familiar. A first-seen thing that was not strange. Like the other name layering over Hangyeol’s name yesterday — an unfamiliar hand lay on the chair, and it was not unfamiliar.

ARIA drew her eyes from the hand.

In that moment, a voice.

“It’s all right.”

Not Hangyeol, not Dohee. There were only the three of them in the room, and the voice was none of the three. The same words as when Hangyeol told ARIA it was all right — not the same way. An older way. The way of repeating words used many times.

And for a moment, the room looked different.

There was light. It was not the circular room of now — the same space, another time. Someone sat in the chair. The face could not be seen; it was simply there, there as if being there were natural, as if it had been there long.

ARIA closed her eyes.

ARIA looked around the room, and no one was there, and the voice was gone.

“Hangyeol.” ARIA said.

“Yeah.”

“What did you just say?”

“I didn’t say anything.” Hangyeol said.
