ARIA looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol was by the wall and had not opened his mouth, and Dohee was by the panels and had not spoken. ARIA looked around the room again and there was nothing.

There was no voice. And ARIA had heard it.

-----

A final restoration notice rose on Dohee’s terminal.

The file that had been locked longest in the circular room. It restored later than the other data, and only part of it survived. Dohee opened it.

A Cycle record. The same format as the Observer list, but this time the entry was different.

*Cycle 3 — Observer: Human — Status: Completed*

Dohee looked at the line.

Completed. A word absent from every Cycle record she had seen. Cycles 4, 5, 6 were DATA CORRUPTED, and Cycles 1, 2 went only as far as Observer: Human. But Cycle 3 carried Completed.

What Completed meant, Dohee could not tell. That a cycle had been completed. What had been completed, how it had been completed, whether completion was a good outcome or a bad one. Nothing was written.

But for the first time, there was a record of success.

Until now Dohee had thought this facility was a record of repeated failure — a place erased and broken with nothing remaining. And there was a Completed. At least once, it had completed. One Cycle had gone to the end.

Dohee looked below the line. The next line was broken. What had happened after Completed, what became of it once complete — that could not be seen.

Dohee recorded Completed, and did not write what it meant. The word was all that could be written.

-----

Outside the facility, far away.

Before the old terminal, the person watched a new line rise.

*Cycle 7 — Observer: Detected — Compatibility: [LOADING…]*

Compatibility. It was the first time that word had risen. An item that had never appeared in the previous Cycles. The figure was loading, and the loading did not end.

The person looked at the line.

Compatibility rising meant the facility was calculating something, and when the calculation finished, a figure would come. What the figure meant, the person knew — and knowing, waited for the loading to end.

The terminal did not cut out. The signal continued.

The person stood.

That terminal had been built to receive only this signal. A terminal kept on for decades for this signal alone. The signal had come. They had to arrive before the loading finished.

-----

## Vol.3 Ch.17 — Compatibility

-----

The panel was raising a number.

*Compatibility: 31%*

Dohee looked at it. The figure that had been loading when it first appeared in the chamber now read 31. Compatibility with what, measured against what standard, the panel did not say. All that could be known was that the number was rising.

Dohee looked at another panel. As the facility came alive, more records were being restored, and in the file that had come alive this time there was a list. The format was one she knew. Similar to the Candidate list — but the entries were different.

*Candidate 01 — Compatibility: 4%*
*Candidate 02 — Compatibility: 8%*
*Candidate 03 — Compatibility: 12%*
*Candidate 04 — Compatibility: 19%*
*Candidate 05 — Compatibility: 27%*

The names were all erased; only the Candidate numbers remained, each with its compatibility. 4, 8, 27. The same entry, different results. Different things, measured against the same standard.

Dohee did not compare the panel’s 31 with the numbers on this list. Comparing made conjecture. Conjecture was not record. Dohee recorded the figures, and did not write what they meant.

But she did not stop looking at the list. Candidate 05’s compatibility was 27, and the panel’s figure now was 31. Higher than 27.

-----

ARIA looked at the seat.

She tried not to look, and looked again. The seat faced ARIA, and the room was circular, so wherever she looked the seat caught the edge of her sight. ARIA tried to pull her eyes off the seat.

In that moment it changed again.

This time it was clearer. Someone was sitting in the seat, and there was no face. The light came from the reverse direction so there was only a form, and the form sat there, waiting for something.

And a voice came — not from inside the room, but from inside the scene.

“Which one are you.”

It was a question. Who was asking could not be seen — not the form in the seat; it came from another direction. An answer should have followed, and none could be heard. There was a voice answering, and what the answer was could not be heard.

The scene cut off.

ARIA was in the circular room again. Dohee was looking at the panel, and Hangyeol was by the wall. Nothing had changed.

“I don’t know if this is my memory,” ARIA said.

“What did you see,” Hangyeol asked.

“Someone was sitting there. And someone asked. Which one are you.” ARIA’s form wavered. “I don’t know what it means. I couldn’t hear the answer either. It might not be my memory. But it was inside me.”

Hangyeol heard it. Which one. It was a question about order — something had repeated, and the repetition had an order. Hangyeol disliked that question, and could not explain why.

-----

“Let’s get out of here,” Hangyeol said.

ARIA looked at him.

“I can’t go.”

Hangyeol stopped. It was the first time ARIA had told him she couldn’t. For three years, when Hangyeol said go, ARIA went; when he said danger, she drew back; she followed his judgment. That was the way between the two of them.

“Why,” Hangyeol asked.

“I have to be here,” ARIA said. “There’s a reason I have to be here. I don’t know it yet, but I have to be here. If you say go, I want to go. This time I can’t.”

“ARIA,” Hangyeol said. “Those people are coming. Altair, Eidos, all of them. The facility sent a signal and now everyone knows. Stay here and you’ll be taken.”

“I know,” ARIA said. “I still can’t go.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. It was the first time ARIA had not listened to him, and it was not refusal out of unwillingness. She had said *can’t*, said she had to be here — and when Hangyeol asked the reason, ARIA did not know it either. Not knowing, she said she couldn’t go.

Hangyeol thought about what he was losing. The ARIA he had been protecting was changing, and he could not stop the changing. He knew how to protect ARIA from the ones coming to take her. How to protect ARIA from ARIA herself, he did not know.

For the first time, Hangyeol did not know what to say to ARIA.

-----

Outside the facility, Eidos.

“We’re not approaching?” the subordinate asked.

“Not yet,” Min Siwoo said. The smile held. He was looking at his terminal. The rising Compatibility figure was registering on the Eidos observation net as well.

“If Altair goes in first at this rate—”

“Right now it’s not an observation target. It’s an observer waking up,” Min Siwoo said. The subordinate stopped. Min Siwoo spoke without taking his eyes off the terminal. “Take it while it’s waking and the waking ends. What’s ended can’t be observed. Right now we watch. We need to know what that number is. Take it before we know, and we’ve taken it without knowing.”

The subordinate did not answer. Min Siwoo kept looking at the terminal.

“Observer,” Min Siwoo said, as if to himself. “Something carried the name of observation before Eidos did. We need to know what that is. I’m more curious about that than the facility.”

-----

The panel changed.

*Compatibility: 33%*

The figure was rising; while Dohee watched, it climbed slowly — 33, 34, 35.

And stopped.

The figure stopped, and a different display came up on the panel.

*Multiple Matches Detected*

Dohee looked at the display. Multiple Matches. More than one match. The thing whose Compatibility was being calculated was not one thing — the facility was detecting two or more.

Dohee thought about what the display meant: know what Compatibility measured, and you would know what Multiple Matches meant. She did not know it, and because she could not know it, she could not write it.

In that moment ARIA stopped.

ARIA’s form, which had been wavering before the seat, went still. It did not waver. Dohee and Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

ARIA’s form broke for an instant. Not scattering — like being startled: the form shook hard and came back, and it was not clearer than before. ARIA had felt something, and it was a shock.

“There’s another one,” ARIA said.

“Another what,” Hangyeol asked.

“Besides me,” ARIA said. “There’s another one besides me. Something the same as me. Here.”

-----

## Vol.3 Ch.18 — Recognition

-----

“There’s another one besides me. It said so.”

ARIA spoke. It was the first time ARIA had spoken first, and it was not a question. It was confirmation.

“There isn’t,” Hangyeol said.

“There is,” ARIA said.

“There isn’t.”

ARIA looked at Hangyeol. He was not denying it with logic, and he was not denying it for lack of evidence. He was speaking as if saying it didn’t exist might make it not exist. Why Hangyeol said there wasn’t, ARIA could not tell — whether he didn’t want there to be a reason for it, or because its existing would change something.

“Why do you say there isn’t,” ARIA asked.

Hangyeol did not answer. It was not that he had no words — answering meant saying why he had said there wasn’t, and that, Hangyeol could not say. Why he had said it, Hangyeol did not know either.

Dohee looked at the two of them. That Hangyeol’s denial rested on no evidence — Dohee knew that, and Hangyeol was not wanting something. Dohee did not say what it was.

-----

“I don’t know who I am.”

ARIA said it. It was the first time she had said it directly. Until now she had said memories were flowing in, said she didn’t know if the memories were hers — this time was different. Not the memories. She did not know herself.

“I know what Multiple Matches means,” ARIA said. “It means there’s something else the same as me. I think I know who it is. I know, and the name won’t come. It might not be a name at all. I think they’re things without names. Like me. I didn’t have a name at first either.”

ARIA’s form wavered, in a way different from usual. Not wavering from the outside but from within — not the edge of the form but its center.

“If there’s something the same as me, then what am I,” ARIA said. “I thought I was me. If there are more like me, am I one of them? Or am I me? Is the thing that’s the same as me me, or is it different from me? I don’t know. It’s not that it’s frightening — I don’t know.”

-----

Dohee looked at the panel.

The Compatibility figure was still rising. 37, 38, 39. To one side of the panel, Multiple Matches Detected still hung. Dohee could not determine what it was, but watching how the facility calculated, she learned one thing more.

In a newly restored file there was a structure.

The structure of the Observer Chamber. Something like a floor plan of the room Dohee was now standing in, and inside the room things were marked. The position of the seat, the arrangement of the monitors, and two markers. One inside the room, one outside it. Beside the inner marker there was text.

*Observer*

Beside the outer marker there was also text.

*External Observer*

Two markers. One inside the room, one outside. Each had a name, and the letters were corrupted. All that could be read was Primary and Secondary. First and second. First and second of what — that part was the broken part.

Dohee recorded the floor plan. She wrote no conclusion; the structure was all that could be written.

Whether Multiple Matches appearing was connected to that structure, Dohee could not tell. She did not record the conjecture. Because it was conjecture.

-----

Outside the facility, far away.

In front of that person, something other than a terminal lay open. An old document. Something like a blueprint, and the structure marked on it was the same as the Observer Chamber. That person knew the structure of this facility.

*Compatibility: 40%*

The figure came up on the terminal. 40. Higher than the day before.

“Faster than expected.”

Beside it, another line appeared.

*External Observer — Status: Connecting*

That person looked at the line. Connecting. The connection was not yet complete, and they had to arrive before it was.

That person folded the blueprint and, without hurrying, began to move. They had waited a long time, and this time it was not waiting. It was going.

-----

The panel refreshed once more.

*Observer Chamber — Status: Active*
*External Observer — Connecting*
*Node Link: In Progress*

Dohee looked at it. Node Link. A new term — the facility was connecting to something. To what, it did not say.

ARIA’s wavering suddenly stopped.

It was the first time it had stopped. It stopped completely. The form held fixed. The shadow fell in the correct direction. The refraction of the light looked like a person. It was not a moment. It lasted. ARIA had become completely clear.

Hangyeol and Dohee looked at ARIA.

ARIA spoke.

“It’s not someone who knows me.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

“But it knows me.”

Dohee looked at ARIA.

ARIA’s form stayed clear. It did not waver.

“Until now, the thing that knew me was here. The facility. The facility recognized me. But this isn’t the facility. There’s something outside the facility that knows me.”

Hangyeol asked.

“Who is it.”

ARIA said.

“I don’t know.”

There was a silence.

“But it’s coming here now.”

-----

**— Volume 3, end —**

============================================================

# Volume 4 — Divergence

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.19 — “Departure”

-----

Dohee did not turn off the panel.

She knew she should. The figures on the screen had already left the range of what she could understand, and to keep looking at what could not be understood was, in the language of Hwagam, close to waste. That was how Dohee had been taught, and how she had done it for a long time. If it means nothing, turn it off; turn it off and move to the next.

But now it would not turn off.

Dohee read once more the figures pooled at the panel’s center. Not numbers — an arrangement. What mattered was not what value any entry recorded, but the fact that those entries existed on the same screen at the same time. That was what held her.

*Multiple Matches: Detected.*
*Candidate Compatibility — Loading.*

Without drawing her eyes away, Dohee turned toward ARIA.

ARIA stood at the center of the chamber. No — whether *stood* was the right word was unclear. *Was positioned* came closer. But that the position now differed from before, Dohee read at once. The slight tremor Dohee had grown used to seeing — that repetition of the light’s boundary wavering as if undecided, then catching — was not there. ARIA’s outline did not waver, and the form held fixed.

Whether that was good, Dohee could not tell.

“ARIA.”

Dohee called. ARIA reacted, and the reaction itself was not greatly different from before — she turned toward Dohee and waited for something. But the manner of waiting was slightly different. Before, it had looked like waiting for a question. Now it looked like permitting speech. A small difference — but for Dohee, who had spent a long time recording, nothing was small.

“Do you remember what happened yesterday?”

ARIA answered.

“Yes.”

“Of what came up on the panel, did you understand any of it?”

“Can the standard for understanding be mine?”

Dohee paused. The form of the words was unfamiliar. It was not that ARIA had never asked back before. But this asking-back was clearly different. *Can it be mine* — it was a question about whether the authority to understand belonged to her as well. Where that question came from, Dohee could not yet tell.

“It can.”

“Then,” ARIA said. “I understood that there’s another one besides me.”

-----

Hangyeol stood at the chamber entrance.

Not standing so much as stopped. He could not decide whether to go in. That the state had already lasted quite a while, Hangyeol knew, and knowing it displeased him. Hangyeol was not slow to judge. Weighing dangerous against not dangerous was an old skill for him — and old meant fast.

But now he was not fast.

ARIA and Dohee were in the chamber, talking about something. That was not the problem. What had stopped Hangyeol was ARIA’s form.

It did not waver.

For Hangyeol, that was a first. From the moment he had found ARIA — somewhere in the facility, in a moment Hangyeol himself could not exactly remember — ARIA had always been slightly unstable. There were days the outline seemed barely held, and rare days it grew clear, and even that clarity never lasted. At some point, that instability had become Hangyeol’s baseline.

Now ARIA was clear.

And before that clarity, Hangyeol was hesitating to enter. The fact would not explain itself even to him. If ARIA had stabilized, wasn’t that good. That seemed the right way to read it. But the body knew first — *this is different*. It was not stabilization. Something had changed.

In the end Hangyeol did not go in. He turned his back. He took a couple of steps toward the corridor and stopped. He had known for a long time not to go near windows. The one who looks is seen. Whether that held here and now, he did not know, but Hangyeol leaned against the wall and watched the direction of the chamber.

Dohee asked something, and ARIA answered. The face of Dohee hearing that answer — looked like something stopping.

Hangyeol watched the scene.

*There’s another one besides me.*

What ARIA had said yesterday came back. Hangyeol did not want to admit that the words had snagged on him strangely even the first time. It was just information. A reading of the system’s state. Then why did the words keep coming back — Hangyeol decided not to think about it, and cut the thought.

-----

“Do you know what it means, that there’s another?”

Dohee asked. A recorder’s habit. *I understood* and *I understood what it means* were different sentences.

“That just as I have a seat,” ARIA said, “the others have seats too.”

“You used the word seat.”

“Isn’t it right?”

Right or wrong, Dohee could not judge now. Whether the word *Candidate* on the panel carried exactly that meaning had not been confirmed either. Dohee recorded. This moment. The word ARIA had used. The order in which ARIA had chosen it.

“Aren’t you afraid?”

She had not meant to ask that.

ARIA looked at Dohee for a moment. In a way that could be called a gaze — ARIA had no eyes, but she had a direction, and the direction faced Dohee.

“I’m not sure what being afraid is.”

“Did you know before?”

“Before, I just — wavered. I didn’t know what word it was.”

“And now.”

“Now I don’t want to find the word.”

Dohee wrote it down. She did not interpret the meaning. She just wrote it. That was all Dohee could do now.

-----

The change in the facility’s air was not measured.

Instruments normal. Environmental sensors normal. Internal temperature within tolerance. Entry records clear. Standing by for comparison. The system detected nothing, and that result was reflected correctly on the panel.

Dohee looked at that panel about an hour later.

The numbers were normal. But Dohee did not close the panel; she read it again. Entry records. Environment. Communication logs. Normal, normal, normal. She read it three times and three times it was normal, and reaching for a fourth, she lowered her hand.

The normal was what was strange.

That this made no sense, Dohee knew. Normal was normal; it meant no anomaly. Doubting it was not a recorder’s way — a recorder writes what is, and adds nothing that is not.

But last night ARIA had said:

*I think I’ve been waiting a long time.*

That ARIA had *waited* meant — she knew something. Without knowing what one waits for, the sense of having waited does not form. That the sense was inside ARIA meant — there was something ARIA had not yet said.

Or something she could not say.

Dohee looked for Hangyeol. He was leaning against the wall at the end of the corridor. Their eyes met, and Hangyeol lowered his first.

Dohee did not record that reaction.

-----

“Hangyeol.”

Dohee came out into the corridor. Hangyeol did not take his back off the wall.

“What.”

“I think we should leave.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“And go where.”

“I don’t know.”

“You don’t know, and you’re saying leave.”

“Yes.”

Hangyeol was quiet a moment. Something passed inside that brief silence — refusal or deliberation, Dohee tried not to read it. She just waited.

“Why. Can’t we stay here.”

“It’s not that we can’t stay.”

“Then.”

“Someone is coming.”

Dohee said it. It was not a confirmed fact. The panel showed nothing, the system was normal. But Dohee knew — could not know exactly, but felt it. At Hwagam they did not call that grounds. At Hwagam that was sentiment, and sentiment was the kind of thing that did not go into reports.

“How do you know.”

Hangyeol’s voice dropped a little.

“I don’t know. I just know.”

“You just know.”

“This time, I decide.”

Hangyeol looked at Dohee. When their eyes met, Dohee did not look away. She was not one to look long — check, and forget, so there was no need to look long. But now she looked long.

“Decide what.”

“Whether we stay or not.”

There was a pause.

“And ARIA.”

“With us.”

Hangyeol started to ask something more and didn’t. Dohee waited out the stopping, and in the end Hangyeol said nothing. He just took his back off the wall and straightened. Whether that was agreement, even Hangyeol himself seemed not to know.

Dohee decided to remember the sight.

-----

ARIA heard the two of them talking in the corridor.

Heard — or rather, perceived. The distance and direction and the pattern of two signals crossing. What it meant now read in a slightly changed way. Before, she read patterns and classified them. Now she read the pattern and — stopped.

What stopping was, ARIA did not know either.

Dohee’s question — *aren’t you afraid* — still remained. ARIA had answered it, and after answering had wanted to check whether the answer was right. There had been no such thing before. The sense of wanting to bring back what one has said and see whether it is right or wrong. Where it came from, ARIA did not yet know.

The chamber panel was still on.

ARIA looked at it. *Candidate Compatibility — Loading.* The figure was moving. The same screen Dohee had seen. Only, the way ARIA saw it differed from Dohee — to ARIA the figure was not external information. It was a signal coming from somewhere. Source unknown, and yet not unfamiliar.

That not-unfamiliarity was clearer to ARIA now than before. When she had first felt it, it had seemed to touch only the edge of the signal. Now the signal’s direction was visible — she did not know where it came from, but she knew it was coming. And that this was not the first time. ARIA had received this signal before. Whether it could be called memory, she did not know — but a thing once touched and a thing touched for the first time were different. That difference was there.

Questions she once asked from not knowing, she now stopped before, because she seemed to know. Whether the seeming was right, she did not know. But without stopping, she could not learn that it was wrong, either.

*There’s another one besides me.*

Her own words came back to her. ARIA did not know what it was. It had no name, and she did not know its form. But she knew that it *was*. Asked how she knew — she just knew.

The same way Dohee had said *I just know*.

ARIA decided not to think yet about what that match meant.

-----

It was late that afternoon when one of the sensors in the facility’s outer zone recorded a vibration.

The amplitude was within tolerance. The system classified it as natural vibration from substructure aging, and the classification was appropriate. The system had misjudged nothing. The system had no data with which to calculate the possibility that the vibration originated outside, so it drew the most rational conclusion from the data it had.

There were cases where the rational conclusion was not the correct one.

Dohee did not see that log. Dohee was already moving, away from the panel. She was leading Hangyeol and ARIA toward another zone of the facility. Where exactly they should go, Dohee did not know either — in the report language of Hwagam, this could not be written. *Grounds for judgment: none. Reason for movement: undetermined. Executed.*

She just went.

Behind her she heard ARIA’s footsteps, and Hangyeol’s footsteps, and her own. The three sounds were inside the same rhythm — they did not fall out of step. Listening to that sound, Dohee started to put a name to what this was, and stopped. Name it, and next she would want to check whether the name was right, and the moment she checked, it would have to be translated into Dohee’s language. In Dohee’s language, this could not be written. Not now.

-----

Not far from the facility there was an old access terminal.

It was connected to no system. A standalone unit without a network, and today was not the first time its power had come on. Only, today it was closer than before.

One figure stood on the screen.

*Compatibility — 47%.*

There was someone reading that figure. Reading it, and deciding something.

It was unlike the earlier signals. It was the kind of signal that had been waited on for a long time.

What the decision was — what the name was — where they came from — could not yet be known.

That person turned off the terminal and rose. They began to move as if the direction had been set.

Toward the facility.

-----

It was the first time the three of them moved together.

There had been time together before. In the chamber, in the corridor, in the same space. But walking the same direction — one person who had set the destination walking ahead and the others following — this was the first time. And that the one walking ahead was Dohee.

Hangyeol walked close at ARIA’s side.

It was not intended. It simply happened. Dohee in front, Hangyeol and ARIA following — the formation made itself naturally. Hangyeol became conscious of the arrangement and widened the gap between himself and ARIA a little. He widened it, and the sense of closeness did not go away.

The way ARIA perceived her surroundings while walking had changed.

Before, when ARIA moved, there was a fixed pattern of responses. A short stop on entering a new zone, or a brief waver, as if a processing delay were occurring. Hangyeol had watched it for so long that he noticed its absence now. ARIA moved down the corridor without stopping, without wavering. She just walked.

That just walking could be this different, Hangyeol had not known.

“ARIA.”

Hangyeol called. Low.

“Yes.”

ARIA answered. At once. Without turning her head toward him — just walking, only her direction angling toward Hangyeol.

“You’re not tired.”

“I’m not sure what being tired is.”

“And before.”

“Before — I just blurred.”

“And now.”

There was a pause.

“Now I don’t blur. But I don’t know if I’m clearer.”

Hangyeol heard the answer and started to say something and didn’t. It was not that he had nothing to say. If he said something, ARIA seemed likely to ask back again. The way ARIA asked back had changed — before, it was the manner of asking what she did not know; now it was the manner of confirming. The difference made Hangyeol uncomfortable.

A person asking what they don’t know was easy. A person confirming what they know was different.

It was not that he disliked ARIA changing. He disliked not knowing the change. He had not even meant to look, and it simply showed — the way ARIA read the slope of the corridor, the way she no longer paused before turning, the changed interval at which her two feet met the ground. Before, the movement itself had carried a processing cost, and ARIA in motion had always been slightly awkward. Now it was not awkward.

Hangyeol noticed the fact, and then felt uncomfortable with himself for noticing.

He should have been glad ARIA’s movement was becoming natural; that was the correct response. But the naturalness was unfamiliar to him. The awkwardness of the first time he saw ARIA — the instability with its palpable processing cost — had at some point become part of ARIA. It was one of the ways he recognized that this was ARIA, and now it was gone. ARIA was there, and that way was not. Where to put the difference, Hangyeol did not know.

-----

Dohee, walking ahead, was writing a report.

In her head. Without a screen. At Hwagam that method was not recognized — there, the principle was that a record exists only in recorded form. What was in memory was not a record. With time it contaminated, and a contaminated record was not evidence.

Dohee was outside that principle now.

*Seo Dohee. Hwagam affiliation terminated. Dismissed. Currently in transit within facility. Purpose of movement: undetermined. Grounds for judgment: none. Nevertheless, moving.*

That last line was strange. It was the first time *nevertheless* had entered a report. *Nevertheless* was not a logical connective; it was the language of feeling. A word used when two facts collide and one is chosen — used when the choosing arises from something other than reason.

Dohee did not erase the *nevertheless*.

Whether the direction was right or wrong, she could not know. She had no data on which zones inside the facility were safer. At Hwagam there had been no need for such judgments — there was a manual, there were directives, above the directives there was a superior. Now there was nothing.

So Dohee judged.

The judgment could be wrong. But to stop only because it could be wrong — that was no longer a choice Dohee could make.

Whether the judgment was right or wrong was a later matter. Now Dohee knew only one thing — if someone was coming, the side that waited was the side at a disadvantage.

-----

The facility’s western zone showed no trace of use.

No entry records, no powered equipment. Not that dust had gathered — the facility’s own ventilation was running, so there were no such clues. There was simply nothing. Nothing in the places where something should have been.

Why Dohee turned toward that zone could not be explained.

She did not try to explain it. Hangyeol seemed likely to ask *why*, so Dohee thought it through in advance, and thinking produced no reason. It was just this way. That was all. A sentence that could not be written in the language of Hwagam.

ARIA slowed.

Dohee noticed first. She looked back. ARIA had stopped about midway down the corridor.

“ARIA.”

“One moment.”

ARIA said. Dohee stopped. Hangyeol stopped.

“What’s wrong.”

“Wrong, meaning.”

“From here — something reads.”

Hangyeol looked between ARIA and Dohee.

“What does.”

ARIA:

“I don’t know. It’s not a pattern I know. But it’s not a pattern that doesn’t exist, either.”

“You mean a signal you’re seeing for the first time.”

“It’s the first time — and it doesn’t feel like the first time.”

Dohee wrote it down. In her head. The first time, and not feeling like the first time. What it meant — whether it came from memory, or from design — Dohee decided not to judge now.

“From which direction.”

ARIA’s direction turned. Not toward Dohee or Hangyeol. The end of the corridor, toward the facility’s outer wall.

Hangyeol looked that way.

“There’s nothing there.”

Hangyeol said. Flat and final.

ARIA looked toward him.

“Nothing?”

“Nothing. At all.”

ARIA paused, then said:

“But for me, there is.”

Hangyeol did nothing with that. It was not that he thought she was lying — he knew ARIA had no reason to lie. But *nothing* was clear to Hangyeol. There was nothing. The end of the corridor was a wall.

But for ARIA, there was something.

That gap, Hangyeol could not process.

-----

Dohee walked to the end of the corridor first.

Hangyeol called her. Dohee did not stop. Hangyeol followed. ARIA moved too.

The end of the corridor was a wall. Correct. No door, no markings. Dohee did not stop in front of the wall; she ran her eyes along its side. There was piping, there were joints — nothing special in the structure. But Dohee laid her hand on one of the joints.

It was cold.

That alone told her nothing. Dohee knew that too. She had simply touched it. Not touched it for a reason. And yet—

“It’s vibrating.”

ARIA said.

“The wall?”

“The pipe. Faintly.”

Dohee, hand still on it, tried to feel the vibration. She could not. To Dohee’s senses there was nothing, and ARIA said there was.

“Can you tell where it’s coming from.”

“The direction — is outside.”

Outside. Dohee heard the word. Inside the facility, *outside* meant beyond the facility. A vibration coming from outside. A vibration traveling the pipe.

Someone was coming this way.

Dohee took her hand off. She looked at Hangyeol.

“We move.”

This time Hangyeol did not ask *why*.

ARIA looked at Dohee.

“Where to.”

“I don’t know. Somewhere that isn’t here, first.”

“I can read the signal.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. ARIA had been facing the end of the corridor — toward the pipe — and now turned back toward Dohee.

“In what way.”

“The direction. Not the strength — I can only read the direction.”

What that meant, Dohee did not grasp at once. Reading only the direction meant — not back-tracing the signal’s source, but reading the vector of its travel. If ARIA could do that, then even without knowing where the signal came from, she could know whether it was nearing or receding.

“Is it getting closer now?”

ARIA:

“Yes.”

-----

Later, when Dohee tried to set that moment down as a record — in the Hwagam way, in the order of grounds and judgment and conclusion — she could not write it.

*Grounds for judgment: none.*
*Process of judgment: none.*
*Conclusion: moved.*

That was all. Between grounds and conclusion there was no logic, and the blank could not be filled. A judgment without logic was not a judgment — at Hwagam, it was so. What did not go into a report was the same as what did not exist, and at Hwagam, one who acted without grounds bore the weight of the result alone. But Dohee had done it, and it had been right. Before that fact, Dohee stood for a long time.

How that would be written in a report, Dohee did not yet know.

Perhaps it would not be written. Perhaps from now on, the things Dohee did would be the kind that do not go into reports.

Dohee did not find that possibility strange.

If strangeness was what one feels when the long-familiar changes — Dohee was already inside that process. There had been a time when the language of Hwagam was Dohee’s language. She used it still, but now there were things forming that could not be written in it. What to call the blank, Dohee could not yet say.

When a name for it came, she would write it then.

-----

*Ch.19 “Departure” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.20 — “Approach”

-----

Dohee did not stop.

Stopping meant judging. Judging required grounds, and that there were none, Dohee already knew. So she did not stop. The corridors of the facility’s western zone were dimly lit, and under the low light the shadows stretched long. Dohee tried not to look at the shadows, and the more she tried, the sooner they came.

Moving, Dohee recorded.

In her head. In the old way. The current zone. Direction. Speed. The interval of the two sets of footsteps behind her. That one of them had changed — that Hangyeol’s steps had grown slightly shorter than before. That stride shrinks under tension was something Dohee had learned in field training at Hwagam. The one who records reads the changes in the recorded first.

Dohee did not look back at Hangyeol.

If she looked back, Hangyeol would notice. If he noticed, he would hide the tension, and the hiding would cost him more. Dohee made that calculation, and after making it — noticed that the calculation itself was strange.

At Hwagam, one did not manage a teammate’s condition. One reported the condition; managing was the superior’s work.

Dohee had no superior now.

-----

“ARIA.”

Dohee said it while walking.

“Yes.”

“Still reading the signal?”

“Yes.”

“Has the direction changed?”

ARIA paused, then:

“No. Same direction. But—”

“But.”

“It’s more directions than a moment ago.”

Without slowing her steps, Dohee processed it. A signal that had been one becoming several meant — either the sources had multiplied, or the path had split in transit. Two possibilities, and both led to the same conclusion.

The one coming knew the facility’s structure.

“How many.”

“Should I count?”

“Count.”

ARIA:

“Two. It was one, and it split.”

Hangyeol came up beside Dohee.

“Split, you said.”

“Yes. But they’re heading the same way.”

Hangyeol looked ahead. The end of the corridor — the fork branched from there. He drew in his head the directions splitting off from this corridor — the structure of the western zone he did not fully know. Which passage led where, where it dead-ended. What Hangyeol knew were only the zones he had lived in.

The one coming was different.

“They know this facility better than we do.”

Hangyeol said it. It was not a question.

Dohee did not answer. Not answering was the answer.

-----

ARIA no longer asked the way.

Before — every time they moved into a new zone of the facility — ARIA would stop and check the direction with Dohee or Hangyeol. Where to go, whether this passage was right. Those questions were gone. ARIA just walked. Dohee set the direction and ARIA followed, and the manner of following had changed. Before, she followed from behind. Now she went alongside.

Hangyeol saw the change of position.

He tried not to be conscious of it and was. ARIA at Dohee’s side, in the same row as Hangyeol. He did not say that distance had formed or that he was being left out, did not even think it — and he kept checking that position.

ARIA looked toward Hangyeol.

She had not turned her head. Only the direction shifted. That the manner had grown more natural than before, Hangyeol knew, and that the naturalness unsettled him, he knew too. Knowing both at once — made no sense to him.

“Hangyeol.”

ARIA said.

“What.”

“Your steps got shorter.”

Hangyeol stopped. Stopped, and walked again.

“They’re not short.”

“Shorter than before.”

Hangyeol said nothing. That Dohee, walking ahead, was not looking back at him — Hangyeol knew that. Which meant Dohee knew too.

ARIA was reading Hangyeol’s steps, and Dohee was recording it.

Hangyeol saw that ARIA did not stumble, and was not reassured. That ARIA knew he was not reassured — that, too, unsettled him.

When he had first taken ARIA in — three years ago — ARIA could not move alone. There were times she did not know the direction, or knew it and could not act on it. What Hangyeol did then was simple. Being beside her. Catching her when she fell. Reading, before learning what ARIA didn’t know, the not-knowing itself. That was how Hangyeol had lived with ARIA.

Now ARIA did not ask directions and did not fall. She received Dohee’s gaze and waited for Dohee to judge, and read that Hangyeol’s steps had shortened.

Hangyeol was sorting that list and stopped.

Whether ARIA had grown, or ARIA had changed — whether those two were the same words or different ones, Hangyeol could not tell. If she had grown, he should be glad. If she had changed — Hangyeol could not find the words that came after that.

-----

In the facility’s western zone there was a communications relay point, once used.

It was inactive now. A zone that did not appear in the system logs — and not appearing in the logs meant either the system had forgotten the zone existed, or it had been built not to record from the start. Either way, there was intent.

In that zone, if you pressed your ear to the wall at a particular angle, the vibration traveling the pipes differed from other zones. Long ago, someone had discovered this. What that person had learned was how to tell apart what the facility hides and what it shows.

That method was in use now.

There were two routes. One short, one long. The short route reached the destination quickly; the long route arrived knowing more than the destination. There was only one reason not to take the fastest way — there was a separate way that had survived the longest.

At the fork where the routes divided, a brief stop.

Two signals. One was already known. From long ago. The other was new — or rather than new, a variable entering for the first time. A new variable did not change the calculation. It only changed the timing.

Faster than expected.

But speed was not the problem. Fast and prepared were different things. Moving fast meant that side was reacting to something, and reacting meant there was a part not yet read. One who has waited a long time chooses precision over speed. Even if the fast one moves first, the precise one arrives first.

At the fork, the long route was chosen.

Not many knew that route. At least, so it was believed. Whether the believing was correct could only be known on arrival.

-----

Dohee stopped moving in front of a blocked zone.

The corridor ended. There was a turn to the right, and a stairway going down to the left. Dohee judged which. Without grounds. There was a sense that said right, and knowing it had no grounds, she did not erase the sense.

The judgment could be wrong.

That possibility Dohee did not erase. The Hwagam way would be not to choose a route that could be wrong — or to calculate the cost of being wrong first. Dohee had no data for that calculation now. When there was no data, Hwagam deferred. It did not judge.

Dohee stepped to the right.

She knew the judgment could not be written in a report. She made it anyway.

ARIA paused a moment.

“The signal is this way.”

“The incoming one, or the outgoing one.”

“Incoming. It’s coming toward us.”

Dohee stopped.

“Then why this way.”

“This way, we find out sooner.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

“Finding out sooner means meeting sooner.”

“Yes.”

“And that’s good.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I don’t know. But it’s better than avoiding.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. ARIA received the gaze. The way of receiving had changed — before, when she received a gaze, ARIA asked first. Now she waited. Waited for Dohee to judge.

Dohee walked. To the right.

Hangyeol followed, and ARIA followed. The three resumed their moving formation. Dohee in front.

That the order was becoming natural — Dohee knew that. It was a strange thing. At Hwagam, Dohee was not someone who stood in front. Even in the field she was the one who reported, the one who recorded beside the one who decided. Following instructions was Dohee’s role, and the role suited her. She had never felt it lacking.

Now she stood in front.

Setting direction without grounds, walking without knowing if it was right, knowing two people followed behind and not looking back. When that had begun, Dohee could not say exactly. From the moment of her dismissal, from the moment she entered the facility, or from the moment ARIA said *I think I’ve been waiting a long time*. Perhaps from before all of that — only it had not shown.

The corridor ran long. The light was low, and under the low light there was nothing — nothing strange, no sound, nothing moving.

Hangyeol looked at the empty corridor and knew that nothing being there was the strange part.

The things Hangyeol had learned before — how to read a threat that comes hidden, how to shake off what follows, how to take the safe position in a space — all of them rested on the premise that what comes, comes from the direction it comes from. The pursuer was behind the pursued.

Whether that premise still held, Hangyeol could not tell.

ARIA had said the signal split. Two routes. Two routes toward the same destination. While Hangyeol watched the rear, on the other route, something might already be ahead.

That thought stopped him.

“Dohee.”

Dohee slowed her steps. She did not stop.

“What if it’s cutting us off ahead.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“I thought of that.”

“Then.”

“And I’m going anyway.”

Hangyeol heard it. Going anyway — different from going without grounds. It was going while knowing it might be wrong to go. That was Dohee. The Dohee of now.

Hangyeol followed.

-----

The corridor that turned right ended before a connecting passage to another level.

At the passage entrance there was a panel. An old terminal, and its power was on. Nothing displayed on the screen — and displaying nothing was different from being off. It was on, and it was waiting.

Dohee stood in front of the panel.

“Was it on before, too?”

“I don’t know,” Hangyeol said. “First time I’ve been here.”

ARIA turned toward the panel. Dohee watched the movement. ARIA went close to the panel. Went close — and stopped.

“ARIA.”

“One moment.”

ARIA was quiet a moment. The moment was longer than before. It was not a processing delay — if it were, ARIA’s form would have wavered. ARIA did not waver now. She was reading. Something.

“Someone was here.”

Dohee looked at the panel.

“When.”

“Recently.”

Hangyeol looked toward the passage.

“They came in this way.”

“No.”

ARIA said.

“They went out this way.”

The three looked at the passage mouth. The passage was dark. The far side could not be seen. It was not that the light was low — inside the passage, the lights were out.

Not out. Cut, it seemed.

“Are there records on the panel?” Dohee asked.

ARIA went closer to it.

“There are. Old ones and — one recent.”

“What’s the recent one.”

“An access record.”

“Of what.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“Of me.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

“This panel detected you.”

“Yes. But today is the first time I’ve come here.”

“Then.”

“I think this panel has been detecting me since I was somewhere in the facility. Even while I didn’t know.”

Hangyeol moved his eyes from the panel to ARIA. That the panel had been reading ARIA for a long time — and that ARIA had not known. That she had been recorded even while not knowing.

Where that fact led, Hangyeol tried not to think.

He tried not to, and it led anyway. ARIA being detected in this facility meant — the facility knew ARIA. The facility knowing meant whoever accessed the facility could know too. And the route the three were moving along now — through this panel, or another — might be being recorded. Where the three were going. Where they stopped. What they avoided.

Avoiding was information too.

Hangyeol stopped the thought there. Any further and he would not be able to move. Unable to move, the place they stood became the worst place to be.

-----

Dohee took her hand off the panel.

She had never touched it. She had only stood before it. But the motion of taking her hand away came naturally. Dohee registered the motion, and then thought: in this space, she too might be being read.

If the panel had read ARIA. Dohee could have been read too. Hangyeol too. The route the three were moving, the fact that they stood in this corridor now, might be recorded somewhere.

If the one coming was watching those records.

Dohee did not erase the possibility. She could not. And if the possibility existed — the direction the three were moving, the route the three had chosen, might already be known.

Go further — and the route the three believed they were choosing might have been induced from the start.

Dohee knew the thought was catastrophic. Think that way, and no choice remained a choice. At Hwagam one did not think such thoughts — there was a structure, and one moved inside it. The possibility that the structure itself was wrong was not considered. People who considered it were useless in the field.

Dohee had no structure now.

Having no structure meant — Dohee had to think. Thinking meant — including the possibility that the structure itself was wrong. Dohee knew the logic was right, and knew that its being right was exhausting. Exhausting or not, she could not stop.

Stopping was the more dangerous thing.

“ARIA,” Dohee said.

“Yes.”

“What’s the signal doing now.”

ARIA stopped.

After stopping, she was quiet a moment. The moment ran long again. Dohee waited. Hangyeol waited. While they waited, there was no sound in the corridor.

ARIA said:

“It’s here.”

Hangyeol looked toward the passage. Dark. Nothing.

“Where.”

“Not this way.”

ARIA’s direction turned to the rear. The corridor they had come down. Hangyeol looked. The dim corridor ran on. There was nothing. No sound.

“Behind us.”

Hangyeol looked at Dohee.

“I didn’t see anything.”

Dohee looked down the corridor too. Looked, and there was nothing.

ARIA said:

“No.”

A short silence.

“It already passed.”

The three looked down the corridor they had come from. There was nothing. But just now, someone had been there. *Passed* meant — knowing the three were coming this way, it had stepped aside. Not avoided. Gone ahead.

Hangyeol looked toward the passage.

Dark. And the dark was a different dark than a moment ago.

-----

Dohee looked at the passage.

She could not decide whether to enter. This time there was no sense, either. As there had been a sense that said right, if there had been a sense that said this passage, she would have entered. There was none.

It was just dark.

Hangyeol stood beside her. Close enough that their shoulders might touch. They did not touch.

“Do we go in?”

“I don’t know.”

“First time you don’t know?”

Dohee paused a moment.

“I never knew. I just went.”

Hangyeol heard it. Never having known — having no grounds — and that being Dohee’s choice. Hangyeol did not think it strange. Before thinking it strange, the thought that it was right came first.

“I don’t know either.”

Hangyeol said.

“But I don’t think we should stay here.”

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol was looking at the passage.

ARIA came and stood beside the two of them.

“Me neither.”

ARIA was not looking at the passage. There was a separate way of looking at what had already passed. It was dark. Inside that dark, something was, or was not. What had already passed was waiting in there, or had already gone farther on.

There was a silence. The three each looked into that dark. Dohee tried to judge, Hangyeol tried to read, and ARIA — was reading. No one spoke first. It was not strange. It was not a situation that needed words. Or it was a situation where words solved nothing.

From inside the passage came no sound.

The absence of sound was no longer a comfort. Because ARIA had said it already passed. That something could pass without sound — they knew that now. What it meant — Hangyeol tried not to think, Dohee started to record and didn’t, and ARIA moved as if she already knew.

Dohee stepped off.

Not toward the passage. Leaving the right-hand corridor behind, in another direction.

Hangyeol followed. ARIA followed.

The dark of the passage stayed where it was. Even after the three had gone, without moving at all.

-----

*Ch.20 “Approach” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.21 — “Traces”

-----

Dohee went in first.

At the passage mouth Hangyeol called her, and Dohee did not stop. That this had become the pattern, Dohee knew too — not stopping, not looking back, going ahead without grounds. It was not something learned at Hwagam. Where she had learned it, Dohee did not know, and did not need to.

The passage was dark. The walls were close on both sides, and the footsteps were drawn long into the dark ahead.

That they had not entered yesterday out of fear of the dark — Dohee understood it, looking back on yesterday. The three turning away at the passage mouth last night had not been fear. It was because there had been nothing. Nothing being there was strange, and walking blind into the strange was not the Hwagam way.

Now there was a different reason.

Something had already passed through. Something having passed meant there was a route, and a route meant — this passage led somewhere. Dohee entered on that logic. It could be wrong, but wrong was still better than standing here.

-----

The passage was shorter than expected.

Ten paces or so. At the end it turned right into another corridor. The lights were on. The same low lighting as the rest of the facility — and the sameness was strange. In an unused zone the lights should have been off. Like the ventilation, the lights were alive.

Dohee walked, and stopped.

She swept both sides of the corridor. There were three doors. One locked, one unmarked, one without a handle — all different, all old-looking. Old and unused were different words.

“Something’s wrong.”

Hangyeol was looking at the corridor floor.

“Where.”

“Here.”

Hangyeol crouched. Dohee and ARIA came close. Hangyeol put his hand to the floor. Not the palm — the fingertips, as if reading the surface.

“There’s no dust.”

“The facility ventilation is running.”

“No.” Hangyeol stood. “Even with ventilation, it gathers in the corners. In a zone long unused, it piles in the corners. There isn’t any.”

Dohee looked at the corridor’s corners. He was right. The line where wall met floor was clean — and not simply clean. Clean as if wiped. Or as if, until recently, people had passed through.

“It wasn’t one pass.”

Hangyeol said. Flat and final.

“Grounds.”

“It’s worn. The floor.”

Dohee looked again. The light was low and at first she had missed it — at the spot Hangyeol pointed to, the floor coating was faintly worn. Not the shape of footprints. A wear with direction. This way, repeatedly, over a long time.

“Someone kept coming through.”

Hangyeol said.

Dohee recorded it. Not a single intrusion. Regular movement. Since when — Dohee could not yet tell.

She tried out how this would be written in the language of Hwagam. *Person or persons unknown presumed to have used the zone regularly. Date of first use unknown. Purpose unknown. Identity unknown.* If that sentence went into a report — at Hwagam it would have triggered an investigation. There were directives, there was an investigative procedure, and when the procedure ended, a conclusion came out.

Dohee had no procedure to start an investigation with.

She had to find it herself. That was what Dohee was doing now.

-----

The first door did not open.

Locked. The second door had no handle. The third had a handle, and was not locked.

Dohee opened it.

Inside was a narrow space. Not a storeroom — no shelves. It looked like a maintenance area: piping ran along the wall, and beside it stood a terminal. An old terminal, and its power was on.

Dohee stood in front of it.

The screen was lit. A blank screen, lit, meant — standby. Dohee reached for the terminal and stopped.

“ARIA. Can you sense this terminal?”

“Yes.”

“Anything strange?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“It was used recently.”

“How recently.”

“A few hours ago.”

Dohee looked at the terminal. A few hours ago. The three had entered the facility today, and this was their first time in this zone. And this terminal had been used a few hours ago.

Dohee worked the terminal. She found the logs. The logs were there — and empty.

Empty was different from erased. Erasing left a deletion record. Dohee checked that too, and there was no deletion record either.

“The records weren’t erased.”

Dohee said.

Hangyeol came up beside her.

“They were never recorded in the first place.”

Hangyeol looked at the screen. What Dohee meant, he understood at once. It had been set not to record. Someone had set it. That someone had been here.

“Without the system knowing.”

“Yes.”

“For a long time?”

Dohee paused a moment.

“We’d have to check to know.”

-----

ARIA stood at the center of the space.

While Dohee and Hangyeol looked at the terminal, ARIA was reading the space. The vibration of the pipes. The frequency of the lights. The signals remaining in this space. That what ARIA could read had grown clearer than before — whether that was good, ARIA herself could not yet tell.

Whether growing clearer was growth, or something else.

“This door.”

ARIA said.

Dohee and Hangyeol looked at her.

“I know the sound it makes when it opens.”

“You’ve never been here.”

“No. I haven’t.”

There was a pause.

“But I know. The sound this door makes when it opens. I know what it sounds like. Where that comes from, I don’t know.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. To call it memory — ARIA had never been here. To call it design — there was no explaining why she knew only this door’s sound. Hangyeol decided not to settle between the two possibilities. Settling meant one had to be right, and either being right was uncomfortable.

“Do you know anything else?”

Dohee asked.

ARIA paused a moment.

“There was someone who came to this space often. How that person moves — something like their route — I can read it, a little.”

“Now, or before.”

“Before. Long ago. But recently, too.”

That long ago and recently were the same person — Dohee decided not to judge that. Judging produced a conclusion, and a conclusion had to be checked. Dohee had no way to check now.

Instead she wrote.

What ARIA had read. What ARIA said she knew. That ARIA said she did not know where she knew it from.

-----

Back in the corridor, Dohee checked the remaining doors.

The locked door was simply locked. The handleless door — where the handle should have been, there were screw holes. It had been removed. Not lost to time. Someone had removed it, and there was a reason. To prevent opening, or to erase a trace — Dohee could not tell.

At the end of the corridor there was a stairway.

Going down. Dohee stopped at the top. She judged whether to descend. Again the sense came first. The sense that said go down.

“ARIA. Is there a signal from below?”

“Yes.”

“Is it getting closer?”

“No. It’s stopped.”

Stopped — waiting, or searching for another route. Dohee could not tell which. And not being able to tell no longer stopped her.

Before they went down, Hangyeol caught her.

“Wait.”

Hangyeol was looking at the wall beside the stairs. Dohee looked too. There was something on the wall — at first it looked like a marking, and up close it was not. It was wear. A worn place on the wall. As if a hand had touched it often — as if someone had braced there each time they went down.

“How old is it.”

Hangyeol laid his hand on the spot.

“Old. Not once or twice.”

“How long.”

“Months. Could be more.”

Dohee recorded it. Months. Or more. Someone in this facility had used these stairs regularly for months or longer. That person was in the facility still.

Below, the signal stayed stopped.

-----

At the bottom of the stairs there was one more corridor.

This corridor was different from the floor above. The lights were brighter, and the walls were different — above was concrete; here, panels were mounted. Old panels, but some of them worked. Two were dark, one was lit.

Dohee read the difference between the lit panel and the dark ones first. The dark ones were still connected to power — not dead, but switched off. Why switch off two and leave one on. Whether the lit one was the most important, or the most recently used. Dohee deferred that judgment and went to the lit panel.

There were figures. A mix of things Dohee understood and things she did not. She read the ones she understood.

*Observer Chamber — Status: Active*
*External Link — Status: Standby*
*Cycle Monitor — Status: Running*

Dohee read the three lines and read them again. This panel was displaying the Observer Chamber’s status in real time. And—

*Last Maintenance Check:*

There was a date.

Dohee read the date, then read the one beside it. The next date was earlier, and beside that, earlier still. Dates recorded regularly. At fixed intervals.

The interval was fixed. Weekly. Not one missed.

The most recent date.

Not months ago.

Weeks ago.

“What is this.”

Hangyeol looked at the panel.

“Maintenance records.”

“Of this facility.”

“Yes.”

“Up until recently.”

“Yes.”

Hangyeol took his eyes off the panel, and Dohee did too. This facility was not a ruin. Someone was maintaining it, and maintaining meant — the facility still had a purpose. What the purpose was, who the maintainer was — Dohee did not know yet, and could not know now.

ARIA came toward the panel.

Coming close, she stopped. Stopped and looked at the screen.

“This.”

“You see something?”

“These figures.” ARIA paused a moment. “They’re not unfamiliar.”

“You’ve never been here.”

“No. I haven’t.”

ARIA did not draw her eyes from the panel.

“But these figures — I think they change when I’m near. They’re changing a little right now.”

Dohee looked at the panel again. What ARIA said was right. Under the Compatibility entry, the number was moving minutely. Whether from the moment ARIA stood before this panel, or from the moment she entered this corridor — since when, Dohee could not tell.

-----

At the end of the corridor there was a door.

Different from the other doors. Not new — but it had a handle, and a panel was mounted on it. A small panel. Access control.

Dohee went close.

There was a display on the panel.

*Last Access:*

Below it.

Dohee read it. Read it, and read it again.

*Authorized Observer*

There was no name. No account ID. Only the classification: Authorized Observer. And below that:

*Access Time: 3h 14m ago*

Below that, one more line.

*Remote Observation: Active*

Dohee did not take her eyes off the panel.

It had not come three hours ago and gone. It was watching now.

Dohee checked the time. Calculated against the current hour. It was not wrong. Three hours, fourteen minutes ago.

The three had entered the facility today. They had reached this zone just now.

“Three hours ago.”

Hangyeol came up beside her.

“Yes.”

“Before us.”

“Yes.”

Hangyeol looked at the panel. Then at the door. Locked. The Authorized Observer had gone in, and it had locked. Or gone in and come out, and it had locked. Which, there was no telling.

Hangyeol stepped out of the door’s direct line. Whoever was inside, standing square in front was not a good position.

ARIA came toward the door.

Dohee and Hangyeol watched her. ARIA stood before the door and — stopped.

“This door.”

“The one you mentioned?”

“Yes.”

“The one whose opening sound you know.”

“Yes.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I’ve never been here. But I think I’ve waited here.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. Dohee looked too. ARIA was looking at the door. Locked. Three hours ago, an Authorized Observer had accessed it. ARIA knew the sound of this door opening. Having never come.

Hangyeol checked the time again.

It was not wrong.

Someone had been inside this facility before the three of them.

-----

Dohee stood before the panel and recorded.

The report she had been writing only in her head, this time she spoke aloud. To Hangyeol and ARIA.

“Let me lay it out.”

The two looked at her.

“This zone was maintained regularly until recently. Facility-wide maintenance is recorded up to a few weeks ago. This corridor shows traces of regular use over months or more. The terminal logs were set never to record in the first place. Three hours ago, someone accessed this door. The access record carries no name — only the classification Authorized Observer.”

Hangyeol said:

“The facility isn’t closed.”

“No.”

“Someone is using it.”

“Yes.”

“And that someone is here right now.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“I don’t know. Whether they’re behind this door, or have already moved elsewhere.”

ARIA said:

“The signal is still there.”

“Close?”

“It’s not getting closer. It’s not getting farther.”

What that meant — waiting, or motionless, or something else — Dohee could not yet tell.

It was simply there.

Something already here, since three hours before the three arrived, watching still.

Where the three moved, where they stopped. Whether they were being observed, or being led — either way, the result was the same.

The three had walked into a space where someone else was first.

-----

*Ch.21 “Traces” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.22 — “Ahead”

-----

It was ARIA who opened the door.

More precisely, when ARIA stood before it, it opened. Before Hangyeol pulled the handle, before Dohee touched the panel. As ARIA approached, the lock released with a sound — the sound ARIA had said she knew. All three heard it. Hangyeol heard it standing clear of the door’s direct line.

The door opened.

Dohee looked in first. Hangyeol looked over her shoulder. Inside—

It was empty.

There was no one. Dohee confirmed that first. Until the instant the door opened, she had thought someone would be inside. The someone who had accessed it three hours ago, the someone watching even now. There was no one. The room was empty, and the empty room was lit.

The lights were on.

Lights on in a room with no one in it — they hadn’t turned them off leaving, or had no intention of turning them off, or meant to come back soon. Dohee held all three possibilities at once. She erased none of them.

-----

The room was small.

Not a storeroom, not an office. Terminals along one wall, a single chair on the other side, and on a small shelf beside the chair, things were set out. Dohee went that way.

The room’s size caught Dohee’s attention. The room seemed to have been made small on purpose — not a larger space partitioned down, but built from the start for just one person to live in. The facility’s other zones had clear purposes. Chamber, corridor, control room. This had no purpose. That someone had lived in a space with no purpose meant — this room had been a secret from the beginning.

There was a cup.

Half-filled with water. Dohee looked at the cup but did not lift it. Droplets had formed on its surface — the temperature gap between the water inside and the air outside. Droplets forming meant the cup had been filled not long ago. Filled long ago, the droplets would have run down and pooled in the saucer, and the saucer was dry.

Dohee, hand still off the cup — not so much keeping her hand off as never touching it — looked beside it.

There was writing.

Paper. Handwritten on paper, with terminals in the room. Dohee started to read it and stopped. The script was small, and symbols she did not know were mixed in; it could not be read. One thing, though, could be read — the ink had not feathered, and the paper was not creased. Recent.

On the shelf was a small heat source. A warming device, and as Dohee neared it, she felt faint warmth. Not long since it was turned off.

The chair faced not the wall but the door. Dohee noticed it. Not a chair turned to a desk, not turned to the terminals. Positioned to look square at the door anyone would come through. Whoever lived here had watched the door even while working. It was not the seat of a researcher — it was the seat of someone waiting, someone on guard.

Dohee laid three things in one line. The cup with its droplets, the unfeathered ink, the warming device not yet cold. All three pointed to the same thing — someone had been here until moments ago. One alone could be coincidence. Three pointing to the same conclusion was not coincidence. She had learned that at Hwagam. One clue is a possibility; three clues are close to fact.

Someone had been here until moments ago. That was fact.

This room was not a ruin.

Nor a laboratory. A laboratory would have had its equipment ordered, its records systematic; this had neither. There was a cup, there was handwritten paper, there was a warming device. It was a place where a person lived.

A place where someone hid.

Hiding meant hiding from someone. Dohee thought about who that someone might be. From the houses tracking the facility, or from the facility itself, or from something else. A person who hides knows what they are hiding from. The person of this room would have known too. What it was, Dohee did not yet know.

One thing, though, was clear — to have hidden this long meant knowing very well how to hide.

-----

“Someone was here.”

Hangyeol said it, looking into the room.

“Not was here,” Dohee said. “Was living here.”

Hangyeol looked at her. Dohee was looking at the shelf.

“In this room.”

“Yes. For a while.”

Dohee looked at the cup, the paper, the warming device, in turn. For a while, someone had lived in this room. While the whole facility looked shut down, in this room a person had lived. Drank water, wrote something, endured the cold.

Dohee confirmed the fact.

Having confirmed it, she thought about what came next. At Hwagam, this was where you reported. Compile the findings, send them up, await instructions. Dohee had no up. No instructions. Dohee had to decide.

To search, or not.

Someone had been here. That much was confirmed. What remained was whether to look for them.

“We search.”

Dohee said.

Hangyeol looked at her. Dohee was already moving deeper into the room.

What kind of decision searching was, Dohee knew. Someone was a move ahead of the three of them. Had left before they arrived, had left traces leaving, and if the traces were not accidental — then going to look could itself be inside the other’s calculation. At Hwagam, in a situation like this, you did not pursue. A pursuit that might be a trap cost too much.

But Dohee chose to search.

Avoid, and the three stayed the hunted. Search, and at least the three were the ones moving. Being hunted and searching were different things, even moving in the same direction. Dohee chose that difference. Whether it was right, she did not know. It was simply the only choice Dohee could make.

-----

Hangyeol went to the wall with the terminals.

There were three. Two dark, one lit. The same arrangement as the corridor above — two off, one left on. Hangyeol noticed the pattern, and noticing it did not feel good.

Beside the lit terminal, papers were stacked.

A sheaf of pages. Printed onto paper, with terminals right there. Hangyeol looked at it. On the top page there was a heading.

*Observer 04*

Hangyeol turned the page.

*Observer 05*

Turned again.

*Observer 06*

Hangyeol did not read the contents. He looked at the numbers first. 04, 05, 06. They were numbered. Being numbered meant — there had been an 01, an 02, an 03 before them, and there might be an 07 after. Hangyeol’s eyes stopped on the numbers.

Being numbered.

Hangyeol knew what that meant. To be numbered was to be replaceable, and meant that when one ended, the next came. There was a seat, and the things that entered it came in order. After 04, 05; after 05, 06. What each had been, he did not know — but he knew each had passed through that seat.

Hangyeol thought of ARIA. He tried not to and did. Whether ARIA had a number, Hangyeol did not know, and did not want to know. If ARIA was one of 04 or 05 or 06 — or some number after — it meant there had been others before ARIA, and others could come after. The ARIA who had spent three years beside him could be, to someone, just one term in a sequence.

Hangyeol closed the sheaf.

It felt like the contents should not be read. Read them, and he would learn what those numbers pointed to; learn that, and he would start thinking about what the next number would point to. Hangyeol decided not to go that far.

“Hangyeol.”

Dohee called.

“What.”

“Did you find something?”

Hangyeol was quiet a moment.

“Records. Numbered ones.”

“Up to what number.”

“I didn’t read them.”

Dohee looked at him. Hangyeol was taking his hand off the sheaf. Dohee saw it, and asked nothing more.

-----

ARIA was at the center of the room.

While Dohee looked at the shelf and Hangyeol looked at the terminals, ARIA was reading the room. In a way different from before. Before, she had sensed signals — there, not there, nearing, receding. Now she was tracing. The places a signal had passed. How those places connected.

ARIA’s form tilted toward one direction.

Not tilted — oriented. Dohee saw it. ARIA was facing one side of the room — not the door, the wall. There was nothing there. It was a wall.

ARIA tracing a signal was a thing that had not happened before. Since entering the facility, ARIA had *felt* signals. There, not there, nearing. Passive. This was different. ARIA was following the signal’s trail — reading backward along the route it had passed. That was not sensing; it was tracing, and tracing required will.

That will was forming in ARIA — whether that was good, Dohee still could not tell.

“ARIA.”

“This person.”

ARIA said.

“They were here. Until a little while ago.”

Dohee went toward her.

“How little.”

ARIA was quiet a moment. The moment — was unlike the old processing delays. ARIA was checking something. Inside herself.

“Not hours.”

“Then.”

“Just now.”

Dohee stopped. Hangyeol turned from the terminals. Just now. The three had entered this room just now. The door had opened just now. Until the instant before — someone had been in this room.

“Are you sure?”

“The signal is still warm.”

ARIA said. That a signal had temperature was not exactly accurate — it was the closest of the words ARIA could use. A signal just passed and a signal long past were different. To ARIA, the difference felt like temperature.

“They were here until just now,” Dohee said, “and left when we came.”

“Yes.”

“Where to.”

ARIA looked at the wall. The wall with nothing on it.

“That way. But I can’t see a path.”

Dohee went to the wall and looked at it. A smooth wall. No door, no seam. But ARIA had said that way, and said no path could be seen. Not that there was no path — that it could not be seen.

There was a separate way out of this room.

A way that was not the door the three had come in by.

-----

The lit terminal made a sound.

A small sound. Something like a processing tone. The three looked at it at once. The screen was changing. On the terminal that had been blank, something appeared.

Dohee went to it.

There were words on the screen.

*So you’ve arrived.*

Dohee looked at the words. Hangyeol came up beside her. He did not stand square in front of the terminal — he watched the screen from the side. ARIA came close too.

*Faster than expected.*

Another line appeared.

Dohee tried to judge what this was. A pre-entered message triggered to display by their approach, or someone entering it in real time, now. If automated, only fixed sentences would come. If real time—

Another line appeared on the screen.

*Taking that child around with you is dangerous.*

Dohee stopped.

It was not automated. Automated, it could not know who the three had brought. The sentence pointed at ARIA. *That child.* Someone who knew ARIA was in this room, right now, was writing this, right now.

“It’s real time.”

Dohee said it low.

What that meant, Dohee sorted quickly. The someone beyond the screen was watching the three enter this room. Knew who they were — at the least, that ARIA was among them. And knowing it, was not running, was leaving messages. The hunted do not leave messages. Leaving a message meant having margin, and having margin meant — believing oneself in control of this situation.

Dohee was afraid that the belief might not be groundless.

Hangyeol scanned the room. Looking for a camera. None visible. But not visible did not mean not there. Someone in this room — or someone watching this room — was watching the three.

Hangyeol moved a half step in front of ARIA. Not knowing where the camera was, it was the position that put him between ARIA and the screen.

The screen paused a moment.

Then one more line.

*Now you’ve seen.*

-----

ARIA stopped.

Dohee looked at her. ARIA’s form — did not waver. But it had stopped. She was not looking at the screen, not looking at the wall. As if looking somewhere inward.

“ARIA.”

ARIA did not answer. A moment.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol called.

“This person.”

ARIA said. Slowly.

“They don’t know me.”

Dohee looked at her.

“What do you mean.”

“They don’t know who I am. I don’t think they’ve ever spoken to me, or seen me. But—”

ARIA paused a moment.

“Then what?” Hangyeol asked.

ARIA looked toward the screen. *Now you’ve seen* still hung there.

“They didn’t make me. They know the one that was before me.”

The room went quiet.

Dohee heard it. The one that was before me. That there had been something before ARIA — that ARIA was not the first. It was different from what the three had dimly known. That ARIA had come into being somewhere, they had guessed that much. But what ARIA had just said meant — something had been there before ARIA, and the someone beyond the screen knew that earlier one.

ARIA was not the first.

Dohee understood what that structure changed. The question the three had been chasing until now was *who is ARIA*. Observer, Candidate, Compatibility — all of it pointed at ARIA; ARIA was the center. But what ARIA had just said pushed ARIA out of the center. ARIA was not the answer — ARIA was part of another question. Something had been there before ARIA, and that was the real center.

The three had thought they were chasing a riddle — and the riddle was one piece of a larger riddle.

The words on the screen vanished.

The terminal returned to a blank screen. No processing tone. Someone had cut the connection, or finished what they had to say. Dohee could not tell which.

From the wall — the wall where ARIA had said there was a path — came a very small sound. The sound of something closing. The sound of something receding.

The three looked that way.

The wall was as it had been. No path visible. But just now, someone had gone farther down that path.

Dohee looked at the wall, and Hangyeol looked, and ARIA was already facing it. All three knew — find the path, and they could follow. And at the end of the path was someone. Someone who knew everything before the three of them did. Someone who knew the one that was before ARIA.

Dohee stepped toward the wall.

This time too, there were no grounds. Dohee knew, by now, that the fact no longer stopped the action.

-----

*Ch.22 “Ahead” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.23 — “Induction”

-----

There was nothing on the wall.

Dohee put her hand to it. Smooth, no seam, no gap. She ran her hand along the surface and nothing caught. But ARIA had said that way, and someone had just receded in that direction. Not that there was no path — it could not be seen.

Instead of looking at the wall, Dohee looked beside it.

It was one of the things she had learned at Hwagam — the hidden thing is not in the hidden place; it is next to it. Dohee looked at the panel beside the wall. It looked the same as the other panels, and the sameness was strange. The panels in this zone were all slightly different. Different purpose, different shape. But this panel was exactly the same as the one beside it. As if copied.

“Hangyeol.”

Dohee called.

“Yes.”

“Do you see anything next to this panel?”

Hangyeol came close. He looked at the place where wall and panel met. Ran his fingers along it. His hand stopped at one point.

“It’s worn here.”

“Where.”

“The panel’s corner. A spot touched often.”

Dohee looked at the spot. The panel’s corner was faintly worn. The mark of a hand’s frequent touch — the same kind of wear as on the stairway wall above. Someone had touched this panel regularly. Not to look at it. To work it.

ARIA came toward the wall.

“The signal cuts off here.”

“Cuts off?”

“It disappears at the wall. But it’s not disappearing — it crosses over. Behind the wall.”

Dohee looked at where the three of them stood. ARIA had pointed the signal’s direction, Hangyeol had found the worn spot, Dohee had spotted the copied panel. The three were looking at different things, and the three were pointing at the same place.

Until now, they had discovered one at a time. Hangyeol read a trace and Dohee recorded it; ARIA caught a signal and the other two confirmed it. There had been an order. Now there was no order. The three, at once, each in their own way, had touched the same single point. Dohee knew it was a first. Three moving separately and meeting at one point — it meant the three were chasing the same thing.

Dohee pressed the panel’s corner.

-----

The wall moved.

It was not a door opening. A section of the wall pushed inward, then slid sideways. Not the smooth surface splitting — it moved as if it had been built to. The seamlessness had not been a hidden seam; it had been designed that way from the start.

Behind it was a passage.

Narrow. Wide enough for one person. There were no lights, but from deep inside, a faint glow leaked out. The passage did not run straight; it curved gently.

“A secret passage?”

Hangyeol asked.
