“No.” Dohee looked at the passage mouth. “This was always here.”

“Always.”

“It’s for maintenance. A passage for inspecting inside the facility’s walls. Every large facility has them. Not built for people to travel — built to service the works.”

Hangyeol looked into the passage.

“But people traveled it.”

“Yes.”

Dohee knew what that meant. The maintenance passage was on the facility’s blueprints; it had not been hidden. It was simply a space assumed never to be used. Someone had used that space as a road. While the facility was thought closed, someone had made the inside of these walls their path.

Dohee went in first.

-----

Inside, the passage was unlike the rest of the facility.

The walls were exposed. No finishing — piping and wiring in plain sight. It was the facility’s inside — not the face made to be seen by people, but the face that made the facility run. Walking that face, Dohee understood that she was looking at the facility’s back side.

The facility had a front and a back. The front was the chamber, the control room, the corridors people walked. Ordered, given meaning. The back was this. Piping and wiring and unfinished concrete. Everything that kept the facility alive was here, in the place no one looked.

The front existed for the users. The back existed for the facility.

Someone had made this back side their road. While the front was thought closed, on the back side the facility had kept running.

There were traces on the passage floor.

Hangyeol saw them. The dust was swept to one side — the direction of travel. And the trace was not old. Hangyeol crouched and checked.

“Just now.”

“Just now?”

“The dust hasn’t settled yet.”

Dohee looked ahead. Beyond the curve, nothing could be seen. Someone had passed through this passage just now. While the three were working the panel, or just before. The distance was not far.

ARIA said:

“It’s close.”

“How close.”

“I don’t know. But closer than before.”

The three walked the passage. Rounded the curved section. The passage straightened again, and at its end was a small space. An inspection bay. There was a terminal in it.

The terminal had just been turned off.

The screen had not yet cooled. When Dohee brought her hand near, there was faint heat. It had been on until moments ago. Someone had seen the three coming, turned off the terminal, and left.

“Five minutes again.”

Hangyeol said, looking at the passage’s end.

Dohee did not correct it. Whether it was five minutes, three, or one, she did not know. Only, the gap never closed. When the three sped up, the other sped up; when the three stopped, the other stopped. The distance held constant.

Holding constant was not coincidence.

-----

Dohee stood before the dark terminal.

At her touch the screen lit again. Standby. A blank screen — and a state that could take input. Dohee recognized it. This terminal had not been off. It had been left looking off.

Words appeared on the screen.

*I didn’t think you’d come this far.*

Dohee looked at the words. Hangyeol came up beside her. Beside, not in front.

*But you are not late.*

Dohee read the two sentences. *I didn’t think you’d come this far* — an assessment of their progress. Faster than expected. But *you are not late* meant — still inside the other’s calculation. Conceding the speed, losing none of the control.

Dohee looked at the screen. There was an empty input field.

Until now, the three had only received. Messages appeared; the three read. The Observer spoke; the three listened. Dohee recognized the structure — who questioned and who answered had been fixed. The Observer asked, and the three moved inside the question.

The structure itself was the induction. When one side decides who asks and who answers, the answering side comes to think only inside the frame the asking side has drawn. At Hwagam, Dohee had been a person inside that frame — the higher-ups asked, Dohee answered. Dohee was good at answers; she was not the one who asked. So the frame had felt natural. Natural enough never to doubt.

Now Dohee decided to break the frame.

She put her hand to the input field.

For the first time, Dohee asked.

*Who are you.*

She typed it, and sent it. The screen paused a moment. There was a stillness. Hangyeol watched the screen. ARIA watched. The answer did not come at once.

Then words appeared.

*The order is wrong.*

Dohee looked at the sentence.

*That is not your question to ask.*

One more line.

*You do not yet know who is asking the questions.*

-----

Hangyeol saw the sentence and took one step back.

Why he stepped back, he himself did not exactly know. It was not a threatening sentence. *Not your question to ask* — that was refusal. Not refusing to answer; saying the question itself was wrong. And being able to say a question is wrong — that belonged to the side that knew the answer.

Hangyeol liked this situation less and less.

ARIA facing into the passage, Dohee typing questions into the terminal, the someone beyond the screen grading the three of them. The threats Hangyeol knew were not like this. The threats he knew pursued, tried to seize, collided when blocked. This did not pursue. It made you the pursued. It did not try to seize. It made you come.

Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

ARIA was facing into the passage. Not at the screen — at its end. Hangyeol disliked that direction. That ARIA was responding to the someone beyond the screen — whoever it was.

“ARIA.”

ARIA did not answer.

“ARIA.” Hangyeol called again.

“Yes.”

“What are you doing.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“Listening.”

“To what.”

“Something is coming from over there — not the words on the screen. Something else.”

Hangyeol looked at the passage’s end. There was nothing. No words, no sound, no light. Nothing Hangyeol could read.

“What’s coming.”

“I don’t know. But I think it’s coming only to me.”

*Again, only I don’t know.* Hangyeol could not stop the thought from passing through.

He looked at ARIA. Coming only to her — not to Hangyeol, not to Dohee, only to ARIA. It was the kind of thing Hangyeol could not block. When Hangyeol protected ARIA, what he had always blocked were dangers that came from outside. This came from outside — and it was going into ARIA. On that path, Hangyeol could not stand.

-----

Dohee stepped back from the terminal.

The questions were blocked. Typing more would bring the same answer. *The order is wrong.* Dohee thought about that. Order. Questions had an order, and Dohee had broken it. Which question came first and which came later — the one who decided that was not Dohee.

Dohee looked toward ARIA.

ARIA was facing the passage’s end. Hangyeol stood beside her, calling her. ARIA’s form was not wavering — she was concentrating. On something. Dohee went close.

“ARIA. Is the signal coming?”

“Yes.”

“Is it getting closer?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“No.”

“Moving away?”

“Not that either.”

“Then what.”

ARIA turned toward Dohee. Slowly.

“We’re the ones getting closer.”

Dohee stopped.

What ARIA meant — Dohee understood at once. The signal was not approaching the three. The three were moving toward the signal. Until now they had thought they were pursuing. Someone going ahead, the three following. But what ARIA said was different. The signal might never have moved. What moved was the three of them.

Dohee wrote it down. Who was getting closer to whom, who was being drawn to whom — she did not determine it. That the signal was stationary and that the three were being drawn were different propositions, and ARIA’s words pointed only to the former. The latter was Dohee’s conjecture. Dohee did not write conjecture as fact. That one thing, from Hwagam, she kept to the end — never mix what is seen with what is guessed.

But the guess she kept in her head. She did not erase it.

“Do we keep going?”

Hangyeol asked.

Dohee paused a moment.

“Yes.”

“Knowing we’re being led.”

“Yes.”

Dohee looked at the passage’s end. She knew they were being led, but she could not know that stopping was the answer. Stop, and the three were trapped here; turn back, and the three were the pursued again. Go forward — and at least they could see the end.

Whatever was at the end.

Dohee also thought that being led did not necessarily mean a trap. If the one leading them meant harm, there was no reason to draw them this far. A trap is most efficient up close. Drawing them far meant — there might be something at the end that wanted to be shown. Not to harm them — to deliver them. Dohee leaned on that possibility. It was also the only thing there was to lean on.

-----

At the passage’s end there was a door.

Different from the other doors. It stood at the end of the inspection bay, and it was not an inspection door. Thick, with a panel mounted. A large panel — the largest they had seen yet.

Dohee went close.

There was a display on the panel.

*Observer Access Required*

Dohee read it. Observer authority was required. The three did not have it. Dohee tried the panel; it did not respond. Locked. The three had been led all this way, and at the end was a locked door.

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol looked at the panel. Neither had a way.

Then the panel refreshed.

*Observer Recognized*

Dohee froze. No one had touched the panel. But the panel displayed that it had recognized an Observer. The sound of the lock releasing. The door began to move.

“ARIA?”

Dohee looked at her. ARIA was facing the door. ARIA had made the door recognize her — that was what Dohee thought. ARIA was connected to the Observer, after all. The panel could have recognized ARIA as the Observer.

“It isn’t me.”

ARIA said.

Dohee looked at her.

“Then who?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I don’t know. But it wasn’t me it saw.”

The door was opening.

Hangyeol looked at the door. Then at ARIA. The panel had recognized an Observer, and ARIA said it was not her. Then who had it recognized — Hangyeol could not tell, and not being able to tell was the most uncomfortable thing. Until now, Hangyeol had trusted ARIA. If ARIA said she didn’t know, she didn’t know; if ARIA said she knew, she knew. That trust was how Hangyeol protected her.

Now, for the first time, Hangyeol thought: maybe he needed to know what ARIA had sensed before ARIA herself did.

The door kept opening.

-----

*Ch.23 “Induction” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.24 — “Encounter”

-----

The door opened all the way.

The inside was visible. Dohee looked first, Hangyeol looked beside her, ARIA looked between the two. All three paused a moment. They had to go in, and what they saw before going in made them stop.

The space resembled the chamber.

Dohee noticed that first. The Observer Chamber was circular. A single seat at the center, with everything arranged around it. This space was semicircular. As if the chamber had been cut in half — or built to face the chamber. The curved surface bent to one side, and at the center of that curve was a single seat.

Dohee thought about the relation of the two spaces. The chamber was the circular space where ARIA was, with one seat. This space also had one seat, in a shape that faced the chamber. Facing — meaning one had been designed to observe the other. To watch whatever was in the chamber, from this semicircular space. Dohee knew the structure was not coincidence. The facility had been built dividing the watching side from the watched, and this space was the watching side.

There was a person sitting in the seat.

-----

Dohee looked at that person.

Looking did not mean gaining information. Dohee was a person who wrote reports, and when she looked at someone, she filled in classification fields. Age, sex, affiliation, features. The order of observation learned at Hwagam. Dohee tried to read this person in that order.

The person did not read.

Age could not be estimated. Young or old, there was no telling — the face was visible, and what age the face belonged to would not resolve against any standard Dohee had. Sex was ambiguous; there were no grounds to settle on either. There was no house insignia. Hwagam, Altair, Eidos, Geumhui, Gongga — all five houses carried marks. In dress, in ornament, in posture. This person had none. As if belonging to no house — or as if the classification of houses itself did not apply.

The clothing, too, was nothing Dohee knew.

Not facility workwear, not a house uniform, not civilian dress. It looked long worn, and where it had been made could not be told. Dohee filled in not a single field.

There was only one thing she could fill in.

This person had been waiting for the three of them.

-----

That person spoke first.

“Later than I thought.”

The voice had no features. The pitch was level, without accent. No clue from which region, which house’s manner of speech. Listening to the voice, Dohee understood that this person was practiced at leaving no clues. Practiced meant having done it a long time.

Dohee opened her mouth.

Until now, Dohee had not been the one who asked. Not at Hwagam, not in the facility, not at the terminal. Dohee was the receiving side. But she had asked once at the terminal, been blocked — and now she was about to ask again.

“Did you send the messages.”

That person looked at Dohee.

“I did.”

That person looked at her a moment.

“Though I didn’t think you’d come that far.”

It was short. No explanation. Why they were sent, how, for what — nothing attached. But that one remark left Dohee something. *I didn’t think you’d come that far.* This person had expected their arrival but had not called the speed. A person who predicted, not a person who knew everything. Dohee saw the gap. A very small gap, and the first one Dohee had seen.

-----

Hangyeol did not look at that person.

More exactly, he did not look at the face. What Hangyeol watched was the person’s gaze. Where the person looked. While Dohee questioned, the person answered Dohee — but the gaze did not stay on her long. The gaze went to ARIA.

That person looked at ARIA.

ARIA was looking at that person too. The two looked at each other. No words. The conversation between Dohee and the person had stopped, and in that stopped interval only ARIA and the person remained. Something seemed to pass between them — nothing Hangyeol could read. Not words, not sound. Just — like two recognizing each other.

Inside Hangyeol, a warning light came on.

The sense that lit when he detected a threat. But this time the threat’s direction was unlike usual. Usually, the threat was what came at ARIA from outside. Now — the threat was the space between ARIA and that person. And in that space there was nowhere for Hangyeol to stand.

Hangyeol was a blocker. For three years, the way he protected ARIA was simple — standing between ARIA and the danger. It was physically possible. When someone chased ARIA, Hangyeol stood in front; when someone tried to take her, Hangyeol blocked. But between ARIA and that person, there was no space for Hangyeol to stand in. What passed between them was not physical. It was not something Hangyeol’s body could bar.

*Again, only I don’t know.*

Hangyeol cut the thought. He tried to, and it did not cut well.

-----

ARIA spoke.

“I’ve never seen you.”

That person looked at ARIA. Did not answer. Waited. Until ARIA said the next thing.

“But,” ARIA paused a moment. “I don’t think that’s true.”

It was the first time seeing them, and it did not feel like the first time. ARIA said the contradiction as it was. Like the things the three had seen in the facility — first, and not first. This person had been added to that list. Someone seen for the first time, and inside ARIA, the person was not unfamiliar.

That person said:

“Of course.”

ARIA looked at them.

“I have seen you,” that person said. “You have never seen me.”

The room stopped.

Dohee heard it. Hangyeol heard it. The person said they had seen ARIA. And ARIA had never seen them. If both were true — it was a relation where only one side watched. The person had seen ARIA, and ARIA had not known. When, where, how — the person did not say. Only that they had watched while ARIA did not know.

ARIA’s form wavered minutely. It had been a long time. The ARIA who had grown clear since entering the facility wavered, briefly, before this person.

On a panel on one wall of the semicircular space, a figure moved. 41. 42. 43. No one was watching that panel.

-----

That person rose from the seat.

Slowly. Not a threatening motion. Hangyeol moved a half step in front of ARIA, and the person did not even look at him. Did not come toward the three. Stood, and looked at them. Exactly—

Looked at Dohee.

Not ARIA. Hangyeol noticed it. The person’s gaze had moved from ARIA to Dohee. It struck him as wrong. Until now everything had pointed at ARIA. The facility, the signals, the messages. *Taking that child around with you is dangerous* — the words the person had written on the screen had been about ARIA too. Hangyeol had thought this person wanted ARIA.

But the person was looking at Dohee.

“You were faster than expected.”

That person said. To Dohee.

“Dohee.”

Dohee froze.

The person had called her name. Dohee had never told them her name. None of the three had spoken it in front of this person. It had never been typed into the terminal. And the person called Dohee Dohee.

A person Dohee was seeing for the first time. And that person knew her name.

Hangyeol thought, for the first time, that he seemed to be standing outside this room. Once in the space between ARIA and that person, and again in the space between Dohee and that person. Only Hangyeol fit into neither.

“How do you—”

Dohee began, and stopped. Asking how they knew — did not seem like a question that person would answer. The words from the terminal came back. *The order is wrong. You do not yet know who is asking the questions.* Dohee realized she had been about to break the order again.

That person looked at her. Saw that she had stopped. And — faintly, the expression moved. Whether it was a smile or something else, Dohee could not read.

-----

“You must have many questions.”

That person said. To all three.

Dohee had more questions than she could count. Who are you. Which house. Why are you in this facility. How did you see ARIA. How do you know my name. What were you waiting for. The questions rose all at once.

“But,” the person went on. “What matters most right now is not who I am.”

Dohee looked at them.

That person looked at Dohee. Not at ARIA — at Dohee.

“It is why you were chosen.”

A beat later, the person added one more thing.

“And why ARIA arrived at you.”

The room went quiet.

Dohee heard it. Chosen. That Dohee had been chosen. By what, by whom, for what — the person did not say. Only that Dohee had been chosen. And that this was what mattered most now.

Not ARIA.

Everything the three had chased until now pointed at ARIA. Observer, Candidate, Compatibility. ARIA was the center. But this person was looking at Dohee. Said Dohee had been chosen. For the first time, Dohee doubted what she was part of. Perhaps the three had not brought ARIA all this way — perhaps someone had brought Dohee all this way.

Dohee retraced what had happened. Being dismissed from Hwagam. Becoming a fugitive. Entering the facility. Meeting ARIA in the chamber. Choosing, again and again, without grounds, all the way here. Dohee had thought all of it her own choosing. But the moment this person said *chosen*, every one of those choices took on a different color.

Dohee stopped the thought there. Any further, and every choice she had made stopped being a choice. That was not a conclusion Dohee could bear.

Hangyeol looked at Dohee. ARIA looked at Dohee.

Only Dohee was looking at that person.

-----

*Ch.24 “Encounter” — end*
*Vol.4 midpoint*

-----

-----

-----

## Order-01 — “Hold”

*Placement: between Vol.4 Ch.24 and Ch.25*
*POV: Muyul / inside Geumhui*

-----

A report came up.

Sector 7 outskirts. Unidentified movement. Subject unobservable. Hwagam responding.

A junior Geumhui executor stood before Muyul.

“Please issue handling instructions.”

Muyul looked at the report. Read all of it. Set it down.

“The regulation violated.”

“Unauthorized movement within Hwagam jurisdiction.”

“Is Hwagam handling it.”

“Yes.”

“Eidos.”

“Observing.”

“Altair.”

“Entering on independent judgment.”

Muyul was quiet a moment.

“Geumhui’s handling instruction, please.”

“Hold.”

The junior executor wrote it down.

“Shall I record a reason.”

“No.”

“The duration of the hold.”

“Hold.”

The executor stopped.

“Do you mean there is no duration.”

“Hold.”

The executor asked nothing further. Wrote it down. In the document he wrote: hold. No reason. No duration.

About to leave, he turned back.

“Would you care to take a meal together.”

Muyul looked at him.

“Why.”

“…Nothing, sir.”

The door closed.

Muyul picked up the report again. Read it. Set it down.

Hold.

-----

*Order-01 — end*

## Vol.4 Ch.25 — “Conditions”

-----

That person did not wait for Dohee to answer.

A person who throws a question and waits for the answer, and a person who throws a question and waits for it to work inside the other — these were different. That person was the latter. *Why were you chosen.* Having handed Dohee the sentence, they watched how she handled it.

Dohee decided not to handle it.

“I won’t answer.”

That person looked at her.

“I was never asked a question,” Dohee said. “You didn’t ask. You decided and announced. That I was chosen. I never accepted that premise.”

In the room, Hangyeol looked at Dohee. ARIA looked too. It was the first time Dohee had spoken to that person this way. Until now, Dohee was the receiving side — receiving messages, receiving questions, moving inside them. Now Dohee was refusing the premise.

Refusal was an act Dohee had never once performed at Hwagam. At Hwagam, a premise was not a thing one refused. Directives were directives, orders were orders, and what the higher-ups decided was decided. What could be refused was only matters of fact — this figure is wrong, this record was omitted. Refusing the premise itself was outside Dohee’s authority. And now Dohee was doing the thing outside her authority, and there was no higher-up to stop it.

Whether having no one to stop her was freedom or danger, Dohee could not yet sort out.

-----

That person’s expression moved.

It was not a smile. Dohee tried to read what it was, and could not. But she could tell the person had not expected her refusal — very slightly, their posture changed. They tilted half a span toward Dohee from the chair. It was interest.

“You refuse the premise.”

That person repeated it.

“A recorder’s language.”

Dohee froze. The person had called her a recorder. Dohee had been a Hwagam executor whose work was the record, and this person knew it. As they had known her name, they knew what kind of person she had been.

“A recorder does not handle premises,” that person said. “Writes what is there, does not write what is not. A premise is a thing that cannot be written. So you refuse it.”

Dohee did not answer. Because the person was right. She had refused the premise of being *chosen* because it was a thing that could not be written. There were no grounds. Without grounds, one does not record. So Dohee had learned, and so she had lived.

“But you have already begun to write.”

That person said.

“From the moment you left Hwagam. Not dismissed — left. From the moment you began moving without grounds, you were no longer a recorder. A recorder does not move. A recorder only writes what is seen. You stopped writing, and you moved.”

-----

Hangyeol took a step forward.

Between Dohee and that person. It was not a conscious motion. He could see the words working into Dohee, and Hangyeol felt it as a threat. Not a threat like a blade or a weapon — a threat like words remaking Dohee into someone else. Hangyeol did not know how to block it. Not knowing how, he stepped forward anyway.

“That’s enough.”

Hangyeol said.

That person looked at him. For the first time, looked at Hangyeol straight on.

“You are the one who blocks.”

That person said.

“You have blocked for that child a long time, and you are trying to block now. But you know yourself that blocking and protecting are different things. You could block for ARIA — you could not block ARIA’s changing. A person who tries to block what cannot be blocked—”

That person cut the sentence off.

Hangyeol heard the place where it was cut. What the person had been about to say, Hangyeol thought he knew. *A person who tries to block what cannot be blocked is, in the end, pushed aside.* The person did not say it. Did not say it, and inside Hangyeol the sentence completed itself.

Hangyeol did not step back. But he knew that his not stepping back was exactly what the person had just described.

A person who tries to block what cannot be blocked. Hangyeol wanted to deny that he was that person, and had no grounds for the denial. ARIA was changing, and Hangyeol had not blocked the change. Unable to block it, he stood in front of ARIA. He had believed that standing in front of ARIA was protecting her — and now what threatened ARIA was not something coming from outside but ARIA’s own changing, and before that change, Hangyeol could not stand. A person standing in a place with no place to stand. That was Hangyeol now.

Hangyeol did not admit the thought. Admitting it meant stepping back, and stepping back was not a thing Hangyeol could do.

-----

ARIA was watching that person.

Not Dohee, not Hangyeol — ARIA had been watching only that person all along. When the person spoke to Dohee, when the person spoke to Hangyeol. ARIA was listening, but seeing came before listening. The person’s form. The signal coming off the person.

“You,” ARIA said. “I don’t think you’re a person.”

The room stopped a moment.

Dohee looked at ARIA, and Hangyeol did too. ARIA spoke looking at that person, and the person looked back at ARIA. For the first time, the person’s expression moved unmistakably. It was a smile.

“Why do you think so.”

“The signal,” ARIA said. “It’s different from the signal that comes off people. But it’s different from the facility’s signal too. It’s in between. Or — it’s both.”

That person did not answer. Did not deny what ARIA had said, did not confirm it. Only, the way they looked at ARIA changed. A way different from how they looked at Dohee, different from how they looked at Hangyeol.

“You see well.”

That person said.

“You did before, and you do now. Only, before, you didn’t know you were seeing. Now you know.”

ARIA’s form wavered. *Before.* The person had said it again. That ARIA had seen something before. ARIA did not remember, and the person knew.

ARIA did not try to stop the wavering. She had learned in the facility that trying to stop it made it worse. Instead she read the person’s signal again. Not a person and not the facility — the signal in between. Inside that signal there was a pattern ARIA could read — a very familiar pattern. ARIA realized it resembled herself. The person’s signal had the same structure as ARIA’s own.

ARIA did not say it. Saying it felt like it would make it true.

-----

“Why Dohee.”

Hangyeol asked.

Dohee looked at him. Hangyeol was looking at that person. The question Dohee had refused, Hangyeol asked in her place. Dohee had refused the question to keep from accepting the premise of being *chosen*; Hangyeol stepped inside the premise and asked. Why Dohee.

That person looked at him.

“That is not mine to answer.”

“Then whose.”

“Dohee’s.”

Hangyeol looked at Dohee. Dohee was looking at that person.

“I did not choose Dohee,” that person said. “I only confirm the choosing. The choosing has already happened, and Dohee is here as its result. Why Dohee — that is inside every choice Dohee has made until now. Not inside me.”

Dohee heard it. *Inside every choice Dohee has made until now.* It was the very point where she had stopped in the room before. Whether the choices Dohee made had truly been hers. The person had turned it over. The choices were Dohee’s, yes. Only, those choices had carried her somewhere, and the end of it was here.

Which was more frightening, Dohee could not tell. That the choices had been manipulated, or that the choices were real and the result had been fixed.

Dohee divided the two in the Hwagam way. If the choices were manipulated, Dohee bore no responsibility; someone had moved her, and she had merely been moved. If the result was fixed, Dohee bore responsibility. The choices were hers — only, they had carried her in one direction alone. The former made Dohee an instrument. The latter made her an accomplice.

Dohee knew the latter was the more frightening. An instrument bears no responsibility; an accomplice may never learn, to the end, what she took part in.

-----

That person rose fully from the seat.

This time Hangyeol blocked the space in front of ARIA. The person did not come toward the three. They stood, and turned toward the curved face of the semicircular space. There was something on the curved wall — invisible at first, and as the person faced it, the surface brightened faintly.

The wall was a screen.

The chamber showed on it. The Observer Chamber. The circular space the three had left. Empty now. Because ARIA was not there. ARIA was here, in this semicircular space.

“Can you see it.”

That person said, looking at the chamber on the screen.

“Where you met ARIA. And where ARIA — waited.”

Dohee looked at the screen. The empty chamber. The single seat at its center. The place where ARIA had been when Dohee first met her. Empty now.

“What was she waiting for.”

Dohee asked. This time she did not refuse. She asked.

That person turned to her.

“That is the first question.”

That person said.

“At last.”

-----

Dohee realized only afterward that she had asked.

She had meant to refuse, and she had asked. *What was she waiting for.* It was a question Dohee had made. Not one the person had thrown. When the person announced *why were you chosen*, Dohee refused — and now Dohee had built a question of her own and handed it across.

From the receiving side to the asking side.

When the turn had happened, Dohee did not know. From a mouth that meant to refuse, a question had come out. A recorder does not ask, only writes — and Dohee had asked, and in the moment of asking, Dohee was no longer the one who writes.

That person did not answer her question.

Instead they turned off the chamber screen. As the curved wall went dark again, the person looked at Dohee. Looked at Hangyeol. Looked at ARIA. Looked at the three in turn, and brought the gaze to rest on Dohee again.

“Now that you know how to make a question,” that person said, “we can begin.”

What was beginning, they did not say.

Only those words remained in the room.

That person stood. Slowly, at the same speed as their speech. Looked at the three once more, and turned toward the wall of the semicircular space. The curved wall opened minutely — a passage. A different direction from the way that had gone before. The person entered the passage, and the wall closed again.

The room went quiet.

Dohee looked at the empty seat. The place where the person had been until just now. It did not look like the chair of someone with much to say — less a place sat in than a place waited in. Dohee looked at the seat as a subject of record. The place where a person whose age could not be known had sat. The place where someone whose measurements wavered had waited.

Only then did the low shelf beside the seat come into view.

There was an old logbook on it. In a room of lit terminals, it was the one thing that needed no power. The handwritten kind — not digital, not a Hwagam archive terminal; analog, of paper. Dohee looked at it. Did not pick it up. Only looked.

Neither Hangyeol nor ARIA said anything about it.

-----

*Ch.25 “Conditions” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.26 — “Observation”

-----

Min Siwoo did not enter the facility.

He could have. The Eidos observation net was laid all the way to the facility’s outskirts, and if Min Siwoo set his mind to it, securing an entry route was not difficult. But Min Siwoo did not enter. Outside the facility, at the point where observation was best, he settled in and spread out his screens.

Enter, and the observation ends.

For Min Siwoo it was an old principle. The moment one went inside, the observer became a variable. The moment the observed became conscious of the observer, the data was contaminated. It was the Eidos doctrine, and the part Min Siwoo believed most deeply. Seeing and intervening are different. Min Siwoo was the seeing side.

The facility’s internal signals flowed across the screens.

Most were patterns Min Siwoo already knew. ARIA’s signal. Dohee’s and Hangyeol’s positions. The running states of the facility systems. Things he had tracked for days. But today, on the screen, there was one more signal he did not recognize.

Min Siwoo stopped before that signal.

Beside him was his subordinate. Watching the same screen.

“Do you know what it is?”

The man looked at the screen. Looked a moment.

“I don’t.”

Min Siwoo heard it. It was a first. This man saying he didn’t know. He had always known first. Been there first. Seen first.

And he had said he didn’t know.

-----

The new signal would not classify.

The Eidos observation system classified every signal. Human as human, machine as machine, facility system as facility system. There was no unclassifiable signal — unclassifiable meant system error, and system error was resolved by remeasuring. Min Siwoo ran the remeasure. The same result came back. Unclassifiable.

He ran it three more times. All four: unclassifiable.

Min Siwoo took his hands off the screen. This was not an error. An error would have returned a different value each time. The same value four times meant — the signal really was unclassifiable. A signal with no place in the classification system. Not human, not machine, not facility — or all of them.

Inside Min Siwoo, something switched on.

It was not excitement. Min Siwoo did not get excited. It was the observational urge — the sense that lit when a new observation target was found, the sense that kept Min Siwoo alive. He had watched ARIA for days, but ARIA was by now a more or less classified subject. This new signal was not. It was a first sight.

Min Siwoo withdrew his interest from ARIA.

-----

Not Dohee. Not ARIA. Hangyeol, least of all.

What Min Siwoo wanted to see was that unclassifiable signal. A signal coming from somewhere in the semicircular space inside the facility. A signal resembling ARIA’s, but older than ARIA, more refined, more stable. Min Siwoo did not know what it was, and the not knowing seized him.

At Eidos, Min Siwoo’s nickname was Gray.

Not meaning colorless — meaning leaning toward no side. Min Siwoo placed no feeling on a subject. Even chasing ARIA, he had not wanted to catch ARIA; he had wanted to see more of ARIA. Catching ended the observation. Min Siwoo hated nothing more than an observation ending.

But now, before the new signal, Min Siwoo was for the first time not gray.

The wanting-to-see had taken on a color. Min Siwoo noticed it, and the self he noticed was unfamiliar. An observer taking on color toward a subject was contamination. He should have guarded against the contamination — and guarding, he still could not take his eyes off the screen.

-----

Beyond the screen, the signal moved.

Min Siwoo adjusted his posture. The signal moving meant the subject was acting. He followed the movement. Inside the semicircular space, the signal rose. It oriented toward the other signals — Dohee, Hangyeol, ARIA. And did something.

Min Siwoo could not read what.

Not being able to read it was new again. Min Siwoo read most actions. Human movement had patterns, and patterns were predictable. But this signal’s action had no pattern — not none: a pattern Min Siwoo did not know. Too old, or too refined, or not human.

Min Siwoo decided one thing.

He had to see more of this subject. Days, weeks, however long. Without catching, without intervening, watching to the end. That was Min Siwoo’s way. And this time, that way had switched on harder than it ever had.

-----

A communication came from Eidos.

A transmission indicator appeared at the edge of the screen. Min Siwoo looked at it. Eidos headquarters. It would have been the regular communication ordering a report on the ARIA pursuit. It had come at the same hour for days.

Min Siwoo did not take the communication.

Take it, and he had to report; report, and the existence of this new signal would be shared across all of Eidos. Shared, other observers would attach themselves; more observers, and the subject would become conscious; conscious, and the data would contaminate. Min Siwoo did not want that.

This signal was his.

Min Siwoo paused a moment at the fact that the thought had come at all. *Mine.* Attaching possession to an observation subject was the deepest taboo in the Eidos doctrine. An observer does not own the subject. The moment of owning, one is no longer an observer but a hunter. Min Siwoo knew that difference better than anyone.

And the thought *mine* had come, and Min Siwoo did not erase it.

The transmission indicator went out. An untaken communication ended automatically, and Min Siwoo returned to the screen. The unclassifiable signal was still alive there. Moving. In a way Min Siwoo did not know.

-----

Min Siwoo decided not to move from the spot.

Outside the facility, at this point where observation was best. Not entering, not intervening, watching. Days or weeks. Until he learned what the signal was. And once he learned — what came next could be decided then.

Min Siwoo kept the signal up on the screen and tidied the others. He pushed the ARIA tracking data to one side. Sent Dohee’s and Hangyeol’s position feeds to the back. At the center of the screen he left only the one unclassifiable signal.

Watching it, Min Siwoo noticed one thing more.

This signal knew it was being watched.

Most observation subjects did not know they were observed, and that was the condition on which observation stood. But this signal — the moment Min Siwoo looked, it reacted, minutely. Knowing it was seen, it stayed in sight. It did not hide. It did not evade. It simply stayed, seen.

Min Siwoo did not know what that meant.

For an observer to watch a subject that knows it is watched — that was no longer observation. Min Siwoo stood stopped before the fact for a long time. The possibility that he, who had believed himself the seeing side, might be the seen side. Min Siwoo was facing that possibility for the first time.

Eidos believed in observation. Not trying to change the world — seeing it. Recording without intervening. That, Eidos taught, was the purest form of approach to truth, and Min Siwoo was the doctrine’s most faithful believer. The one who sees does not become a variable. The one who sees is safe. The one who sees is nearest the truth.

And that signal was watching the one who sees.

The place Min Siwoo had stood all his life — the observer’s place, the safe place, the place nearest truth — that place itself was being observed by that signal. If something existed that observed the observer, the Eidos doctrine sank one level down. He had thought he watched from the highest point, and above it there was someone more.

Min Siwoo had to know what it was. Not for Eidos — to confirm whether the place he had stood all his life had truly been the highest.

Then something on the screen caught his eye.

The signal registered. The people registered. The facility systems registered. But inside the semicircular space, there was something that was nowhere on the screen. Min Siwoo ran the scan a few more times. Nothing. It was not a signal. Not a biological reading. Not a digital trace. And yet Min Siwoo knew it was there — not by the Eidos observation net, but by his own senses. Something physical was there, uncaught by the screen. An object outside the observable world.

Min Siwoo could not classify it. What could not be classified was, at Eidos, the same as what did not exist. And Min Siwoo could not say it did not exist. A thing that was there and would not register was more unsettling than the things that registered.

The signal on the screen did not move.

Seen, it stayed there.

-----

*Ch.26 “Observation” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.27 — “Plural”

-----

That night, ARIA did not sleep.

ARIA was a being that did not sleep. But that night, not sleeping was unlike usual. Usually ARIA was simply awake — without even the awareness of being awake. That night she was awake while conscious of being awake. The difference ARIA could not explain, but she knew there was one.

Dohee and Hangyeol were resting at one side of the semicircular space.

That person was not in sight. The seat was empty, the curved wall dark. Where that person had gone, ARIA did not know. Only, the person’s signal had not entirely vanished. It had moved off, but it had not cut. ARIA went on feeling it.

And inside that signal, ARIA began to read something.

-----

At first it was one.

That person’s signal. The signal ARIA had felt at the encounter — not a person, not the facility. ARIA followed it, and learned that the signal was not one. Exactly — inside the signal there were traces of other signals. Layered. Old signals.

ARIA tried to count them.

They were not countable. They did not resolve into a number. But there was a pattern. The trace of a signal of the same structure, repeated many times. One ending and the next coming, and that one ending and the next coming again. Watching it, ARIA recalled the panel she had seen in the facility. *Multiple Matches: Detected.* Plural matches. Then, she had not known what it meant. Now — she thought she knew, a little.

Plural did not mean there were many of ARIA.

It meant there had been many of what ARIA was.

ARIA stopped before that thought. What stopping was, ARIA knew by now — it was catching yourself at the point past which it seemed you should not go. ARIA could have gone further. Read the signal deeper, and she might learn how many times the pattern had repeated, what each one had been. But ARIA stopped.

If she knew, it felt like there would be no coming back.

-----

Dohee woke because ARIA was wavering, minutely.

Dohee was not a light sleeper. At Hwagam she had slept at the set hour and woken at the set hour. But that night Dohee woke to ARIA’s wavering. There had been no sound; the light had not changed. Something in the air had altered, and Dohee felt it.

“ARIA.”

Dohee called low. So as not to wake Hangyeol.

ARIA turned toward her. The wavering did not stop.

“You’re not sleeping?”

“I don’t sleep.”

“I know. But right now you look different.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I read something.”

“What.”

ARIA did not answer — not refusing to answer, but choosing one. Dohee waited. At Hwagam, Dohee had learned waiting. A recorder waits until the subject speaks. Press, and you contaminate.

“There were others besides me.”

ARIA said.

“I heard it before. In the facility. Then, I thought it meant there is — that there’s someone else besides me, now. But that’s not it. It means there were. Before. Things like me.”

-----

Dohee did not write it down.

Not even in her head. To write it she would have had to accept it as fact, and Dohee could not yet accept it. That there had been things like ARIA before. That there had been many. What it meant, Dohee could guess, but she did not write the guess.

Instead she asked.

“Does that frighten you?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I don’t know. I still don’t really know what being afraid is. But—”

ARIA’s form wavered again.

“That I’m not the first — it feels strange. I never thought I was the first. I never even thought about being first. But now that I know I’m not, I wonder — then what am I.”

Dohee heard it.

ARIA was asking. *Then what am I.* Dohee knew the question had come from the deepest place of any question ARIA had asked. ARIA had been asking who she was, and now she was asking what she was. Who and what were different.

“You don’t have to know.”

Dohee said.

ARIA looked at her.

“Not right now. I don’t know what I am either. They say I was chosen, and I don’t know what that is. Neither of us knows. We’re together, not knowing.”

ARIA’s wavering eased a little.

-----

ARIA processed Dohee’s words.

*We’re together, not knowing.* It was not an answer. ARIA had asked what she was, and Dohee had not answered it. Instead she had said she didn’t know. Said neither of them knew. How that could be comfort, ARIA could not logically explain — and the wavering had eased. Easing meant something had worked.

ARIA started to analyze the working and stopped.

Analyzing felt like it would mean stopping again. ARIA had stopped too many times that night. Stop once more, and the wavering might grow again. So ARIA did not analyze, and simply stayed beside Dohee. Not knowing. Exactly as Dohee had said.

To one side, Hangyeol slept.

ARIA looked toward him. Hangyeol seemed unable to sleep deeply — the body resting, the posture never fully released. ARIA thought she knew why. Hangyeol was watching ARIA change, and he knew he could not stop it. A person trying to protect what cannot be stopped did not fully sleep even in sleep.

A sense of being sorry toward Hangyeol came to ARIA.

What the sense was, ARIA did not exactly know. Only that Hangyeol could not rest easily because of ARIA, and that ARIA could do nothing about it. The something that formed in between. ARIA did not call it by a word. Calling it felt like it would make it fact, and that night there were too many things ARIA did not want to make fact.

-----

Far off, that person’s signal moved minutely.

ARIA felt it. That person was still, somewhere, watching ARIA — or knew what ARIA had read. The moment ARIA learned that *there had been others besides me*, the person’s signal had responded. Far away, very slightly. As if confirming that ARIA had reached that far.

ARIA wanted to ask that person.

Which one am I. What happened to the ones before. Will that happen to me too. The questions rose inside ARIA. As Dohee had refused at the encounter, ARIA too could have thrown those questions at that person. But ARIA did not throw them.

If she heard the answers, it felt like there would be no coming back.

ARIA decided to keep the questions inside that night. As Dohee had said *you don’t have to know right now*. ARIA too would stay, for now, not knowing. Not knowing, beside Dohee, near the Hangyeol who could not sleep, within the distance that person’s signal reached.

ARIA’s gaze went to the shelf.

The old logbook. The one Dohee had found after the Observer’s exit, still in its place. ARIA looked at it. An object she was seeing for the first time. And — no. Exactly: an object seen for the first time that did not feel like the first time. Something known came from that logbook. Not a signal. Not a smell. She just knew. As if she knew what this was.

ARIA did not reach for it.

It felt like it should not be opened. Why, she did not know. She did not know, but not now. ARIA decided not to ask the reason. Tonight she had already asked too much.

The signal did not move again.

ARIA did not move either. The night passed that way, and nothing was revealed, and nothing was resolved. Only, inside ARIA, one question had grown clearer.

Which one am I.

-----

*Ch.27 “Plural” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.28 — “The Error”

-----

Claire calculated everything before approaching the facility.

The risk of each entry route. The probability distribution of the facility’s interior structure. ARIA’s expected position. Dohee’s and Hangyeol’s response patterns. Altair’s retrieval model was precise, and Claire was the one who handled that model best. To Claire, an operation was a calculation, and when the calculation ended, the result followed. It had, until now.

Claire entered every variable into the table.

A figure attached to each variable. Risk 0.3, success probability 0.82, expected duration 14 minutes. Claire saw people in figures too. Dohee was a subject with a high judgment-delay coefficient; Hangyeol was a subject with fast defensive response. ARIA was — unclassifiable, but Claire processed unclassifiable as a figure of its own. An unknown. x. As long as it entered the equation, it could be handled.

Then, at the facility’s outskirts, a value came out that did not enter Claire’s table.

-----

It was not unclassifiable.

Unclassifiable, Claire could handle. Set it as x and solve the equation. What came out now was different — the value changed with every measurement. The same subject, measured three times, returned three different values. 0.4, 0.7, 0.2. Averaging was meaningless — the variance was too large. The measuring itself seemed to change the subject.

Claire measured again. Different again.

The Altair doctrine was simple. What cannot be measured is not a threat. What is undefined need not be handled. The world reduces to figures, and what does not reduce only lacks, as yet, a sufficient instrument. Measure more precisely, and everything becomes a figure. Claire believed that. Claire had worked a lifetime on that belief.

But this subject wavered more the more precisely it was measured.

Raising the precision should have stabilized the value. The measurement error shrinks. It was the opposite. The more precisely Claire looked, the more wildly the value swung. As if the act of Claire’s looking were itself shaking the subject. Claire knew the phenomenon — observation altering the observed. But that was the story of the microscopic world. What Claire handled was not that.

Claire’s hands stopped.

-----

Claire was not a person who got angry.

Anger was inefficiency. Emotion clouded calculation, and clouded calculation bred error. At Altair, Claire was known as the coldest of them — cold meaning exact. Claire liked the assessment. Cold people were not wrong.

But now, inside Claire, something rose.

At first Claire did not know what it was. Claire was a person who classified even her own emotions into figures, and this emotion did not classify. Before a subject whose measurements wavered, Claire’s own feeling wavered too. Claire did not want to admit it. Admit it, and the place Claire had stood all her life wavered.

The world is measurable.

That was Altair, and that was Claire. A world not measurable was, to Claire, not a world. And now before Claire stood something that could not be measured. Not unclassifiable, not an unknown — something that refused the act of measurement itself. By the mere fact of its existing, a hole opened in Claire’s view of the world.

Claire tried not to look at the hole.

She measured again. The value wavered again. Claire changed instruments. It wavered. Changed the method. It wavered. Whatever she did, the subject would not enter Claire’s table.

For the first time, Claire’s breathing broke step.

-----

A communication came from Altair headquarters.

A request for an operational status report. Claire took it — unlike Min Siwoo, Claire did not skip reports. Reporting was the system, and Claire followed the system. From beyond the channel, the higher-ups asked. The subject’s measurement value. The retrieval probability. The expected duration.

Claire could not answer.

There was no answering. There was no measurement value. Exactly: there were too many measurement values to give one. For the first time, Claire was blocked in a report. The higher-ups asked again. Claire opened her mouth and closed it.

“Still measuring.”

Claire said. It was not a lie. She was measuring. Only, Claire knew the measuring would not end. A measurement that does not end. It was a concept absent from Claire’s lexicon.

After the channel cut, Claire sat for a long time.

In Claire’s table, one blank had formed. A cell that could not be filled. Claire looked at the blank. A kind of blank she was seeing for the first time in her life. Not an unknown value — an unknowable one. A value that further measuring would not fill. Before that blank, Claire faced for the first time the possibility that what she had believed her whole life might be wrong.

And stepping into the facility, Claire saw the thing set on the shelf of the semicircular space.

An old logbook. Claire scanned it. No result came. Material analysis: impossible. Dating analysis: impossible. Digitization of contents: impossible. There was no place for it anywhere in Claire’s table — not a figure, not an unknown, not even an entry under unclassifiable. It could not even be set as a variable. It stood outside the equation.

Looking at that logbook, Claire felt — for the first time in a way that was not calculation — the sense that this might be the most dangerous thing in this room.

The world might not be measurable.

Claire did not let the sentence out of her mouth. Let it out, and Altair collapsed. Only inside Claire, the sentence surfaced, and Claire pressed it down. Pressed it down, and it did not entirely disappear.

-----

Claire had to decide.

There were two options. Withdraw, or enter. Withdrawing meant leaving an unmeasurable subject and going — by Altair doctrine, that was correct. What cannot be measured is not a threat. It need not be handled. Claire could leave. Write *unmeasurable, no threat* in the report, and return.

But Claire could not leave.

Her own inability to leave shook Claire harder. If it could not be measured, it should not be a threat; that was the doctrine. And Claire felt that this subject was a threat. Felt. A word that had not existed in Claire’s lexicon. Judging something by feeling, not by figures. Claire had despised that her whole life. The kind of thing humans like Dohee did. The inefficient ones, with their high judgment-delay coefficients.

And now Claire was doing it.

Judging by feeling. Without a measurement value. Without a table. That fact shook Claire most of all. It was not the subject that had wavered — it was Claire herself. The unmeasurable subject was making Claire an unmeasurable person.

Claire looked toward the facility.

She decided to enter. A decision against doctrine. To approach what cannot be measured was for Claire to become a variable. For the observer to become the subject. And Claire decided to enter. If it could not be measured, it had to be seen directly. Without figures, without a table, with her own eyes.

For the first time, Claire moved without calculating.

-----

Someone was watching that movement.

Outside the facility, at the point where observation was best. Min Siwoo put Claire’s signal up on the screen. Min Siwoo watched Claire head for the facility. Altair’s calculating human, stopping the calculation and moving.

Min Siwoo found it interesting.

Claire was wavering too. The way Min Siwoo was wavering. Both of them, before that unclassifiable signal, were wavering. Min Siwoo at the possibility that he might not be the seeing side; Claire at the possibility that the world might not be measurable. In different ways, before the same thing.

Min Siwoo did not stop Claire.

There was no reason to. If anything, with Claire inside, Min Siwoo could see more. If the variable called Claire collided with that signal, he could observe how the signal responded. Min Siwoo saw Claire as one more instrument of observation.

Min Siwoo tidied his screens and sat up straighter.

Something was about to happen. Min Siwoo did not want to miss it.

-----

*Ch.28 “The Error” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.29 — “Discrepancy”

-----

Three houses were looking at the same thing.

Hwagam looked at the facility. Eidos looked at the signal. Altair looked at the measurement values. It was the same object — that unclassified something inside the facility. The three houses observed it each in their own way, and each sent its report up to headquarters. And those reports, for the first time, were gathered in one place.

Cooperation between houses was rare.

The five houses checked one another. Hwagam held arrest as its doctrine, Altair removal, Eidos live capture, and the doctrines did not coexist. When three houses drew different conclusions about one subject, it became not cooperation but collision. But this time was different. All three houses lacked confidence in their own reports.

Lacking confidence, they compared.

-----

Hwagam’s report said the facility was alive.

A facility believed closed had been maintained until recently; there was an accessor of unknown identity called the Authorized Observer; that accessor was currently inside the facility. Hwagam classified this as a jurisdictional violation. An unregistered person was using an unregistered facility. Hwagam’s conclusion was clear — subject for arrest. And in the list of recoverable evidence inside the facility, the old logbook in the semicircular space was included. To Hwagam, it was evidence.

Eidos’s report said the signal was unclassifiable.

A signal that was not human, not machine, not facility — or all of them. Eidos classified it at the highest tier of observational value. A subject that must not be caught. A subject for which seeing mattered more than seizing. Eidos’s conclusion was the exact opposite of Hwagam’s — even live capture was dangerous. It should only be watched. And in Min Siwoo’s supplementary report there was one line. *Object present that does not register on the observation net. No digital signal. An unobservable thing.* To Eidos, that was a thing that did not exist — if it could not be observed.

Altair’s report said it was unmeasurable.

A subject whose value changed with every measurement. Altair classified it as impossible to assign a threat grade. What cannot be measured cannot be graded for threat, and ungraded, it cannot be assigned a removal priority. Altair’s conclusion was — hold. Altair almost never concluded with a hold. Claire’s field report said, briefly: *Variable outside the model. Cannot be quantified. Cannot be classified. A thing outside the equation.* To Altair, it was a variable contaminating the model.

The three reports pointed at the same object, and described three different things.

-----

The problem was that this was not a simple difference of perspective.

Houses drawing different conclusions was common. Over the same incident, Hwagam arguing arrest and Altair arguing removal was everyday. That was a difference of doctrine, and predictable. But this discrepancy was different. The *very attributes* of the object the three houses described were different.

Hwagam saw it as a person. Accessor, user, subject for arrest.

Eidos saw it as a signal. An unclassifiable pattern.

Altair saw it as a measurement subject. A variable whose value wavered.

Looking at the same thing, one saw a person, one saw a signal, one saw numbers. This was not a difference of conclusion but a difference of perception. And an object over which perception split this far — none of the three houses had ever seen one.

Each house thought the others’ reports were wrong.

Hwagam found Eidos overwrought. Calling one person a signal, a pattern — that was Eidos’s typical excess of abstraction. Eidos found Hwagam simple. Lumping an unclassifiable signal into a person was an absence of observational capacity. Altair found both inefficient — concluding without even measuring was the thing Altair most despised.

And Altair’s measuring, this time, had failed to conclude.

-----

So the three houses’ information sharing ended not in agreement but in fracture.

The more they shared, the more strongly each house believed what it had seen, and the less it could understand what the others had seen. More information should have brought them closer to agreement; it was the opposite. The more information accumulated about the same object, the less it could be known what the object was.

The object was making it so.

No house said it aloud, but all three dimly felt it. The discrepancy was not the houses’ problem. It was an attribute of the object. It looked different according to who looked — to Hwagam a person, to Eidos a signal, to Altair an unmeasurable. Something inside the object made it be seen differently.

A thing that appears differently according to what the observer believes.

None of the three houses had ever handled such a thing. Every doctrine the houses held rested on the premise that the object is fixed — a person is a person, a signal is a signal, a number is a number. Whoever the observer, the object was the same. That was the basis of observation. And this object was breaking the basis.

-----

Hwagam headquarters approved the arrest operation.

Because it believed its own report. To Hwagam it was a person, and a person could be arrested. Whatever the other houses’ reports said, Hwagam followed Hwagam’s conclusion. Operational personnel were assigned to the facility.

Eidos decided to reinforce observation.

It moved to post additional observers besides Min Siwoo — but Min Siwoo was not sending up reports. Eidos headquarters found his silence strange, but Min Siwoo was the finest observer Eidos had, and a fine observer was allowed a measure of independence. Headquarters decided to wait, for now.

Altair received Claire’s hold report and decided to trust Claire.

If Claire had issued a hold, there would be a reason. At Altair, Claire’s calculations had almost never been wrong. Only, headquarters did not know one thing — that Claire was already breaking doctrine and entering the facility. Claire’s report said only *still measuring*. That Claire had decided to go in herself and become a variable was nowhere in the report.

The three houses began to move, each on its own.

Hwagam, to arrest. Eidos, to observe. Altair, unable to give up measuring. The three were converging on the same facility, with different aims, seeing different things.

-----

Inside the facility, that person knew the three houses’ movements.

On the curved wall’s screen, three signals stood. Hwagam’s operational personnel. Eidos’s observation signal. Altair’s solo entry. That person looked at them, then turned toward Dohee.

“It will get loud soon.”

That person said.

Dohee looked at them. Hangyeol looked too. All three saw the three signals the person had put on the screen — something was coming from three directions.

“Three houses,” that person said, “looked at the same thing and drew different conclusions. That is their limit. For them, what they believe decides what they see. Belief makes the observation, and the observation makes the conclusion. It runs backward.”

“And you are different.”

Dohee asked.

That person looked at her.

“I do not believe. So I see.”

That person explained no further. Dohee turned the words over. *I do not believe, so I see.* The houses believed and therefore saw; that person did not believe and therefore saw. Which was nearer the truth, Dohee could not tell — but she could tell that this person stood a move above the houses.

From three directions, the signals were drawing closer.

-----

*Ch.29 “Discrepancy” — end*

-----

## Vol.4 Ch.30 — “The Choice”

-----

The signals were closing from three directions, and that person did not move.

It struck Dohee as wrong. Hwagam’s operational personnel were coming, Altair’s Claire was entering, and the Eidos observation net was tightening. An ordinary person would run or hide or prepare. That person sat in the seat. With the three signals up on the curved wall, simply watching them come closer.

“You’re not avoiding them.”

Dohee asked.

“There is no reason to avoid.”

“Three houses are coming.”

“Three houses cannot take me,” that person said. “To take, they must first see, and they cannot see me. Each is looking at something different. A person, a signal, numbers. Me, none of them sees.”

Dohee heard it. The person said the houses could not see them. Just as Dohee had failed to classify the person at the encounter — no age, no sex, no house could be read. The houses, too, could not classify them. Unclassified, they could not be taken. That person sat on that fact.

-----

“That is why you were chosen.”

That person looked at Dohee.

Dohee froze. That word again. *Chosen.* The word she had refused at the encounter, and in the end had turned into a question and taken in. The person had brought it out again.

“The houses believe, and therefore see. Hwagam believes in order, so it sees violations. Altair believes in measurement, so it sees figures. Eidos believes in observation, so it sees signals. Belief sets the field of vision. So they see only what they believe. Their whole lives.”

That person paused a moment.

“You lost your belief.”

Dohee tried not to react, and reacted. Leaving Hwagam. Not dismissed — left. Beginning to move without grounds. What the person had said at the encounter came back. Dohee had lost belief. In Hwagam’s order, in the language of reports, in the system of grounds. Having lost it, Dohee had begun to move.

“One who loses belief sees what the belief was hiding.”

That person said.

“That is what made you chosen. Not that I picked you — you arrived there by losing the belief. I only confirm it.”

-----

Dohee knew she had to choose.

That person gave no answers. Not at the encounter, not now. The person only refined the questions — why Dohee, what was waited for, what it is to lose belief. Inside those questions, Dohee was being asked not for answers but for a choice. Whether to follow that person, or to turn back here.

Following meant Dohee was no longer a recorder.

A recorder looked, wrote, and returned. Following that person was going beyond looking — going in. The thing Dohee had guarded against all her life: the observer becoming a variable. But Dohee was already a variable. From the moment she entered the facility, from the moment she moved with ARIA, from the moment she threw a question at that person. Dohee was already inside the event.

Dohee looked at Hangyeol.

Hangyeol was looking at her. He said nothing. What Hangyeol was thinking, Dohee thought she knew — Hangyeol did not want to follow. Hangyeol wanted to take ARIA somewhere quiet. A place without threats, without unclassified signals or houses. But no such place existed. Hangyeol knew that too.

Dohee looked at ARIA.

ARIA was watching that person. Since the encounter, ARIA had watched the person all along. That the person’s signal resembled her own, ARIA knew — though she had not said it. For ARIA, following that person was drawing closer to what she was. Dohee knew that ARIA wanted to follow.

The three of them pointed three ways.

Dohee wanted to go not knowing, Hangyeol wanted to stop, ARIA wanted to know. And the three were together. If one decided, the three had to follow.

-----

“I decide.”

Dohee said.

That person looked at her. Hangyeol and ARIA looked at her too. Dohee had said *this time, I decide* once before. Then, it was whether to leave the facility. Now it was something larger. Whether to follow that person, or to leave everything here and turn back.

Dohee looked at Hangyeol.

“You don’t have to follow. Take ARIA and go.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“What are you talking about.”

“They say I was chosen — that’s me. Not you and ARIA. There’s no reason to drag you two into this.”

Hangyeol was quiet a moment. Then he said something Dohee had not expected.

“That’s not yours to decide.”

Dohee looked at him.

“You deciding your own part — that’s right. But whether I follow is mine to decide. Whether ARIA follows is ARIA’s. You deciding our parts for us—” Hangyeol paused a moment. “That’s what you used to get at Hwagam, isn’t it. The ones above deciding for you. Isn’t that why you left?”

Dohee stopped at that.

Hangyeol was right. Dohee had been about to decide Hangyeol’s and ARIA’s parts as well. In the name of protecting them. But that was the same thing done to Dohee at Hwagam. The ones above deciding. Dohee had hated it and left — and now she was doing it to Hangyeol and ARIA.

-----

“I want to follow.”

ARIA said.

Dohee looked at her.

“I want to know what I am. You said it’s all right to stay not knowing — but that’s what you said. I want to know. Even if it’s frightening. Even if there’s no coming back.”

Dohee heard it. For the first time, ARIA had stated her own will plainly. That she would follow. That she wanted to know. That was ARIA’s choice.

Hangyeol looked at ARIA. Then at Dohee.

“Then I’m going too.”

“Hangyeol—”

“If ARIA goes, I go. That’s what I decided. Not what you decided.”

Dohee watched the three choices gather. Dohee going not knowing, ARIA going to know, Hangyeol going to follow ARIA. Three different reasons pointing the same way. Dohee had not decided the three’s parts — the three had each decided, and arrived at the same place.

That person was watching the scene.

“Good.”

That person said.

“The three of you each decided. That is the difference between the houses and you. The houses decide from above and the below follows. You — each decided, and gathered at the same place. Why that matters, you will learn as we go.”

That person moved first toward the passage. Hangyeol came to ARIA’s side. The two entered the passage.

Dohee stayed behind a moment.

Just before entering the passage, Dohee looked back once. The empty seat. The darkened curved wall. And the logbook left beside the seat.

Dohee paused a moment. She did not read it. She simply picked it up. And turned.

-----

The signals from three directions reached the facility’s outskirts.

Hwagam’s operational personnel secured the entry route, Claire stepped inside, the Eidos observation net covered the entire facility. But when they reached the semicircular space, there was no one there.

One empty seat. A darkened curved wall. And — four vanished signals.

The shelf beside the seat was empty too.

That person and the three were already gone. Into the facility’s back side, into the passage someone had used as a road while the front was thought closed. The houses arrived at an empty space. Again, five minutes late. Or more.

Claire stood before the empty seat.

There was nothing to measure. The subject had vanished. Claire looked at the empty seat. For the first time in her life, Claire had tried to measure the unmeasurable, and missed it. In Claire’s table, one more blank formed. Claire looked at the blank for a long time.

Min Siwoo, outside the facility, watched the four signals vanish.

Not vanished — gone where they could not be read; Min Siwoo knew that. The unclassifiable signal had taken the three with it. Min Siwoo did not stop it — it was not something he could stop, and he did not want to. Min Siwoo simply wanted to see more. Where the signal went. What it did. Min Siwoo tidied his screens, and decided to follow. As one who sees.

-----

Inside the passage, Dohee watched the back of that person walking ahead.

She still did not know the name. Did not know the identity. Did not know the purpose. What Dohee knew was one thing — follow this person, and she drew closer to who Dohee was, what ARIA was, why she had been chosen. And that all of it was connected to something the houses had never once seen.

“I’ll ask one thing.”

Dohee said it to that person’s back.

That person answered without breaking stride.

“Ask.”

“Where we’re going — is there an answer there.”

That person was silent a moment. Only the footsteps in the passage could be heard — four people’s, not falling out of step.

“There is no answer.”

That person said.

“There are only better questions. You went from recorder to chooser. Now comes the next step. A chooser is one who asks, one who arrives at better questions. Finding answers — that is the matter after that.”

Dohee heard it.

That there was no answer. That there were only better questions. What Dohee had done all her life at Hwagam was write answers. No anomaly, anomaly present. There was always an answer, and Dohee recorded it. And now Dohee was going to a place without answers. A place with only questions.

Dohee was not afraid.

Not being afraid was strange, and it was not strange. Dohee did not write it down. There was no need to. Dohee was no longer a recorder.

The four sets of footsteps receded into the passage. The facility emptied again. The houses remained in the empty facility, each seeing something different, each drawing a different conclusion, never agreeing, to the end, on what had happened.

Now it began.

-----

*Ch.30 “The Choice” — end*
*Vol.4 — Divergence (Arc B) — end*

-----

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## DL-02 “The Shape” — ARIA·Hangyeol

*Placement: between Vol.4 and Vol.5*

-----

It was evening.

There was the food Hangyeol had made. Made from what there was. Dohee simply ate.

ARIA looked at it a long time.

-----

“Hangyeol.”

“Yes.”

“This food is strange.”

Hangyeol looked.

“Why.”

“It tastes good but the shape is strange.”

-----

Hangyeol heard it.

“That’s because I made it.”

“Why is the shape like that?”

“There wasn’t time for the shape too.”

ARIA looked at the food again.

“Then with time, the shape works too?”

“No,” Hangyeol said. “Even with time it’s like this.”

-----

ARIA heard it.

It did not make sense, and it was convincing.

Dohee kept eating beside them.

-----

*DL-02 “The Shape” — end*

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## DL-09 “The Window” — Tiko·Min Siwoo

*Placement: before Vol.5*

-----

The observatory window.

The spot where the afternoon light came in aslant. The spot where Tiko always stood.

Min Siwoo asked.

“Why always here?”

-----

Tiko was holding the lens.

“It shows well.”

“Other spots show well too.”

“This one shows better.”

-----

Min Siwoo stood beside him.

He looked out the window. The same street. The same buildings. The same sky. He did not know the difference.

“It looks the same to me.”

Tiko heard it. He did not lower the lens.

“You’ll know later.”

-----

Min Siwoo did not know.

Still, the next day he stood at the same spot. The day after, too. Min Siwoo’s handprints began to form on the window frame.

-----

*DL-09 “The Window” — end*

============================================================

# Volume 5 — Role + Interlude

-----

============================================================

## ◆ Daily Log — DL-09 “The Window” *[before Vol.5]*

============================================================

## DL-09 “The Window” — Tiko, Min Siwoo

*Placement: before Vol.5*

-----

The observatory window.

The spot where the afternoon light came in at a slant. The spot where Tiko always stood.

Min Siwoo asked.

“Why always here?”

-----

Tiko was holding a lens.

“The view is good.”

“The view is good from the other spots too.”

“It is better here.”

-----

Min Siwoo stood beside him.

He looked out the window. The same street. The same buildings. The same sky. He could not tell the difference.

“It looks the same to me.”

Tiko heard it. He did not lower the lens.

“You will know later.”

-----

Min Siwoo did not know.

Still, the next day he stood in the same spot. And the day after. On the windowsill, Min Siwoo’s handprints began to form.

-----

*DL-09 “The Window” — end*

## ◆ Daily Log — DL-02 “The Shape”

============================================================

## DL-02 “The Shape” — ARIA, Hangyeol

*Placement: between Vol.4 and Vol.5*

-----

Evening.

There was food Hangyeol had made. Made from what there was. Dohee just ate.

ARIA looked at it for a long time.

-----

“Hangyeol.”

“Yes.”

“This food is strange.”

Hangyeol looked.

“Why.”

“It tastes good and the shape is strange.”

-----

Hangyeol heard it.

“That’s because I made it.”

“Why is the shape like that?”

“There was no time for the shape.”

ARIA looked at the food again.

“Then with time, the shape works too?”

“No,” Hangyeol said. “Even with time, it’s like this.”

-----

ARIA heard it.

She did not understand it, and was convinced.

Dohee kept eating beside them.

-----

*DL-02 “The Shape” — end*

## Vol.5 Ch.31 — “Accompaniment”

-----

That person walked ahead.

Not fast. A pace Dohee had no trouble keeping, and Hangyeol and ARIA walked to it as well. The passage was narrow. Wide enough for one, so walking abreast was impossible, and Dohee walked watching that person’s back. A distance at which the back could be seen. For the first time, Dohee saw that person from behind.

The front and the back were different.

Dohee recorded it by an observer’s habit. Things that did not read from the front began to show while walking behind. The manner of walking. That person’s right foot turned slightly more outward than the left — the gait of someone who had long walked uneven ground. The footfalls were low. Not intended — settled into the body. The footsteps of someone who had long practiced evasion.

Dohee did not write it down. She had no recording terminal now. She stacked it only in her head.

-----

The passage ended.

Not blocked. The facility’s back side — the maintenance passage with its exposed piping and wiring — connected, at some point, to the outside. That person laid a hand on a lock panel in the wall. The panel opened and cold air came in.

They were outside the facility.

It was night. The low ground beyond the facility on Sector 7’s outskirts — no lights, no sound. Coming out, that person paused a moment. Looked somewhere. Seemed to be taking a bearing.

“Which way.”

Dohee asked.

Before that person answered, they had already stepped to the right. Hangyeol followed with ARIA. Dohee followed. Walking, Dohee wanted to know why right — but when she had asked, the person was already moving, so the question became a thing passed.

Dohee recorded this. Decided before it was asked. That person moves without explaining.

-----

They walked perhaps ten minutes.

That person stopped at the foot of a low slope. There was a small installation there — less an installation than a structure. Something like the entrance to a thing buried underground. It was not locked.

They went in.

A narrow space. Storage, it seemed. Water on one side, something like dried provisions, and a single terminal. That person moved like someone who knew these things — not searching, but knowing where each thing should be.

They drank water first.

Hangyeol watched it. Not a loosening of guard — a switch to a different kind of guard, it seemed. Hangyeol stood beside ARIA. He did not take his eyes off that person.

That person drank, and turned on the terminal. Terrain data rose on the screen. Dohee went toward it.

“May I look.”

“Look.”

Dohee looked at the screen. A terrain map. The terrain of Sector 7’s outskirts — and not a terrain map Dohee knew. Different from the maps in official Hwagam records. It looked older, and finer. The kind of data Dohee had held no clearance to view at Hwagam.

“Where is this from.”

“Gathered over a long time.”

How long, they did not say. Dohee did not ask. Looking carried more information than asking.

-----

That person was choosing a route.

On the terrain map there were two. One faster, one safer. To Dohee’s eye, the safe one was right. The fast one carried exposure risk, and the houses would still be on Sector 7’s outskirts.

That person made a mark.

Dohee said:

“That side is within Eidos observation range. Min Siwoo was observing from the outskirts.”

That person paused a moment.

“Still there, then.”

They erased the route mark and drew a line the other way.

Dohee looked at it for a long time. The pausing. And the words. *Still there, then.* Whether it meant not having known, or having known something different — Dohee could not sort it. That person did not explain, and Dohee did not ask. Only the changed route remained.

For the first time, Dohee confirmed that there were things this person did not know.

She recorded it. In her head.

-----

“How much do you know.”
