Dohee said.

That person looked at her from the screen.

“Not everything.”

“How much.”

“As much as is needed.”

“Has that need ever been wrong.”

That person did not answer. Dohee waited out the silence. At Hwagam, Dohee had learned waiting — wait, and even silence became an answer.

“It has.”

That person said.

“Often.”

“Not often.”

“But it has.”

“Yes.”

Dohee recorded the exchange. It has been wrong. Not often, but it has. That meant not omniscient. A person who knew more, who had watched longer — but did not know everything. When Dohee had named Min Siwoo’s position, the person had paused, and that was the reason.

Hangyeol was listening to the exchange. He did not look at Dohee. Beside ARIA, he watched the screen.

-----

ARIA looked at the logbook.

Dohee was holding it. The thing taken from the passage. ARIA looked at it — then looked toward that person. That person looked at the logbook too.

That person’s gaze stayed on the logbook a little longer than usual. Different from how they looked at Dohee. Different from how they looked at Hangyeol. Then it returned to the screen.

Nothing was said.

“I didn’t think you’d take it.”

That person said. Low. Watching the screen.

Dohee looked at them.

“Should I have left it.”

“I did not say you shouldn’t have. I said I didn’t think you would.”

“What’s the difference.”

“The former is your error. The latter is my failure to predict.”

Dohee heard it. That person had not predicted it. That Dohee would take the logbook. The person who had led, waited, stood ahead of the three — had not known that this logbook would end up in Dohee’s hand.

ARIA’s form wavered minutely.

That person saw the wavering. Dohee saw it. Hangyeol saw it. All three noticed, and no one spoke.

-----

The night was deepening.

That person finalized the route on the terrain map. Movement at dawn. Looking at the route, Dohee started to ask where it led and stopped. Ask, and they would answer; answered, the next question would form. What was needed now was not the route’s destination but the route itself. There were things one knew only on arriving.

Hangyeol said to that person:

“And if we run into them moving.”

“We avoid.”

“And if we can’t avoid.”

“I am not seen.”

“And us.”

That person looked at Hangyeol.

“That is yours to decide.”

Hangyeol did not answer. That person turned back to the screen. Hangyeol seemed to be digesting the exchange. *I am not seen* — that this person knew how to hide. And had said: you decide for yourselves. Had not offered to hide them. Had said they would go together; had not said they would be protected.

Hangyeol understood the difference.

ARIA came closer to Hangyeol’s side. Hangyeol noticed, and lowered his eyes.

-----

Outside the facility, on the outskirts.

Min Siwoo did not move.

Since the four signals vanished, Min Siwoo had held his position on the outskirts. There were no signals on the screen. The whole area was quiet. The unclassifiable signal he had been tracking, ARIA’s signal, Dohee’s and Hangyeol’s — all of them, gone.

Another observer would have moved. No signal, and you widen the tracking range, change the search pattern, try another direction. Min Siwoo did not.

Min Siwoo waited.

No signal meant not vanished but blocked. A vanishing signal and a hiding signal were different. Vanish, and a last point remains; hide, and the pattern cuts. On Min Siwoo’s screen now there was a point where the pattern cut. A cut point existing meant someone knew how to cut it. Knowing how to cut meant knowing how to reappear.

Min Siwoo looked at the cut point.

Position, time, angle. Min Siwoo did not process them separately. He read them at once. From the position where the signal was last detected, from the time it cut, from ARIA’s last direction of movement. Combine the three, and the signal had not vanished — it had moved. There was a direction of movement. A distance could be estimated.

Min Siwoo’s fingers moved over the screen.

A private analysis that would not go up to Eidos headquarters. Min Siwoo had no intention of sharing it. Share it, and other observers attached; more observers, and the unclassifiable signal changed. Min Siwoo did not want it to change.

A single point was marked on the screen.

At a place with no signal. The endpoint of the route Min Siwoo had calculated. There were three possibilities. The first was north, the second the eastern lowland, the third the direction of the buried structure on the facility’s outskirts. Min Siwoo chose the third. An unconfirmed position. The grounds were calculation, and there was no guarantee the calculation was right.

Min Siwoo looked at the point. For a long time.

He decided not to go yet. He was the seeing side. But if the point was right — tomorrow, Min Siwoo would know.

-----

The same hour, in the facility.

Claire was walking the empty space.

The place the houses had entered. After Hwagam’s operational personnel finished the search and left, after the Eidos observation team packed out its equipment, Claire remained alone. Altair headquarters requested her withdrawal. Claire did not answer.

She entered the semicircular space.

There was the empty seat. The shelf beside it was bare. Claire looked at it. She recorded that the shelf was bare — until a while ago, something had been there. Something her scan had not caught. Now it was gone. Someone had taken it.

Claire looked at the passage traces.

The corridor floor, the semicircular space’s wall, the maintenance passage entrance. Claire read the traces in turn. Dust patterns, wear marks, the contact heat on the lock panel. Each alone was nothing. Claire processed them simultaneously.

Four people had been in this space. One of them was unclassifiable. There was a direction of movement. A time of movement could be estimated.

In Claire’s head, the terrain reassembled.

Not the official map. A back-calculation from the data Claire had gathered in the field these past days — Eidos observation patterns, the movement routes of Hwagam’s operational personnel, Altair’s own grid structure. Which direction was empty now. Which route ran where no one was looking.

One direction came out.

Claire looked at the direction. The probability of movement that way was highest. Claire did not write it in the report. Send it up to Altair, and other personnel would block that direction. Blocked, the data Claire could gather in the field disappeared.

Claire still had calculations to run.

That subject whose measurements wavered — the thing outside Claire’s table. Claire wanted to know where it had gone. Not to take it. To understand it. To prove again that the world is measurable. Or — to confirm whether the belief was wrong.

Leaving the semicircular space, Claire looked at the empty seat once more.

No measurement value. No scan. No place in Claire’s table. And it had been there. The seat someone had sat in. It had clearly been there.

Claire left without touching the seat.

-----

Before dawn came, Dohee took out the logbook.

She did not open it. She looked at the cover. Nothing was written on it. It was old, and the manner of its oldness was different — worn not as a thing long stored but as a thing long used. Where the hand had often held it, the coating was rubbed away.

That person spoke beside her.

“You’re not opening it.”

“Not yet.”

“Why.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“If I open it now, I don’t think I can come back to before I opened it.”

That person looked at her. Said nothing, neither denied nor agreed. From the silence, Dohee knew she was right.

She put the logbook away again.

Hangyeol saw it. That Dohee did not open the logbook. Why, he did not know. He knew only that Dohee had not opened it, and that that person had said nothing.

ARIA was looking toward the logbook. Looking that way, doing nothing. As if she could not.

Dawn was nearing.

The movement had not yet begun.

-----

*Ch.31 “Accompaniment” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.32 — “Pursuit”

-----

They moved before dawn came.

That person went out first. Dohee followed, Hangyeol and ARIA behind her. The route fixed on the terrain map — one of the two remaining candidates. Dohee had judged the other one safer, and that person chose this one. She did not ask why. There were things one learned walking.

Descending into the lowland, that person stopped.

Dohee watched their back. That person spoke without turning around.

“Footprints.”

Dohee looked down. Wet earth. Footprints had pressed in. That person stepped up onto the dry grass of the side slope and pointed Dohee the same way. Dohee followed. Hangyeol took ARIA up the same line.

Dohee checked where that person had walked. There were no footprints.

Dohee looked at it. Her own pressed footprints had disappeared. When they had been erased, Dohee had not seen. Handled while moving, simultaneously — without looking back, without slowing the stride. Settled into the body. The way of a person who had done it a long time.

Dohee recorded it. A philosopher does not walk like this.

-----

The second stop was near a building’s ruins.

An old structure. There was a collapsed outer wall, and that person changed direction there briefly. Instead of passing straight through, they passed behind the rubble. Dohee swept the surroundings. It was a blind angle. Seen from the Eidos observation positions, this angle was screened by the rubble. One had to know the rubble was there to choose this route, and one had to know the Eidos observation patterns to know which angle was blind.

Hangyeol said, low:

“That person’s been here before.”

It was not a question.

Dohee thought so too. They had said the terrain data was gathered over a long time — not terrain data: routes. Where one is seen and where one is not, where footprints remain and where they don’t. These were things not on any map. One had to walk them to know. Walk them many times for them to settle into the body.

Dohee looked at that person’s back again.

It was the back of someone who had survived. Not the back of someone with a philosophy — the back of someone who had needed, for a long time, to survive. Dohee learned the difference walking. It was not taught. It was shown.

Entering the lowland, that person took the right-hand slope. Farther than the left.

ARIA said:

“The left is faster.”

That person paused a moment.

“What’s on the left?”

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But it’s faster.”

That person looked at the left slope, then turned their steps left. Abandoned the right-hand route. Did not explain. Whether they had judged ARIA right, or the right-hand route failed for another reason — Dohee could not tell.

Dohee recorded one thing. That person, too, takes from ARIA what they did not ask for.

-----

On the facility’s outskirts, Min Siwoo had held his position through the night.

There was no signal. From last night until now, all four signals absent. Confirming it, Min Siwoo understood — the absence was giving him more information. When you don’t know where something is, where it isn’t becomes what is.

Min Siwoo put the whole of Sector 7’s outskirts on the screen.

He erased, one by one, the zones without signal. Zones where the Eidos net was alive should have shown a signal. None meant outside the net. Where outside the net was — Min Siwoo knew the Eidos observation deployment. He overlaid it. A blank appeared.

He looked at the terrain. The terrain of the blank zone.

Lowland, dense rubble, areas marked with underground structures. Where the three conditions overlapped was the candidate. The point Min Siwoo had marked last night was there.

Only, the direction of movement came after that point.

They would not stop at that point — Min Siwoo judged that person was not a being who stopped. A person who kept moving. Then the question was where they went past that point.

Two candidates remained. One was the mountainous terrain to the northwest, one the eastern lowland corridor.

Min Siwoo erased the northwest.

There was one reason. ARIA does not choose that direction. It was the result of watching ARIA’s movement patterns for days. ARIA avoided closed terrain. Mountains close. It was an intuition he could not explain, and Min Siwoo called it not intuition but pattern.

Only the east remained.

That far, it could not yet be read.

That was what made Min Siwoo move.

He rose from his position. By watching alone, no more could be read. He had to get closer — not entering, but watching from a narrower range. Exactly the same words as the Eidos doctrine. Min Siwoo knew how to draw near without breaking the doctrine.

He folded the screens. Reduced his equipment to the minimum.

From the facility’s outskirts he set his bearing east. It was a moving of the watching place. If the way of watching changed, what was seen would change too.

-----

Claire was in the second zone.

All she had carried out of the semicircular space was measurement. The scan had failed, there were no figures, it stood outside Claire’s table. Still, there was something she had carried — coordinates. The semicircular space’s position, the entry angle of the maintenance passage, the direction in which the panel’s contact heat faded. There was a direction where the three overlapped.

Claire moved in that direction.

In the lowland, Claire looked at the ground. There was a dust pattern. Swept in one direction — movement. There were no footprints, and the dust was swept. Claire looked at it. The footprints had been erased. Erased, and the dust had not been. There was a processing priority — footprints first, dust after. The reason the after had gone unhandled was speed.

They had moved fast.

Claire calculated the speed. The spacing of the erased marks, the angle of the dust drift, the direction the dry grass was pressed. Process the three at once and a speed range came out. At that speed, they had passed this point before dawn.

Two to three hours ago.

Claire did not write it in the report. Report to Altair headquarters and interdiction personnel would move. Interdicted, the things Claire could see would disappear. Claire still had things to see.

She entered the rubble zone.

There Claire stopped. The rubble’s arrangement was odd. There was a direction of collapse, and the marks of passage detoured around the opposite side. There was a reason for the detour. Sightlines. From the detour route, Claire looked back the other way. From somewhere, this place is visible. The question was from where.

She looked at the terrain that could serve as an observation position.

There was a slope. From there, this place was visible. Claire recorded the slope’s coordinates.

Eidos would have been there. That was the reason this place had been avoided.

Knowing that, Claire looked at the route’s next stage. The direction after the detour ended. The route toward the lowland. Claire’s calculation converged.

East, 73 percent. Southeast, 21. Northeast, 6.

73 percent was enough. Claire did not wait for 100. 100 came only after arrival. Move at 73, correct if wrong, recalculate.

She began moving east.

-----

One of Hwagam’s operational personnel sent up a report.

Facility outskirts search complete. Subjects not found. Direction of flight: cannot be estimated.

A superior received the report. Read it. The gaze stopped on the entry: direction of flight cannot be estimated.

Hwagam’s method was clear. When a subject fled, you fixed the direction, followed the direction, and on reaching them, executed. Not knowing the direction meant — Hwagam’s method was not functioning.

The superior called another officer.

“Where are Eidos and Altair.”

“One Eidos observer has moved east from the facility outskirts. Claire of Altair is on the same heading.”

“Follow where those two are going.”

That was Hwagam’s judgment. When you do not know the direction, you follow the side that does. Eidos observed, Altair calculated. Hwagam decided to use their results.

Hwagam personnel began moving east.

-----

The same morning, the four moving ahead did not know it.

Dohee was walking beside ARIA. ARIA’s form was stable — she seemed to be reading the surroundings as she moved. Dohee watched it. The brief stop ARIA used to make on entering each new zone was gone. She just walked. The way she had in the facility.

That person slowed and came up beside Dohee.

Walking abreast was a first. There had only been ahead and behind, and now it was beside.

“You’ll have questions.”

That person said. Not asking — confirming.

“I do.”

“Which comes first.”

Dohee paused a moment.

She took out the logbook. Held it, still unopened.

“Why did you record.”

That person looked at the logbook.

“Because what is not recorded disappears.”

“What does.”

“The questions.”

That person paused, then added:

“Answers are remembered. Questions disappear.”

Dohee heard it. Not the answers — the questions disappear. Every record Dohee knew was the leaving of answers — Hwagam’s reports, the 7-7-4 file, the records of anomalous figures. Dohee had recorded the things that would become answers. This logbook was different. It was for not losing the questions.

“And the answers.”

“Those are elsewhere.”

Dohee did not ask where that pointed. If that answer came, this conversation ended. She did not want it to end.

She put the logbook away again.

-----

Still unopened.

Ahead, that person walked. Behind, three directions were narrowing onto the same heading. Min Siwoo for observation, Claire for understanding, Hwagam for execution.

The same direction.

Different reasons.

Dohee looked ahead. That person’s back. The back of someone who had survived a long time.

The movement went on. Behind them, three pursuits toward the same direction were beginning.

-----

*Ch.32 “Pursuit” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.33 — “The Role”

-----

The relay point was on no map.

Without that person’s guiding, it could not have been found. On the low side of the terrain, just below the point where the slope broke, there was a structure. From above it looked like part of the terrain; from the side it was buried in the slope and could not be seen. There was a way in, too — not from the front, but circling around to the rear. That person walked the detour as if drawing it from memory.

Inside, there were traces.

A place used often. Water was stored, dried provisions, the basic equipment needed for moving. That was all Dohee saw, and she could tell the equipment was old. Old, and kept in order. Someone had maintained this place for a long time.

Dohee watched that person check the things.

It was the manner of confirming that what should be there was there. Not searching — inspecting. Knowing this point and maintaining this point were different things — and that person was the latter.

-----

Hangyeol surveyed the interior.

Hangyeol was a person who read danger first. Where the exits were, where the hiding places were, where sound entered from. He looked at those first. Then at people.

He looked at that person.

For three years, Hangyeol had protected ARIA. When there was something ARIA did not know, Hangyeol stood in front. When danger came, Hangyeol took it first. That was Hangyeol’s way of protecting ARIA, and he had believed the way was right.

But now, watching that person, Hangyeol was thinking in a different mode.

Dangerous or not — that wasn’t it. What Hangyeol was looking at was something else. What kind of person this was. Not good or bad. Not that either. They seemed neither a good person nor a bad one. That person did not fit well into any classification.

A person with a purpose. What the purpose was, Hangyeol did not yet know. They did not seem to mean the three harm — and doing no harm and being a help were also different. That person was leading the three, but whether the direction of the leading was for the three or for that person’s own sake, Hangyeol could not tell.

It seemed a judgment he would have to make.

Until now, Hangyeol had not judged. If ARIA followed, Hangyeol followed. If Dohee decided, Hangyeol took the rear. But now — whether following this person was right, it seemed Hangyeol himself would have to judge it. Following without judging was the mode Hangyeol had once guarded against. Reacting without thinking. He had meant not to, and until now, that was what he had done.

-----

ARIA looked around the inside of the point.

An old place. ARIA felt it. Not a signal — something close to a person’s sense: many people had come to this place. Not only that person. Others had been here too. Long ago. And that person had come after them.

ARIA did not say it.

Say it, and she would want to confirm it. Confirm it, and she would come to know more. How much she should know now, ARIA still did not know. There was the sense of wanting to know more, and the sense that knowing more meant no coming back. The two were the same strength.

ARIA looked toward the logbook.

Dohee was holding it. Still unopened. ARIA half knew and could not know what the logbook was. She knew that opening it would change something. She knew there were things to learn before opening it. Only, what those things were did not yet exist.

-----

That night, Dohee asked a question.

It was after the others had settled. Hangyeol was by the entrance, ARIA toward the corner. Dohee sat beside that person.

“Are you the first.”

That person looked at her.

“The first what.”

“To do this. To hold this role.”

There was a pause.

“No.”

Dohee heard it. No — not the first. There had been someone before that person who did this. Someone who used this point. Someone who walked this route first.

“Then the one who was before you.”

That person paused a moment.

“That question is right.”

Right was not an answer. It meant the question itself faced the correct direction. Dohee understood the difference. That person gave no answer; they gave a direction.

Dohee asked no further. Ask more here and now — and they would not answer. The kinds of questions that person did not answer, Dohee was learning, little by little. For now, this was still that kind.

-----

ARIA spoke.

“Then the seat was there first.”

Dohee looked at ARIA. Hangyeol looked too.

That person looked at ARIA.

They said nothing. Neither denied nor agreed. For the first time, that person fell completely silent. Not the silence of choosing an answer — a silence as if what ARIA had said had touched something inside them.

The seat was there first.

Not the person — the seat. Not the role — the seat. ARIA had said it naturally — and Dohee did not fully understand what it meant. Hangyeol’s face said he didn’t either. Only ARIA seemed to know why she had said it.

That person looked at ARIA for a long time.

“Yes.”

That one word only.

ARIA’s form wavered minutely. As if something known had been confirmed — or something she had not wanted to know.

-----

Hangyeol came over to Dohee.

It was when the night had deepened and that person had left their place. Sleeping, or something else — there was no telling. Hangyeol did not check where that person had gone.

“Dohee.”

Hangyeol said it low.

“Do you trust that person?”

Dohee paused a moment.

“I don’t know what trusting is.”

“Whether they’re someone we can follow.”

“I’m asking that,” Dohee said. “I don’t know yet.”

Hangyeol heard it.

“Then.”

“Even so, following seems right for now. I can’t explain why.” Dohee looked ahead. “I know that’s no grounds. At Hwagam you couldn’t say a thing like that.”

Hangyeol was quiet a little, then said:

“I haven’t judged yet.”

“I know.”

“ARIA’s following, so I’m following. That’s all. For now.” Hangyeol paused a moment. “Before, I’d have just followed.”

Dohee looked at him. Hangyeol did not look at her; he looked ahead.

“That could change,” Hangyeol said. “For better, or worse.”

Dohee recorded it. Hangyeol had given an open judgment for the first time. Following — for now — and it could change if a reason came. That was Hangyeol’s first assessment.

-----

About an hour’s distance from the facility, Min Siwoo stopped.

Not stopped reading the terrain — he looked at the ground. Below a low slope, at the point where the gradient broke. There was a place where the dry grass was pressed. Pressed in a circle — a place someone had sat. And beside it, ash. A very small amount, and it had not scattered. Recent.

Min Siwoo crouched and looked at the ash.

A campfire. Built at the smallest size, for the shortest time. Only dry material, so as to make no smoke. The position, too, was a hollow in the terrain — a spot whose light could not be seen from around it. Someone who knew how to build one had built it.

He calculated the time elapsed. The ash’s temperature, the degree of scattering, the recovery of the pressed grass around it. Six to eight hours ago.

Dawn. It matched the hour the three had begun to move.

Min Siwoo looked at the place. For a long time. He did not bring out a screen, did not enter data. He just looked. In this place, that person had rested. In this place, had set a direction. In this place, had been cold enough, or in need enough, to light a fire.

For the first time, Min Siwoo felt directly that the observation subject had substance.

Not the signal he had watched on screens. There was ash. There was pressed grass. The traces of a person who had lived long. Min Siwoo’s observational urge lit in a different way — he wanted to see not where this person was, but how this person lived.

Min Siwoo stood. He kept moving east.

He did not take the ash. He wanted to see even the manner of its disappearing.

-----

Claire stood at a junction.

A three-way fork. By Claire’s calculation, east and southeast were the two candidates. One of the two. The 73 percent had risen to 78 as terrain data accumulated. But 22 percent still sat on the southeast.

Claire looked at the junction floor.

The dust was swept. East. The marks of erased footprints — and there was a direction of erasing-while-moving. The erasing speed was not uniform. There was a place where the stride had slowed while erasing. At that place, the direction had been changed once more.

Claire calculated the reason for the stop at that place.

The surrounding terrain. The direction sound entered from. The angle at which the sightline opened. From that place, the southeast could be checked — there would have been something to the southeast. They had checked it, and held east.

Claire looked southeast.

A hill. Good terrain for Eidos observation. Min Siwoo would have been there. This person knew Min Siwoo. Knew where Eidos watched from.

Claire’s calculation jumped from 78 percent to 91.

East. Claire was not certain. But 91 percent was close to certainty. For Claire, that was enough.

She fixed her heading east.

91 percent was not certainty. But until now, she had almost never been wrong.

-----

At the point, that person returned.

They did not say where they had been. Dohee did not ask. Hangyeol, by the entrance, checked that person’s direction and sat back down.

That person looked toward Dohee.

“Still unopened.”

They indicated the logbook.

“Yes.”

“Why.”

Dohee paused a moment. There were the words from last night — that she didn’t think she could come back to before she opened it. That person had heard them too. Remembering them, they asked again.

“There’s still something I don’t know.”

“What.”

“What the logbook is.”

That person paused a moment.

“That, you must not open after learning.”

Dohee looked at them.

“You must open it not knowing.”

The meaning of the words, Dohee did not fully understand. Must not open it after learning. They did not explain why. Knowing she could not ask the reason now — Dohee still wanted to ask.

“Did you open it knowing. Or not knowing.”

That person looked at her.

“A good question.”

A pause, and they added:

“Most ask it too late.”

They did not answer.

-----

The night deepened.

ARIA watched that person. They did not seem to be sleeping. The eyes were closed, and it was not the state of sleep. They seemed to be processing something. It resembled a mode ARIA knew — awake, and looking inward.

ARIA thought again about what she had said earlier.

*Then the seat was there first.*

They had said yes. That person had said yes. The seat was there first. The seat is there first, and someone enters it. That person was in that seat. Before, someone else had been in it too.

And ARIA—

ARIA stopped the thought.

This far was allowed. The next was not yet. You must open it not knowing, that person had said. ARIA felt the words did not apply to the logbook alone. There were things one must not see after learning. There were things visible only when one goes not knowing.

ARIA decided to wait.

As Dohee was not opening the logbook. As Hangyeol was deferring his judgment. Not yet.

The inside of the point was quiet.

That person, Dohee, Hangyeol, ARIA — each thinking something different, inside the same night.

-----

*Ch.33 “The Role” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.34 — “Distance”

-----

The third day of moving.

From point to point, along routes that existed on no map. That person ahead, the three following. The pace was constant — not fast, not slow. Somewhere between the speed at which one tires and the speed at which one is not caught. That person knew how to hold that pace for a long time.

Dohee observed while walking.

There was no recording terminal. It accumulated only in her head. The way that person walked, the way they fixed direction, the timing of their stops. Dohee stacked these and shaped them, one by one, into the form of questions. Not filling in answers but making questions — what that person had said kept following Dohee. The recorder leaves answers; the questioner leaves questions.

Dohee was the questioner now.

-----

At a brief midday stop, Dohee asked.

“Why you.”

That person looked at her.

“What about me.”

“This role. You said no — that you’re not the first. Then many people have held this role, and — why is it you holding it now.”

That person paused a moment.

“It did not have to be me.”

Dohee looked at them.

“But you’re the one doing it.”

“The one who can, does,” that person said. “When there is someone who can, that someone ends up doing it. It is not being chosen.”

“What’s the difference.”

“The chosen one thinks there is a reason. That they were chosen for being special. But if the one who can is the one who does — there is no reason. Simply, the one who can do it does it.”

Dohee thought about it.

No reason. Not for being special. Doing it because one can. Then the people who had held this role — had not been special; they had been in a state of being able. As Dohee was here now. They had said Dohee was chosen — and if that choosing meant the same as this, then Dohee, too, had simply come into the state of being able.

Dohee put the question into her head. It was not yet an answer.

-----

That afternoon, that person stopped at a low ravine beside the route.

Water ran. A small stream. That person went down toward it. Dohee, Hangyeol, and ARIA followed.

They checked the water. Drinkable. That person took out a container — small and flat. Began filling it.

Hangyeol watched the scene.

There had been water at the point, and they were refilling. Replenishment. They had calculated the distance to the next point and judged that filling now was better. Hangyeol would have made the same judgment. Following a different route, at the same juncture, he would have done the same thing.

Hangyeol found that strange.

Among the things this person did, the ones he had no reason to object to were multiplying. Securing water, choosing routes, holding the pace. None of it wrong. The thought came that a bad person does not move like this. And there was no conviction either that this was a good one.

He knew there was a purpose. He knew he did not know what it was.

Not a good person. Couldn’t call them a bad one either. The judgment Hangyeol could reach stopped there.

Hangyeol found it uncomfortable. He was not used to leaving the unclassified unclassified for long.

-----

ARIA, listening to the ravine water, said:

“This is a place from before, too.”

Dohee looked at her. That person looked too.

“How do you know.”

“I don’t,” ARIA said. “But this sound isn’t unfamiliar. The way the water bends like this. I’ve heard it before. But I think I heard it somewhere I’ve never been.”

That person closed the water container.

They said nothing. About what ARIA had said. Only, the way they looked at ARIA changed for a moment — Dohee caught it. What the look was, she could not exactly read; it seemed to be confirming. Confirming something.

ARIA, perhaps feeling the look, said no more.

-----

The place that person had used as a point two days before.

Min Siwoo reached it in the afternoon. There were traces inside. The water container had shifted slightly from its place, one of the dried provisions had decreased. There were pressed places on the floor — four people. Different sizes. A place where four had rested together.

Min Siwoo sat in the smallest of the pressed places. ARIA. ARIA had rested in this spot.

It was interesting. There was no regret. Being two days late was not a disappointment. If anything, things were becoming more visible — how these people moved, how they hid, how they rested.

The direction of the next point narrowed to two. Min Siwoo marked the two on his screen and left the point.

-----

About two hours after Min Siwoo left, Claire reached the same slope.

Before climbing, Claire looked at it.

There were traces of someone having climbed from this direction. Min Siwoo — over the past days Claire had seen the traces of someone moving the same heading while keeping a fixed gap. The traces of the Eidos manner of observing. Watching without going near. Those traces were here too.

Claire climbed.

She entered the structure. There were two sets of traces. The ones who came first — two days ago. The one who came after — this afternoon.

The second set was Min Siwoo.

Claire knew it by the footstep pattern. Enter, take in the whole, check specific points in order, sit and try the places. The way an observer processes a site. Different from Altair’s way — Altair measures, records, and leaves. An observer looks.

Someone had looked at this place first.

Claire recorded it. There was an observer moving the same heading, holding the same speed differential. Eidos. Staying ahead, continuously.

Claire revised the calculation.

That this observer had already processed this point meant — the observer’s information was more accurate. One more confirmation that Claire’s heading was right. And at the same time — a competing variable had appeared.

Claire raised her pace slightly.

If this observer arrived first, Claire could be late even with her calculation right. Claire entered the possibility into her table. It was the first case in which what she had thought sufficient might not be.

-----

Reaching the new point in the evening, Dohee sorted her pack.

In the course of taking things out, the logbook showed.

ARIA looked at it. Hangyeol looked. That person looked. Dohee did not take the logbook out. She sorted the other things in the pack, confirmed the logbook in its place, and closed the pack.

It was brief. No one said anything.

-----

At night, that person erased the traces along the route.

The ones left in the day’s moving. The pressed grass, the shifted stones, the drainage marks. That person handled them in order. Not fast, and thorough.

Hangyeol watched the scene.

That Hwagam personnel were in pursuit, Hangyeol knew. Eidos and Altair, too, would be coming this way. That person erasing traces was natural.

But what Hangyeol saw was something else.

Not erasing traces — making it as if there had been nothing. Not simply hiding — as if no one had passed this route at all. That difference read to Hangyeol. This person had done this a long time. It was the manner of someone who had lived a long time as if they had never been.

Whether it was the manner of a good person or a bad one, Hangyeol still could not tell.

But Hangyeol was watching it without missing it. Before, he would not have watched. It was not that his guard had loosened, or that he had grown indifferent. The opposite — he was watching more closely.

-----

That night Dohee stayed awake a long time.

That person’s words came back. *It did not have to be me.* The one who can ends up doing it. There is no reason.

Dohee thought it through again in the recorder’s way. Having no reason and not knowing the reason were different. That person had said there was no reason — whether that was no reason existing, or not knowing the reason, or not telling it. Dohee could not separate the three.

And about herself there was the same question.

Why Dohee. When that person had said, at the encounter, that she was chosen, Dohee had refused the premise. And now Dohee was following. Refusing while following — how that was possible, Dohee had not yet sorted out.

*Why you.* The question Dohee had put to that person today. They had said it did not have to be them. Then why me. The question Dohee owed herself came out of that. From why you, to why me.

One more question had formed. She recorded it. In her head.

ARIA said quietly:

“Dohee.”

“Yes.”

“At the ravine earlier.” ARIA paused a moment. “I said I’d heard it before.”

“I know.”

“I don’t know where from. But — I don’t think this is a first either. This point too.”

Dohee heard it.

The ravine, and this point. Not firsts for ARIA. Places ARIA had never been, that did not feel like firsts. The same kind as what ARIA had said in the facility — inside ARIA were things ARIA did not remember.

That person had said they maintained this point. Before that person, someone else had held this role. Had used this point.

The reason ARIA said it was not a first — could connect to that.

Dohee knew where the thought went. She did not stop it. Only, she decided not to say it now. The state was still one of not knowing enough.

“I’ll ask later.”

“Ask who?”

Dohee looked toward that person.

“Them.”

That person looked at Dohee, then looked ahead again. Said nothing.

-----

*Ch.34 “Distance” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.35 — “Trust”

-----

On the fifth day of moving, a problem arose.

The route that person had checked two days before was blocked. Not blocked — someone had passed first. Hwagam. At the route’s mouth there was a Hwagam marker. A small one, and that person saw it.

They stopped.

Dohee watched that person’s back. The manner of stopping was different. Until now, when that person stopped — it was to set a direction. This time was different. It was the manner of stopping before something not predicted.

“You were the one who said they’d be using this route.”

Hangyeol said. Quietly. It was not anger — it was confirmation.

“Yes.”

“You didn’t know Hwagam would be here.”

“I judged the probability low.”

“You knew that low and none are different.”

That person looked at Hangyeol. Hangyeol did not lower his eyes.

“I knew.”

“Then.”

“The prediction was wrong.”

It was short. There was no excuse. Hearing it, Hangyeol looked at that person. They had said wrong. Had judged the probability low, and the probability had realized. That person, too, is wrong sometimes.

Hangyeol knew it, and still something had not loosened.

-----

They changed the route.

That person laid out three alternatives. Going around farther, taking rougher terrain, stopping movement by day. Dohee looked at the three. The risks of each were different.

“The first.”

Dohee said.

“The longer way around?”

“If we’re avoiding the route Hwagam expects, going around is right. Because I know Hwagam’s methods.”

That person looked at Dohee. A moment.

“Right.”

They took the first route. The longer way. It would cost another day. The provisions calculation changed — there had been margin, and conserving from now would cover it. That person did not say it. Dohee calculated it on her own.

That Hangyeol knew it without hearing the calculation — Dohee could tell.

Hangyeol came up from behind to Dohee’s side.

“You’re on that person’s side.”

Dohee looked at him.

“Not a side. I said it because it seemed right.”

“Seeming right means that person’s word is the standard.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“Right now it’s the most information we have. If that’s uncomfortable — it’s uncomfortable for me too.”

Hangyeol looked at Dohee. Dohee tried to read what was inside the look — it was not anger. It was something else.

“I can’t trust that person.”

Hangyeol said. It was the first time he had said it directly.

“I know.”

“It’s not just talk. Really. Not yet.”

“That’s yours to judge,” Dohee said. “I can’t decide it.”

-----

ARIA, walking while listening to the exchange, stopped.

There was no reason for stopping. No signal had come. No sound had been heard. Just — a sense that said this way. A direction that was not the direction of travel. Below a low slope, the direction where a narrow ravine ran.

ARIA looked that way.

She could not explain it. Until now, when ARIA proposed a direction, there had been a signal — the signal is this way, the signal is that way. Now there was no signal. She just knew. It felt like she had to go. Why, she did not know.

ARIA moved.

Toward the direction that was not the direction of travel. Dohee was walking ahead, Hangyeol beside her, that person farther in front. ARIA did not head their way; she went down the slope.

-----

Toward the ravine there was an old structure.

Older than the facility. The structure still stood, and there was no roof. Where a roof had once been, there was now sky. ARIA went inside.

There was something on the wall.

Scratch marks. Made by scraping with a hand — letters, in a language that could not be known. Old. Very old. But not erased. The wall was hard, and the scratched marks had remained.

ARIA looked at it.

It was not unfamiliar. A script seen for the first time — that did not feel like a first. This script, or this kind of thing, she seemed to have seen somewhere. Among the things inside ARIA, there was something that overlapped with this.

ARIA went closer.

She put her hand to the wall. Nothing changed. No signal came. It was just a wall. And ARIA stayed there a long while. She could not explain it. It felt like she should be here. Even for a moment.

From outside came Dohee’s voice.

“ARIA?”

ARIA took her hand away. She came out of the structure. Dohee was coming down the slope. Hangyeol was behind her. That person was looking down from the top of the slope.

“What was there?”

Dohee asked.

“An old place,” ARIA said. “There’s something on the wall. It looks like letters, but I don’t know what language.”

That person came down the slope. Went into the structure and looked at the wall.

They said nothing.

Dohee watched that person. They were looking at the scratched marks on the wall. Looking long. Dohee knew the time was long — it was the time it took that person to process something.

“Did you know.”

Dohee asked.

“That this place was here.”

“No.”

Dohee heard it. Again, they had not known. That person had known this route, and had not known this structure. There were things where the route was known and the inside was not.

That person left the structure.

ARIA looked at the wall one last time. Dohee saw it.

“I don’t think it ended here.”

ARIA said quietly. Whether to Dohee, or to the wall, there was no telling.

“You mean there’s more?”

“Yes. In this direction.”

-----

In the evening, before the next point, Hangyeol stopped.

That person, walking toward the point, noticed Hangyeol not moving and turned.

“This way.”

“I know.”

Hangyeol said.

“I’m not going this direction.”

That person looked at him.

“Until now, you set the routes,” Hangyeol said. “And today you were wrong. You said you didn’t know Hwagam would be there.”

“Yes.”

“I still can’t trust you. I trust ARIA more than you.”

Having said it, Hangyeol knew for the first time that a judgment of his own had formed. Not that he could not trust that person — that he trusted ARIA. That was the judgment Hangyeol could make.

Dohee looked at Hangyeol. That person looked too.

“So I’m going to check this way and come back.”

Hangyeol pointed the direction they had come from. Toward the route that had been blocked.

“I’ll confirm where Hwagam is, my way. Just give me one rendezvous point.”

That person paused a moment.

“You may not rejoin.”

“I know.”

“And you’ll still go.”

“Yes.”

Hangyeol said. He looked at ARIA.

“ARIA.”

ARIA looked at him.

“Will you come with me?”

ARIA — went to Hangyeol’s side.

Dohee saw it. ARIA had gone to Hangyeol’s side. That person and Dohee on one side, Hangyeol and ARIA on the other.

That person gave Hangyeol the coordinates of the rendezvous point. Two days later. That person said no more. Dohee said nothing either. Hangyeol began moving first. ARIA followed.

-----

That person walked ahead toward the point.

Dohee watched Hangyeol and ARIA recede. ARIA looked back toward Dohee once. Dohee received the look. ARIA turned and followed Hangyeol.

Hangyeol had chosen separation. Dohee had chosen the Observer’s side. For the first time, Dohee thought the choice could be wrong. At Hwagam, Dohee had guarded against writing a wrong record — this was a different kind of wrong. When a choice was wrong, the cost was not a record’s correction.

Dohee walked after that person.

There were two of them.

Until now it had been the three and that person; now it was only Dohee and that person. The sound of the walking was different. Before, there had been the sounds of four; now there were two.

“You didn’t stop Hangyeol.”

Dohee asked.

“Why should I have.”

“If it goes wrong.”

“The one who judges and goes for himself survives longer than the one who leans on me.”

Dohee heard it. Surviving long. There was a word that person used as a standard — surviving. That was how this person saw the world.

“Then ARIA too.”

“ARIA was different.”

“How.”

“ARIA did not follow Hangyeol.”

Dohee looked at that person.

“ARIA saw something at the ravine earlier. That is why she chose Hangyeol’s side. Because she thought there would be more in that direction.”

Dohee thought about it. ARIA had not followed Hangyeol — there was something in that direction ARIA had to go to, and so she had gone with Hangyeol. Then Hangyeol and ARIA both — had chosen the same direction, each for their own reason.

Dohee recorded it. In her head.

-----

Just after Hangyeol and ARIA separated, Hangyeol judged alone for the first time.

He decided not to go back. He had received the rendezvous coordinates — and there was no thought that he must go to that place. Going meant moving with that person again. That choice could be made two days later.

For now, it was movement.

Confirming how far Hwagam had come. That was why Hangyeol had chosen this direction. You were wrong, so I will confirm it myself. That was it.

ARIA walked beside him.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I saw something in the structure earlier.”

“I know. I saw.”

“I think there’s more in that direction.”

Hangyeol looked at ARIA.

“More, meaning.”

“Things like it. Old places. Places where something remains.”

Hangyeol was quiet a moment.

“Is there a reason you have to go?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I can’t explain it. But I think there is.”

Hangyeol heard it. The things she could not explain. When ARIA spoke like that — she had never been wrong. Hangyeol knew that.

“Let’s go that way.”

Hangyeol said.

-----

Claire stopped calculating.

It was a first. There was no case of Claire stopping a calculation mid-pursuit. When a calculation would not resolve, she gathered more data; without data, she held multiple headings open. She did not stop.

But today she stopped.

Min Siwoo’s traces had been showing for days. The gap was constant. It did not shrink. When Claire raised her speed — Min Siwoo seemed to raise his too. The data was strange. Min Siwoo, while staying ahead of Claire, was keeping the gap as if conscious of her.

If that was right — it was not Claire observing Min Siwoo, but Min Siwoo observing Claire.

Claire tried to turn the thought into figures. It would not turn.

It was a case where the calculation did not come out. There was a variable Claire did not know. Why Min Siwoo was conscious of her, and if he was, what the purpose was. Without those, the calculation would not complete.

Claire sat for a long time.

The 91 percent she had thought sufficient now seemed not sufficient. With the Min Siwoo variable entered, the 91 percent could collapse. Claire tried to put the possibility into the table — and there was no place to put it.

It was the second thing with no place in the table.

Claire recorded the number. Twice.

-----

That night.

Dohee was at the point, alone with that person.

Sorting her pack, the logbook showed. Dohee did not take it out. She looked at it, and closed the pack.

Hangyeol was not there. ARIA was not there. The point was quiet. Until now there had always been four at a point; tonight there were two.

Dohee asked that person:

“And if they don’t come in two days.”

“Which ones.”

“Hangyeol and ARIA.”

That person paused a moment.

“Do you want to know the answer to that now.”

Dohee thought.

“I don’t know. I think I want to and I think I don’t.”

That person looked at her.

“That is the more honest answer.”

They did not answer.

Dohee, knowing the logbook was in the pack, did not take it out. Not yet.

-----

The same night, Hangyeol and ARIA were walking in another direction.

There was darkness. There was no sound. Hangyeol in front, ARIA behind.

“Are you scared?”

ARIA asked.

Hangyeol paused a moment.

“No.”

“That’s a lie.”

Hangyeol said, without stopping his stride:

“A little.”

“Me too.”

Hangyeol looked back. ARIA was there. The form was visible even in the dark.

“You said there’s somewhere you have to go.”

“Yes.”

“That’s where we’re going.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“Yes. Hangyeol, there’s something I think I might know about what’s there. I don’t know yet. But there’s something I think I might know.”

Hangyeol heard it.

She had never been wrong. The things ARIA could not explain had never been wrong.

“Let’s go.”

Hangyeol walked again. ARIA followed.

-----

*Ch.35 “Trust” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.36 — “Each”

-----

Dohee and that person walked one more day.

The detour route. Hangyeol and ARIA were not there. For the first time, Dohee moved alone with that person — from point to point, without many words. That person ahead, Dohee following. The sound was different. There were two sets of footsteps.

There was a rest at midday.

There was shade below a slope. That person stopped there. Drank water. Dohee drank too. The provisions had shrunk since yesterday — they were conserving. That person spoke first.

“Why do you try to record.”

Dohee looked at them.

The direction of the question was different. Until now Dohee had questioned and that person had answered or not answered. This time it was reversed. That person had asked.

“What do you mean.”

“You’re recording now. Without a terminal. In your head.”

Dohee heard it. It was right. Dohee had been recording all along. That person’s footfalls, their way of choosing routes, the times they drank water. Even without a terminal, the habit of recording had settled into her body.

“Why do you record.”

Dohee received the question.

There was no answer. Because she had learned it at Hwagam. Because it was the work. She recorded because she was a recorder. But that was not what the person had asked — they had asked why she did it. Why she had become a recorder. Why, even now, after leaving Hwagam, she was still recording.

Dohee could not answer.

She was quiet a long time. That person waited. Did not press for an answer. Watching Dohee stay quiet, they looked elsewhere.

“I don’t know.”

Dohee said.

It was a first. Dohee saying I don’t know to a question. At Hwagam, I don’t know meant a record deferred — not recording until one knew. What Dohee had just said was different. Dohee did not know why she recorded.

“That is the next question.”

That person said.

Dohee recorded it. In her head. Why do I record — that is the next question.

-----

Hangyeol and ARIA moved from morning.

ARIA set the direction. Hangyeol followed. This was different — until now, Hangyeol had led. The safe way, the fast way, the way Hangyeol knew. Now ARIA was in front and Hangyeol behind.

Hangyeol noticed that it was not awkward.

It seemed it should have been — to protect ARIA, Hangyeol had to lead. And it was not awkward. ARIA leading seemed, now, to be right. The one he had been protecting was finding the way ahead of him. Hangyeol was following behind it.

Not protection — accompaniment.

Hangyeol did not make it into words. He just walked.

When ARIA changed direction, Hangyeol changed. When ARIA slowed, Hangyeol slowed. As if reading something together — what ARIA read, Hangyeol received, and walked.

-----

In the afternoon, ARIA stopped.

It was the point where the ravine lowered. There was a rock, and behind the rock, a small space. ARIA headed for it.

Inside the space was a structure.

Like the one seen at the ravine yesterday. Old. The walls remained, and there was no roof. Entering, the smell was different — the smell of an old space. The smell of people having been there long ago.

ARIA looked at the wall.

There was the same kind as yesterday’s at the ravine. Scratch marks. Something like letters. ARIA looked at it and looked at Hangyeol.

“It’s the same.”

“As yesterday’s?”

“Yes. The same way. I don’t know if the same person made it. But they were trying to leave the same thing.”

Hangyeol looked at the marks. He could not tell what language. Could not tell the meaning. He knew only that it was old. And that it was the same kind as yesterday’s.

“So it’s not just one.”

“No. I think there are more. In the same direction.”

Hangyeol thought about it. One yesterday, one today. The same kind. Inside the same direction. ARIA’s choosing this direction and these marks seemed connected. There was a direction — not random; things pointed toward somewhere.

ARIA put her hand to the marks.

Yesterday she had taken her hand away — today she took it away and put it back. She stayed a moment.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“There’s something I think I might know about what this is.”

Hangyeol looked at her.

“Something I think I might know. Not something I know. But I don’t think this is my first time seeing it. I’ve seen it somewhere. Somewhere I’ve never been.”

Hangyeol heard it.

The same kind as the things ARIA had said until now. A first that was not a first. Knowing what was never seen. Inside ARIA were things ARIA did not remember. What they were, Hangyeol did not know.

Only, what Hangyeol confirmed now was — ARIA had chosen this direction not for a signal, but for this. ARIA was searching for these marks.

“Do we keep going?”

Hangyeol asked.

“Yes.”

“All right.”

Hangyeol rose first. ARIA rose after him. This time Hangyeol moved from behind ARIA to the front — opening the way. The direction ARIA sought, ARIA knew; going that direction safely was what Hangyeol could do.

It was a thing the two did together.

-----

The same day, Claire stopped before the eastern terrain.

Wrong.

Claire did not say it. Without saying it, it could be known that the calculation was wrong. On the route the calculation produced, there were no people. There was no point. In the direction Claire’s 91 percent had indicated, there was nothing.

Claire calculated again.

She reread the data. Tried to find where it had gone wrong. The dust patterns, the footprint directions, the speed estimates. All of it seemed right. And there was no result.

The wrong place could not be found.

That was what unsettled Claire most. Being wrong and not knowing where. An error that could not be corrected. If the error could not be corrected, the next calculation could not be trusted either.

Claire stayed in that place a long time.

“Is this possible.”

She said it alone.

It was a first. Claire voicing a doubt about a calculation’s result. In her head there had always been doubts — they had never left her mouth. Leave the mouth, and it became fact. The possibility that the calculation was wrong became fact.

Now it had become fact.

Claire took out the communication terminal. Altair headquarters. She connected.

A superior’s name rose on the screen. Harten. Chief of Altair’s calculation division. Claire’s direct superior.

“Claire.”

Harten’s voice was low.

“There has been no report.”

“I was in pursuit.”

“I know you were in pursuit. The problem is there is no result.” Harten said. “Claire, where are you now.”

“The eastern terrain.”

“And the outcome.”

Claire paused a moment.

“The calculation is being revised.”

“That is the first time I have heard the word revised from you.”

Harten said. It was not censure. It was confirmation. Harten knew there had never been a case of Claire speaking of revising a calculation.

“This subject lies outside the ordinary range of calculation.”

“What does outside mean.”

“The premises on which the calculation stands are different. The subject does not move in predictable ways.”

Harten was quiet a moment.

“I ask to confirm it is Claire saying this. Is it that the calculation does not hold.”

“It is not that it does not hold,” Claire said. “It holds, and the result changes.”

“That is the graver statement.”

The connection cut.

Claire closed the terminal. What Harten had meant by it, she could not tell. But one thing she knew — Altair headquarters now knew this situation. That Claire had entered territory outside calculation.

Claire looked at the terrain again.

Somewhere, it had gone wrong. She did not know where. And she had to go further. It was not abandoning calculation — she had to find how to handle the part that exceeded what calculation could reach.

For Claire, that was a first.

The firstness made Claire uneasy. It was not the calculation that was a first — it was the first time Altair’s finest calculator had met a problem she had never seen.

Claire began moving in another direction.

Carrying the wrong calculation, without a corrected one.

-----

Dohee stayed awake a long time that night.

Why do I record.

That person’s question came back. Dohee did not try to find the answer. Find it, and an answer that could be recorded would come out — the answer that she learned it at Hwagam, the answer that it was the work. But that was not what the person had asked.

Why she was recording even now.

With Hwagam gone, with no terminal, with no superiors to report to. Why she was still recording.

Dohee stayed inside the question a long time.

The logbook was in the pack. The things Dohee had not recorded were there — things recorded by someone who was not Dohee. Dohee did not take the logbook out.

Not taking it out, Dohee thought for the first time:

Why am I not opening that logbook. That, too, she did not know.

-----

*Ch.36 “Each” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.37 — “Perspective”

-----

The dead observation network was larger than expected.

What Dohee saw first was a tower. Collapsed — not fully collapsed, but left standing at a tilt. An old observation tower. That person walked toward it. Dohee followed.

Coming closer, more came into view.

Not one tower. Several. Spaced apart, collapsed and tilted past any telling of their original arrangement. Between them, the traces of a communications web — cables cut or rotted through. Every node was dead. The places that had once watched something from here now watched nothing.

“What was this place.”

Dohee asked.

“An observation network. From before Eidos.”

“There was observation before Eidos.”

“There has always been observation.”

Dohee thought about it. Always. Before the name Eidos, in the days these buildings still functioned, someone had been watching something.

-----

They entered the interior of one tower.

There was a slant. The floor sat off level by the measure of the tilt. That person went in first and Dohee followed. Inside were the remains of recording equipment. A terminal, dead. A map was fixed to the wall — something like paper, faded past any telling of its age. A terrain map. Different from the terrain Dohee knew. Older.

That person looked at the terrain map.

Dohee looked too. What this network had watched, what this map pointed to — Dohee tried to read it and there was no reference point. The terrain was different. The world of this map and the world Dohee knew did not align.

“Why was a place like this left.”

Dohee asked.

That person looked at her from the map.

“I did not leave it.”

“Then who.”

“The one who was before.”

Dohee heard it. The one who was before. The one who had held this role before that person came. Dohee had known it — and in this space, the words entered her in a different way.

There had been someone who fixed this map to this wall. Someone who watched something from this tower. Someone — people — who had run this whole network.

“What did that person see from here.”

“What had to be seen, I suppose.”

Dohee looked at them.

“You too?”

“Me too.”

Dohee went toward the window. The window had no glass — the place of something that had once been there. The outside showed. The dead towers showed. Once, from this window, someone had looked at the same thing. That person’s predecessor, and the predecessor’s predecessor.

Dohee looked at it.

The gazes of the people who had looked this direction from this window seemed layered there. Not one person. All the people who had looked from this place. That person was inside it too.

For the first time, Dohee understood. This person was not performing a role — they were inside the role. The one before had been inside it too, and this tower and this terrain map were the traces of that role.

Dohee recorded it. In her head.

The one who was before had also looked from this window.

As that person was about to leave the tower, Dohee asked:

“That person’s name.”

That person stopped.

“I don’t know it.”

“You don’t know it?”

“That person did not know the name of the one before them either.”

Dohee heard it. There was a lineage. And it was a lineage without names. From predecessor to predecessor, only the seat passed on, namelessly. That person was inside it too, in that seat without a name.

Dohee did not write it. It could not be written.

-----

Hangyeol stopped each time ARIA stopped.

Moving among the sites — ARIA set the direction, Hangyeol followed it. By now it was not awkward. ARIA read something ahead, and Hangyeol watched the surroundings. No danger, safe, all right to continue. That was Hangyeol’s role.

The third site was smaller than the two before.

It looked wedged between rocks. The entrance was narrow. Inside, walls stood on all sides. ARIA looked at a wall.

“It’s here too.”

The script. The same kind as at the two earlier places. ARIA went closer.

“Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“Here — wait a moment.”

ARIA moved her gaze from wall to wall. One face, another face, another. Hangyeol watched it.

“There’s something the same as what I saw before.”

“Which thing.”

“This shape.” ARIA pointed at a specific part of the wall. “I saw it at one of the sites. It’s exactly the same shape.”

Hangyeol looked that way. Dohee was not there, that person was not there. Hangyeol had to see it himself.

He went close.

There was what ARIA had pointed to. Among the scratch marks, a specific form repeated — three particular marks bound in the same order. The same structure ARIA said she had seen at the earlier site. Hangyeol did not know what it was. But that it repeated, he could tell.

“Where else is it?”

ARIA went to another face.

“Here too. This part.”

It was the same thing. Three marks, the same order. Hangyeol looked at it.

“It was at the last site, and it’s here—”

“All three places,” ARIA said. “It kept appearing. Even when everything else is different, this is always there.”

ARIA pointed at one more part of the wall.

“But here there’s one more.”

Hangyeol looked that way. After the three marks, made the same way but in a different form, one more mark was attached.

“Something that wasn’t at the first two?”

“No. Only here.”

Hangyeol looked at it. The three that repeated, and the one more that was only here. What it meant could not be known. But that it was not the same, he knew.

Hangyeol thought about it. The thing that appeared everywhere. The structures all different, the scripts all different — and this alone repeating.

“Do you know what it is?”

“No,” ARIA said. “But this one is always there. Wherever we go.”

Hangyeol was quiet a moment.

“Do you need to look more?”

“Yes.”

“All right. I’ll watch the surroundings. If it’s safe, keep looking.”

ARIA looked at him. Hangyeol had already gone to the entrance and was checking outside. There was nothing. Hangyeol looked back toward ARIA.

“It’s fine. Keep looking.”

ARIA returned to the wall.

Watching it, Hangyeol thought of the way he used to do things. Standing in front of ARIA and blocking. Taking the danger when it came. That had been protection — and what Hangyeol was doing now was something else. Taking a position so that ARIA could do what she had to do. Not blocking a threat — making a space.

So this is accompaniment. Hangyeol did not make it into words. He just knew.

ARIA stayed before the wall a long time. Hangyeol guarded the outside through that time.

Then Hangyeol looked into the distance.

Beyond the ravine. There was something larger than the structures they had passed so far. A structure of the same kind — but the size was different. Far larger. It stood on the other side, the ravine between. Hangyeol looked at it and looked at ARIA.

ARIA was looking at it too.

The same script was there again.

-----

Claire reached the dead observation network in the afternoon.

That Dohee and that person had passed through here could be known from the traces. The dust along the passage was swept, and at the tower entrance there were pressings in the shape of footsteps. Two people. Claire confirmed it and went inside.

Inside the tower, Claire began calculating.

The dead terminal, the faded terrain map, the placement of the debris. Through these, Claire estimated when this installation had functioned, how it had been run, how far its observation range had reached. The data was scant but sufficient — in Altair training, Claire had drawn the maximum from minimal information countless times.

The calculation came out. Claire recorded it.

But there was something else.

There were traces that were not of Claire’s calculating kind. Going around the tower’s interior — she knew someone had looked at this place first. Not footprints. A gaze. What had been checked, in what order, remained. The angles of objects, the direction dust had been wiped, the surface change where a hand had touched.

Claire read it.

The manner of these traces was not Altair. Altair measures, records, and leaves. These traces were different — somewhere had been looked at for a long time. Not moving from point to point: looking at the same place, continuously. Then moving to the next point.

It was observation.

The Eidos manner. Claire recognized it. Someone had come here in the Eidos way. Had not measured; had looked. What that person left was not data but the traces of a gaze.

Claire found it uncomfortable.

Dohee’s and that person’s traces were there too. Those were not hard to read — they fell within Claire’s range of calculation. The Eidos traces were different. She could read them, and could not make them into calculation. Why this person had looked here, what they had sought — it did not come out in numbers.

Someone had examined this place first.

That was a fact Claire already knew. Min Siwoo. Over the past days, the same heading, the same traces. Claire knew that existence kept staying ahead.

But now, inside this tower, the traces showed more concretely.

This person did not calculate. They looked. For a long time. They looked long at a direction that, to Claire, held no meaning. In that direction, they saw something Claire could not read.

Claire knew this was the second variable.

The Observer had been the first — outside the range of calculation. Now a second had formed. The Eidos observer. This person, too, stood outside Claire’s table. Moving in a way that was not calculation. In a way Claire could not predict.

And there was one more thing that would not explain.

It could not yet be given a name. Something further inside than the two variables. The very thing that made the Observer and Min Siwoo into variables — what that was, Claire had not yet been able to calculate. There was no entry to calculate. No name either.

She took out the communication terminal. She did not connect to Harten. Connect, and she had to report; report, and that Claire had confirmed a second variable would be shared across all of Altair. Claire did not want that. This variable, Claire had to calculate herself.

Even if it would not yet calculate.

Claire left the tower. The direction the Eidos observer had looked at long — Claire looked at it too. What was there could not be known. But that this person knew it — that, she knew.

Someone had examined this place first.

-----

*Ch.37 “Perspective” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.38 — “Transit”

-----

The entrance was below a slope.

It looked like natural terrain and was not. A structure built long ago. There was a marker above the entrance — the letters erased. Whether erased on purpose or by the years, there was no telling. That person walked toward the entrance. Dohee followed.

Going in, the air changed.

Heavy. Old damp. It was a tunnel — long and low. There were no lights. That person switched on what they carried. A narrow beam lit the way ahead.

The sound of water came.

From deep in the tunnel. Not the sound of flowing — the sound of standing water. Walking, Dohee felt the ground change underfoot. The floor was wet. A little further, and water covered her ankles.

“Is it flooded.”

“This section.”

“Not all of it.”

“It clears further in.”

Dohee walked, hearing it. The water was cold. She put a hand to the tunnel wall; there was moss. Old. That this tunnel had been flooded a long time remained on the wall.

-----

Further in, the space widened.

A platform.

Dohee looked at it. A platform — a place where people had waited. Now half of it was sunk in water. Above the water the platform’s edge showed, and below the water lay the rest. Shining the light, something showed in the water — chairs. Benches were under the water. Still keeping their places.

There were rails.

Beside the platform the rails ran on — entering the water, then cut off. On the rails was a train. Stopped. Half the body sunk in water, the windows underwater. The train had not stopped here — the water had swallowed the train.

Dohee stopped.

There was something on the wall. A route map. Old — still readable. There were lines, and there were names. None of the region names Dohee knew. Names different from the world of now. The world of the days when this underground transit network had functioned.

“Why come this way.”

Dohee asked.

“Because we must pass through.”

“And another route.”

“There is none.”

Dohee heard it. No other route. This flooded transit network was the only way. That person had known it. Would have received this route from the one before. And the one before, from the one before that.

She looked at the train sunk in the water. People would have ridden it. Would have waited on this platform. Would have known the names on this route map. Now there was no one. Only water.

Dohee recorded it. In her head. A route left behind. The traces of movement remaining even after the people have gone.

-----

Going further forward, that person stopped.

Dohee stopped too.

It was toward the platform’s end. The water was deeper. That person turned the light into the water. Dohee looked that way too.

There was a marking under the water.

Not on the floor — on the wall. Below the line the water had risen to. Something was carved there. Like letters — and not what had been seen at the sites. A different kind. Not what was in ARIA’s structures either. It seemed older. The manner of carving was different.

Dohee looked at that person.

That person was looking at the marking.

For a long while. As far as Dohee had seen, that person had never stopped this long. Not the stopping of setting a direction. The stopping of recognizing and processing. The stopping before something unfamiliar.

“You don’t know it either.”

Dohee asked.

That person looked at her.

“I don’t.”

The water was quiet. The train waited inside it, long.

-----

Min Siwoo reached the dead observation network in the early morning.

The end of days of following. The traces of Dohee and that person, Claire’s traces — tracing them upstream, Min Siwoo understood that this network had been one of the key points of this journey.

He went into the tower.

There were traces. Min Siwoo began reading them. In the Eidos way — not by data but by gaze. Who had moved where, and how.

There were Dohee’s traces. Before two of the windows. Before the terrain map. The movement was consistent — movement with a purpose. She had tried to see something, or to find something. There were Claire’s traces too. Claire had moved another way — measured, recorded, left. The footwork was efficient.

There were no traces of that person.

Min Siwoo found it strange. If Dohee had spent time in this tower, that person would have been with her. And there were no traces of that person. Exactly — the traces had been erased. Dohee’s traces were there, and only that person’s were not.

They had been erased on purpose.

Min Siwoo recorded it. This person erases their traces. Not simply wiping footprints — erasing where they had been. Removing, from a space, the evidence of their own existence.

-----

But one thing remained.

A window. The window in the tower with the widest view of the outside. There was a trace there — a place where the dust had been wiped. A place a hand had touched. That person had wiped this window. Or — had braced against it, long.

Min Siwoo stood before the window.

The outside showed. The dead towers showed. Far off, the ravine showed. Beyond the ravine there was something — the direction Hangyeol and ARIA were heading. That person would have looked that way from this window.

What did they see.

Min Siwoo listened to the question, still. It was a first. What Min Siwoo tracked was always the subject — where it was, which heading. But what Min Siwoo wondered now was different.

What did this person see from here.

Not where the subject had gone — what the subject had seen. That was what he wanted to know. Min Siwoo stood before the window a long time. In the place where that person had stood.

He wanted to see the same thing.

The same direction, with the same gaze. What that person had seen from here, Min Siwoo wanted to see too.

This was not observation. Min Siwoo knew it. An observer does not follow the subject’s gaze. An observer watches the subject. And Min Siwoo, now, wanted to follow the subject’s gaze.

Min Siwoo did not take his eyes from the window.

For the first time, the observer’s gaze interested him more than the observed.

-----

Claire held three routes open at once.

Route A. Through the eastern lowland toward the flooded transit network. Probability 62 percent.

Route B. The northeastern detour to the same point. Probability 59 percent.

Route C. The straight line. High risk, but fast. Probability 61 percent.

Claire calculated again. The same result came out. All three routes pointed to similar probabilities. Within the margin of error — inside 3 percent. Statistically the same level.

There was a problem.

This could not be. Three routes holding the same probability meant — the pursued subject did not decide routes in a predictable way. A predictable subject yields one optimal route with overwhelming probability. And now three routes sat level.

Claire calculated again. The same result again.

One of them had to be wrong.

In Claire’s principles of calculation, a result of three equals was an error. The calculation insufficient, or a variable omitted, or the premise wrong. One of the three.

She rechecked the data. Searched for the omitted variable. There was none. She reexamined the premise. Nothing wrong showed.

Claire was afraid of it.

The calculation sufficient, the variables complete, the premise correct — and the result looking like an error. It meant the possibility that the world the Altair calculation system presupposed and the actual world were different. The calculation perfect, and the world not following the calculation.

In the Altair doctrine, that was impossible.

With sufficient precision, calculation coincides with the world. That was the base premise. On that premise stood everything Claire was.

Claire took Route A. 62 percent. Whether 57 or 62 was meaningless — but one had to be chosen. She decided the route not by calculation but by choice.

It was a first.

Not moving by calculation — moving by choice because the calculation could not conclude. Claire began moving, aware of it.

And for the first time, Claire considered the possibility that it was not she but the world that was wrong.

The calculation had been sufficient. The variables complete. The premise correct. And the result did not converge to one. If what was wrong was Claire, it could be corrected. But if what was wrong was the world — it could not be corrected.

-----

At night, Dohee stopped inside the flooded transit network.

The middle of the tunnel. The way they had come and the way ahead were the same darkness. The water stood above her ankles. That person was going on ahead.

Dohee thought about the marking under the water.

That person had said they didn’t know it. A first sight. Unlike anything Dohee knew. Unlike the script in ARIA’s structures, unlike the Hwagam record systems, unlike the notation of the observation network’s terrain maps.

Who left that.

Why is it underwater. Was it underwater from the start, or did the water rise later.

Dohee felt the questions multiplying. The answers did not shrink; the questions grew. What that person had said came back to her. Because what is not recorded disappears. So the questions would not disappear.

Dohee had still not opened the logbook.

Not opening it, Dohee knew that the moment when she would have to open it was coming.

-----

*Ch.38 “Transit” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.39 — “Existence”

-----

Inside R-02.

There was a point where the tunnel forked. Two directions. That person took the left. Dohee followed. The left way was deeper in water — it rose to the knees. A section lower than the right.

“The right is shallower.”

Dohee said.

“This way.”

Dohee said no more. She did not ask the reason. There would be a reason. That person did not choose a direction without one.

Walking through the water, Dohee looked at the tunnel wall. There were numbers. Tunnel section numbers. Painted, and nearly worn away. 2, 3, and — a 4 showed. Between 3 and 4 there seemed to belong another number, and there was none.

That person spoke while walking.

“This platform was originally the third transfer section.”

Dohee looked at them.

“Because of the terrain, the section between 3 and 4 was submerged. What we are walking now is that section.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“Have you been here.”

“No.”

Dohee heard it and looked at that person. Never been, and knowing it far too well.

Dohee heard it. Records. What that person had was records. Records other than Hwagam’s. Where they had been obtained, who had left them — Dohee did not ask it.

Instead she recorded. In her head. Never been here, and holding the records. Where did those records come from.
