How that person’s records and the memory of this network of points connected — Dohee knew that was the next question.

-----

Further on there was a second platform.

Smaller than the first. The degree of submersion was different — nowhere was sunk to the floor. The whole platform remained. On the wall were route guidance markers. Connecting directions were written there — the names of places that no longer existed.

That person went toward the platform’s end.

And stopped.

It was the wall. The same position as the underwater marking seen at the earlier platform — the low part of the wall. Here the water level was lower and the whole wall showed. There was a marking. The same kind as before. Carved the same way.

Dohee looked at it.

Two places. Two places within this tunnel alone. Whether one person had carved them, or different people each — there was no telling. The manner of carving was similar. Whether the content was the same could not be known. It could not be deciphered.

That person looked at the marking.

Looked long. The longest Dohee had seen.

“Is this in the records too.”

Dohee asked.

That person did not answer.

Dohee heard the silence. It meant no. This marking was not in that person’s records. Not in what was received from the one before. Nor the one before that.

-----

Hangyeol and ARIA reached the fourth site by day.

It was larger. A different scale from the three before. There were several structures — not one freestanding structure, but similar ones gathered close. Hangyeol looked at it.

“Is this the center.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“I don’t know. It’s just big.”

Hangyeol watched the surroundings from behind ARIA. There was nothing strange. Quiet. Far off, beyond the ravine, the larger structure seen before showed — whether this led to it, or stood apart, could not yet be known.

“Do we go in?”

Hangyeol asked.

ARIA walked toward it. Hangyeol followed.

-----

Inside, ARIA looked at the walls in order.

Hangyeol guarded the outside from the entrance. That was the role now. While ARIA looked at what she had to look at, Hangyeol made the space. Not guarding — making space.

ARIA stopped.

“It’s here too.”

Hangyeol turned.

“The same one?”

“Yes. The one from the three places. It’s here too.”

Hangyeol looked where ARIA pointed. The three marks. The thing that had appeared from the first site to the third. It was here too.

“There was one more at the third.”

“Here too.” ARIA pointed at another wall. “There’s one more here too. But it’s different from the third’s.”

Hangyeol looked that way. After the three marks there was a fourth — like the one seen at the third site, but the form was different.

“It’s different at each place?”

“Yes. The three are always the same. The fourth is different.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“This fourth one I’m seeing for the first time. Only here.”

Hangyeol looked at it. The three fixed, the fourth changing. What it meant could not be known. But there was a pattern.

“And the others. The rest of the script.”

“All different,” ARIA said. “All different every time. But only this is the same.”

ARIA pointed at the three marks.

Hangyeol looked at it. This was why ARIA had gone searching through the four places. Not searching — heading toward where this was. ARIA was recognizing this.

“Do you know what it is?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“No. But I have to look.”

Hangyeol heard it. She could not explain it, and said she had to look. Hangyeol asked no more.

“How much longer?”

“Just a little.”

Hangyeol went back to the entrance and watched the outside. There was nothing strange. Quiet.

ARIA looked for a long time. Hangyeol waited.

-----

Inside Geumhui.

A report came up.

At the top of the document was the house seal. Gold-thread border, rank markings. The Geumhui internal record format. There was a document number, an author, a recipient. The recipient field was blank — the direct-delivery method.

The body was short.

*Sector 7 outskirts. Multiple unregistered movement routes confirmed. Additional traces of Observer facilities discovered. Moving parties unidentified. Hwagam, Eidos, and Altair each confirmed to have dispatched personnel to the same zone.*

*Addendum: within an underground structure in the same zone, an old-form marking matching Geumhui’s proprietary record format confirmed. Source unknown to date.*

At the document’s foot was a review request mark.

The recipient’s name.

Kangwi.

The one charged with delivery spoke, holding the document.

“Shall I take it up to Kangwi.”

“He has already been informed.”

There was a silence. The deliverer set the document down.

Kangwi already knew. Before the document went up. By what route he had come to know — that was not in the document. At Geumhui, Kangwi knowing first was an old thing. Grasping it before the report was complete. That was the reason Kangwi held the head seat.

And Kangwi had not yet moved.

At Geumhui, not moving did not mean no interest. When Kangwi did not move — it meant something else was already moving.

The document was left where it was. Without the review-complete mark.

-----

Within the same zone, different things were moving.

Dohee and that person were inside R-02. Before the markings of the submerged transit network. Hangyeol and ARIA were at the fourth site. Before the repeating sign. Kangwi was somewhere. In the state of already knowing.

Whether these connected, and if they connected, how — no one knew.

Only, they were looking the same direction.

-----

*Ch.39 “Existence” — end*

-----

-----

## Deletion-01 — “Absence”

*Placement: between Vol.5 Ch.39 and Ch.40*
*POV: the Geumhui records-hall keeper*

-----

There was no record.

The records-hall keeper checked the access log. The Sector 7 materials. There was an access history. The accessor field was blank.

An unrecorded access.

The keeper asked the previous keeper. The previous keeper asked the one before. It had been so for a long time, they said.

“Who accessed it.”

“I don’t know.”

“Why is there no record.”

“The absence is the record.”

The keeper could not tell what the words meant. That the absence is the record. That nothing is the mark.

He pulled the Sector 7 materials themselves.

Original preserved: no. Disposed. Reason. Blank. The reason field existed and held nothing.

Whether it had been blank from the start, or erased — there was no telling.

The keeper looked at the date. Old. From before the present house system was formed.

He closed the document.

The next day, someone accessed that document. The accessor field was blank.

The records-hall keeper recorded it.

*Absent again.*

-----

*Deletion-01 — end*

## Vol.5 Ch.40 — “Order”

-----

The inside of R-02 grew darker.

The tunnel deepened. The distance the light reached shrank. That person’s walking pace did not change. Dohee watched it — the darker it grew, they did not slow. A person to whom this darkness was familiar.

Dohee looked at the walls as she walked.

Advertisements. Fixed to the wall — not paper but board. Printed long ago, and after the flooding and the years, almost without color. Still the forms remained. There was a human figure. Holding something. Below it were letters — letters Dohee could not read. A language no longer in use.

The people who passed through this tunnel had looked at this.

Dohee thought about the fact. In the days this advertisement hung, there were people in this tunnel. People who read these letters. Now there was water.

Further on there was a display board. Stopped. It stayed in its place with the screen dark. Once it would have shown something — the hour, the lines, the notices. Now there was nothing. A display board that would not light kept its place above the water.

In a section where the inside of the train showed, Dohee looked in through a window.

There were seats. At a height the water had not reached. There was something like a bag — left behind, long ago, by someone. The form remained. They would have meant to come back for it. They had not come.

Dohee recorded these things. In her head. Traces of the former world. Evidence that people had lived.

Dohee asked while walking:

“Hwagam pursues. Eidos watches. Altair calculates.”

That person listened without breaking stride.

“Why has Geumhui not moved yet.”

“Did I say it has not moved.”

Dohee heard it. That person had asked back. The premise that Geumhui was not moving was wrong.

“Is Geumhui’s way different.”

“Geumhui does not look at anomalies.”

Dohee looked at them.

“Hwagam removes the anomaly. Eidos observes the anomaly. Altair explains the anomaly,” that person said. “Geumhui looks not at anomalies but at order. An anomaly is a violation of order. To Geumhui, we are not an anomaly. We are a violation.”

Dohee thought about it. Anomaly and violation. The same thing defined in different ways. The word anomaly defines an existence; the word violation defines a relation.

“Then Geumhui sees us as having broken the order.”

“Not as having broken it — as breaking it.”

“Is there a difference.”

“What is finished is handled. What is in progress is waited on.”

Dohee knew where the words pointed. That Geumhui was waiting. And that the waiting side could be the more dangerous.

Kangwi. Dohee had not yet heard the name. But in the way that person explained Geumhui, it could be felt that there was someone inside Geumhui. Someone who, without moving, knew everything.

-----

The fifth site stood on a hill.

Hangyeol climbed first. ARIA gave the direction from behind. Near the hilltop there was a structure like the earlier sites. Smaller. There was a space one could enter.

ARIA went inside.

Hangyeol watched the outside from the entrance. There was nothing.

“How is it.”

Hangyeol asked.

ARIA did not answer.

Hangyeol turned. ARIA was standing before a wall. Not moving. Hangyeol went inside.

He looked at the wall.

There was script. The same kind as the earlier sites. But — what Hangyeol had been seeing was not there. The three marks. The thing that had been present without fail at the four earlier places. Every other character could differ, and this alone was always there. Now it was not.

“It’s not here.”

ARIA said.

“What isn’t.”

“The thing that’s always been there. It’s gone.”

Hangyeol looked at the wall again. The characters were there. But the three marks that had repeated were not. Hangyeol remembered the four earlier places — there had been no place without the three marks. Only here, they were absent.

“Was it there and then removed?”

“I don’t know,” ARIA said. “But it should be here too. There’s a place where it should be.”

Hangyeol looked that way. The wall face ARIA pointed at. There were other characters, and among them was an empty space. Not naturally empty — like the place of something that had been there and was gone. The surface was different. Slightly less old than the rest.

“Was it erased.”

“I don’t know.”

Hangyeol looked at it. An erased thing. Present at the four earlier places, absent here. If someone had erased it — they had erased it knowing. Someone who knew what these three marks were.

ARIA was quiet.

Hangyeol looked at her. ARIA’s form was unlike usual. Not wavering — stopped. ARIA’s way of stopping was her way of processing, and this was something else.

“Are you all right?”

ARIA was quiet a moment, then said:

“I’m bewildered.”

It was a first. ARIA saying she was bewildered.

-----

Inside Geumhui.

The space was wide. There was light, and it was not a warm light. Things hung on the walls — a space with formality. A seating arrangement with hierarchy. The ones standing and the ones seated were different.

One person reported.

“We are attempting to identify the moving parties on the Sector 7 outskirts. Hwagam is in direct pursuit, Eidos is tightening its observation net, and Altair is entering on independent judgment.”

Another spoke.

“What will Geumhui do.”

There was no answer.

The space went quiet. The standing ones looked toward the seated. They were waiting for words to come from the seated side.

“It concerns the Observer.”

The one who had reported said.

“I know.”

The voice was low. There was no explanation. He already knew.

The space went quiet again.

“The other houses are approaching each in their own way. Should Geumhui not move as well.”

“Hold.”

One word.

No explanation. No reason either. Inside Geumhui, no one asked for reasons. When the word hold came out — that was the decision.

The standing ones bowed their heads.

“Do you mean to watch while Hwagam, Eidos, and Altair move first.”

One person asked, last.

There was a pause.

“The ones who move can be seen.”

That was all.

The space went quiet again. All of Geumhui stood on hold. Not moving was the decision. While the other houses moved, Geumhui was watching it.

The ones who move can be seen.

The words remained in the space.

The one who had brought the report, leaving the space, said low to another beside him:

“How does Kangwi know. Before the documents go up.”

“There is a records hall.”

“A records hall.”

“The Sector 7 materials,” that person said. “They are inside Geumhui. The other houses cannot access them.”

“When were they recorded.”

There was a short silence.

“Long ago.”

No further explanation. Long ago was all of it. How long ago — no one asked either. At Geumhui, the words long ago were both an explanation and a boundary.

-----

Claire restarted the calculation that same night.

For two days she had not found the reason the three routes had been equal. She tried adding variables — the result did not change. She tried changing premises — it did not converge. Claire took the calculation method itself apart.

Starting from the base premise.

The subject takes the optimal route. All of Claire’s calculation stood on this premise. But this subject might not take the optimal route. If there was a purpose other than survival. If the very criterion of route selection differed from Claire’s.

Claire tried replacing the premise.

The subject takes a route that is not the optimal one. The calculation changed. One of the three routes dropped. Not convergence — but different.

For the first time, she had run a calculation that changed the premise.

Claire recorded the result. *If the calculation is sufficient and the world does not converge, the premise itself must be replaced.* It was not abandoning the calculation system. It was revising the premise. To Claire, that was a different kind of act.

-----

That night Dohee looked at the logbook.

She did not take it out. She looked at it inside the pack. Still unopened. What that person had said came back. That it must be opened not knowing. That it must not be opened after learning.

Dohee still did not know. Enough.

Whether she was ready to open it, she did not know either.

Only, there were the things she had seen passing through R-02. A route left behind. The traces of movement remaining even after the people have gone. A record could be such a thing too — the traces of questions remaining even after the recorder has gone.

Dohee thought about it.

She did not open it.

-----

*Ch.40 “Order” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.41 — “The Crack”

-----

The Hwagam office was as it had been when Dohee was there.

The same terminals, the same routines, the same report formats. The executors sat in their places and processed the routines. No anomaly, no anomaly, no anomaly. That was Hwagam’s work. It had been so when Dohee was there.

Gadeung reviewed the reports that had come up today.

He stopped at the second item.

Sector 7 northeast. Deviation in data traffic figures. Within margin of error. Inspection routine manual, article 3, clause 2 — figure deviations within the margin of error are not subject to recording.

It had been processed as no anomaly.

Gadeung looked at the item. He checked who the executor had been. A new appointee. The routine had been followed exactly. By the manual.

When Dohee was there, reports like this did not come up.

That was the problem. Would Dohee have processed this as no anomaly. She would not have. Dohee did not let figures within the margin of error pass. She recorded them. The manual said they need not be recorded — Dohee recorded them. That was Dohee’s way.

Was that way wrong.

Gadeung did not judge it. That Dohee had acted outside the rules was fact. The record file outside official channels, the unauthorized archive access, the non-cooperation during the inquiry. Dohee had done wrong.

But since Dohee was gone, the Sector 7 reports had grown cleaner. No anomaly had multiplied. The numbers squared. No one held onto the margin-of-error figures.

Gadeung judged that to be the greater wrong.

-----

He opened the next report.

A list of zones with inspections complete. The zones Dohee had been responsible for were there. New executors had been assigned. Routine passed. No anomaly.

Gadeung selected one of those zones and opened the raw logs himself.

The data flowed.

It sat within the margin of error. It looked like no problem. Gadeung compared it against the past thirty days. There was a pattern — very faintly, at the same hours, in the same direction, deviation was accumulating. Each within the margin, and summed, it was different.

The same way Dohee had first found 7-7-4.

Gadeung recorded it. Through official channels. Sent it up to his superior. He did not make a personal terminal file as Dohee had. He processed it by the rules.

And he thought one thing.

The way Dohee had found this had been a way that broke the rules — if Dohee had done it within the rules, would this have surfaced earlier than now. Or could it not have surfaced within the rules at all.

Gadeung did not answer the question.

Dohee had broken the rules. Gadeung still did not think that judgment wrong. The problem was something else. Only the rule-keepers remained, and the records had grown worse. If the rules are right and the results are bad — then there is something that must stand outside the rules. Whether Dohee had been that something, Gadeung did not know. He knew only that the place stood empty.

-----

Deeper inside R-02.

The water receded. The terrain had risen. The floor began to show. Dohee’s feet touched it. Old tiles. Cracked, but not broken.

There was a display board on the wall. Stopped. Not switched off — halted: the last display remained. The characters were broken and only part could be read. Only an expected arrival time remained. That train had not arrived.

A little further there was an advertisement board. Not paper but panel. Old, and the form remained. Something was written in a language that could not be read. There was a human figure — smiling. What the advertisement said could not be known. Only the smiling person remained.

On the wall was a lost-property cabinet.

It looked like personal lockers — small in size. Numbered. There were locks, frozen open since long ago. Some were empty. Some were not.

Dohee stopped in front of it.

A bag showed. A small one. Old, and the form remained. There was something inside — a thing with bulk. Dohee did not open it. There was no reason to open it. And it felt like a thing that should not be opened. A thing that should be here.

“They would have meant to come back for it.”

Dohee said.

That person looked that way.

“They could not come.”

Dohee heard it. Could not come. Had meant to come and could not. There had been someone who knew the number of this locker. Now there was no one.

Looking at the locker, Dohee thought of the things left behind at Hwagam. The suspension notice. The personal terminal. The 7-7-4 file. The things Dohee had left. She could not go back for them. They remained as things left behind.

The same as this locker.

-----

Claire revised the calculation as she moved along Route A.

She did not discard the existing premise. She added one.

*The subject selects, in place of the optimal route, the route that lowers the probability of discovery.*

With this premise entered, the calculation changed. One of the three routes fell. One rose. It did not converge — but it was narrower than two days before.

Claire looked at the result.

Change one premise and the whole calculation changes. A fact Claire had known — applying it to her own calculation was another thing. In Claire’s calculation system, premises were verified things. Not things subject to revision. And now she had revised one.

It had not been wrong. It had been incomplete.

Claire recorded the difference. *Not a wrong premise but an incomplete one. There was no complete description of the subject’s principle of action.* It was not abandoning the calculation system. It was completing it.

For the first time, revising a premise had changed a result.

Claire kept moving.

-----

The Geumhui records-hall access log.

Access date and time. Accessor. Document classification accessed.

Most of the fields were filled. A few were not. The ones with the accessor field blank. Not no access record — no record of the access itself. Unrecorded access.

One of the document classification entries.

Sector 7 materials.

Access grade: restricted.

Below it was a disposal record.

*Original preserved: no. Disposed. Reason:* — the reason field was not filled. Not blank. Deleted. The place where the field itself had been erased.

By the document’s date, it was old. From before the present house system was established.

The people who could access this document were restricted even within Geumhui. That there had been access without an access history — that was the fact only the records-hall keeper knew. Whether Kangwi had accessed it directly was not in the records.

That it was not in the records did not mean it had not been.

-----

That evening, Dohee stopped near the R-02 exit.

She opened her pack.

The logbook was there. In the place it had always been. Dohee looked at it.

This time, she did not turn her eyes away.

-----

*Ch.41 “The Crack” — end*

-----

## Vol.5 Ch.42 — “The Record”

-----

Dohee opened the logbook.

There was a first page.

Handwritten. Not by terminal — by hand. The pressure was uneven — some letters pressed hard, some faint. The one who wrote it had been in different states while writing.

Dohee read.

*It does not matter who was first. This question has already been recorded many times.*

Dohee read the sentence again.

Already recorded many times. This question. The question in this logbook was not Dohee’s alone. Before, someone had asked this question, and recorded it. It was here.

Dohee turned to the next page.

The sentence was unlike any record Dohee had expected.

-----

Inside Altair.

Claire’s communication connected. The receiving side was in another room. There was only a screen — the other’s location did not show.

“Connecting.”

A form appeared on the screen.

No face came up. Only a space showed — there were terminals. Calculation equipment. Things in the Altair manner. Equipment Claire knew — and more of it. Older ones too. Versions Claire had never seen.

A voice came.

“Claire.”

Claire froze.

“The report was late.”

“I was revising a calculation.”

“Did you say revising.”

There was a short silence.

“I added a premise. The subject’s principle of action was not completely described by the existing premises alone.”

“Adding a premise and revising a premise are different.”

Claire paused a moment.

“That is correct. It is an added premise.”

“Continue.”

Claire reported the revised calculation. That the three routes which had been equal had narrowed to one. That with one premise added, the result had changed.

The voice said:

“Who proposed that premise.”

Claire paused a moment.

On the day Claire first learned calculation, Julius had asked the same question. *Who proposed that axiom.* Claire answered. Julius asked again. *Who verified it.* It went on that way. At the end of every calculation there was a first premise, and the first premise demanded verification. That was the Altair way.

What Claire had just done was outside that way.

“I derived it myself.”

“At Altair, only verified premises are used. Self-derivation is hypothesis.”

“Yes.”

“Is the content of your report that you calculated on a hypothesis.”

Claire heard it. By the Altair standard, a hypothesis was not calculation. If what Claire had done was not calculation but hypothesis — then what she had until now thought of as revision became something else.

“I used an unverified premise.”

“Was the premise correct.”

“The result narrowed.”

“Is the result narrowing evidence that the premise is correct.”

Claire did not answer.

The voice said:

“Claire. At Altair, calculation begins from premises. Change the premise and it is a different calculation. It is not a revision of the same calculation.”

“Understood.”

“Have you identified what the subject is.”

“Not yet.”

“Reexamine your approach. Return if necessary.”

The connection cut.

Claire looked at the darkened screen.

Julius. The one who designed the Altair calculation system. It was in Julius’s system that Claire had first learned to calculate. Within that system, Claire had never once been wrong.

Now Claire had done something outside that system.

Whether it was right or wrong — by the standard Julius had spoken, it could not be judged.

-----

Hangyeol and ARIA were resting.

A little away from the fifth site. ARIA was quiet. Since coming out of the place where the three marks were missing, her words had thinned.

Hangyeol was beside her.

“Are you all right?”

ARIA was quiet a moment, then said:

“I don’t know.”

“What don’t you know.”

“I felt something there — and it’s gone. It was erased, that’s right, and I know what was erased. But I don’t know what it is.”

Hangyeol heard it.

“What do you mean, you know.”

“I know that something that should be there isn’t. The thing that should be there. But I don’t know what it is.”

Hangyeol thought about it. Knowing that what should be there is not. And not knowing what it is. That was the way ARIA knew things — knowing the direction and not the content.

“Do we keep searching?”

ARIA paused a moment.

“Yes. And — Hangyeol.”

“Yeah.”

“I just thought of something,” ARIA said. “The extra one at the fourth site. Besides the three marks.”

“Yeah.”

“It was different from the third site’s, but thinking about it now, it’s a similar structure. Not exactly the same — made the same way.”

Hangyeol heard it.

“You mean there’s another repetition?”

“Yes. The three marks are one — and the fourth is a repetition too. It looks different at each place, but it’s the same method.”

ARIA changed a little as she spoke. The bewilderment turned into something else. From a thing that could not be explained, into a thing where something was beginning to show.

“Let’s keep going.”

Hangyeol stood.

-----

Near the R-02 exit, Dohee looked at the logbook.

The first page began with a question. It was not what Dohee had expected. Not an answer. A question. For Dohee, recording had always meant recording answers — anomaly present, no anomaly, confirmed, unconfirmed. The first sentence of this logbook was not an answer.

*It does not matter who was first.*

Dohee thought about it. It does not matter. And someone had recorded it. Recorded that it does not matter. Recording what does not matter — different from the recording Dohee knew.

*This question has already been recorded many times.*

The record left no answer. It left only that the question had repeated. Many times, many people, the same question.

Dohee started to go to the next page and stopped.

She had not yet looked at the second page. There was only the first. Whether that was enough for Dohee — it was not enough. But before going further, it seemed she should stay a long time on the first page.

That the question repeats.

The record was not for answers but for not losing the questions. It was what that person had said at the start. Dohee had thought she understood it then; only after opening the logbook did she actually understand.

She did not close the logbook.

The second page showed. There was a first line. Dohee knew her gaze was going there. She did not read it.

Not yet.

-----

*Ch.42 “The Record” — end*

-----

-----

-----

## Archive-02 — “The Blank”

*Placement: after Vol.5 Ch.42*
*POV: Gadeung / inside Hwagam*

-----

A new appointee brought a report.

“It is within the margin of error.”

Gadeung looked at the report.

“And.”

“By the manual, no handling is required.”

“Dohee would already have followed it.”

The new appointee looked at Gadeung.

“Dohee was dismissed for violating regulations.”

“I know.”

Gadeung set the report down.

He opened the desk drawer. There was a black notebook. Not an official format. A thing Gadeung had kept for a long time. The things that did not go into Hwagam’s official reports — figures within the margin of error, the things the manual said not to record — were in there.

He opened the notebook.

There were the figures Dohee had written down. The things Dohee had called strange before her dismissal. At the time, Gadeung had said it was a manual violation. He had not said it was wrong.

Looking now — she had been right.

The new appointee stood beside Gadeung.

“Eidos has been adding observation personnel in Sector 7 lately.”

“Altair too.”

“And us.”

Gadeung closed the notebook.

“We must find Dohee.”

The new appointee stopped.

“By regulation, a fugitive—”

“I did not ask about regulations.”

He put the notebook in the drawer.

“Eidos only watches. Altair only calculates. Hwagam executes,” Gadeung said. “But right now Hwagam cannot execute. Do you know why.”

The new appointee did not answer.

“Because we lost the person we should be finding.”

Gadeung picked up the report again. A figure within the margin of error. Processing it as no anomaly would do. But Gadeung took out the notebook and wrote it down separately.

It was a method not in the manual.

-----

*Archive-02 — end*

-----

## Observation-02 — “Intervention”

*Placement: after Vol.5 Ch.42*
*POV: Tiko and Min Siwoo / inside Eidos*

-----

Two Eidos agents were fighting.

A matter of observation zone assignments. One said they had to go closer; one said the current distance was appropriate. The voices grew loud.

Tiko sat beside them.

He held an observation lens. A small one. The thing Tiko always carried. It was closed now. When he opened it — the others could not tell.

“Tiko.”

One agent said.

“Which of us is right.”

“Observing.”

“We’re fighting right now.”

“Observing.”

The two agents looked at each other, then at Tiko. Tiko sat holding the lens.

The fight continued.

-----

Min Siwoo arrived at the point.

A temporary Eidos point. There was food. Min Siwoo passed it by.

Tiko saw it.

“Observing?”

Min Siwoo said, spreading out his equipment:

“Yes.”

“And the food.”

Min Siwoo stopped.

Tiko was wiping the lens. He did not look at Min Siwoo.

“Hwagam writes reports. Altair does calculations,” Tiko said. “Eidos sees. To see, you must live.”

Min Siwoo looked toward the food.

“I’m observing.”

“You can observe while eating.”

Min Siwoo took the food. Tiko kept wiping the lens.

Outside the window, the two agents were still fighting.

“And those two.”

“Observing.”

Min Siwoo said no more.

-----

*Observation-02 — end*

-----

## Calculation-02 — “3%”

*Placement: after Vol.5 Ch.42*
*POV: Julius and Claire / the Altair calculation hall*

-----

The day Claire first learned calculation.

The result came out. 97%.

Julius looked at it.

“Why is 3% left.”

Claire explained the calculation process again. Premises, variables, equations. There was nothing wrong.

“It is 97%.”

“And the 3%.”

“It is the margin of error.”

“Error has a reason.”

Claire calculated again. The same result. 97%.

“It is 97.”

“So the 3% is.”

Claire looked at Julius.

In front of Julius was a white calculation board. An old one. There since the Altair calculation system was first built. Julius checked every calculation against it. If the white board showed no error, the calculation was complete.

On the white board, not 97 but 100 was written.

“It must be 100.”

“There is measurement error.”

“Measure completely and it is 100.”

Claire looked at it.

100%. No error. The number reached when measurement is complete.

“When does it become 100.”

“When you measure completely.”

“Is complete measurement possible.”

Julius looked at Claire.

“Calculating that is Altair.”

There was a pause.

“It is mealtime.”

“Calculating.”

Claire left. Julius looked at the white board. 100. No error.

Since that day, Claire had never been satisfied with 97%. There was always 3% left. She always found the reason. She always headed for 100.

Now it was not the 3% but the premise itself that wavered.

On the white calculation board, 100 was written. It was Julius’s. In a place Claire did not see.

-----

*Calculation-02 — end*

-----

## Order-02 — “Execution”

*Placement: after Vol.5 Ch.42*
*POV: Muyul / inside Geumhui*

-----

A request for execution authorization came up.

Hwagam said the identities of the moving parties related to Sector 7 had been partly confirmed. Hwagam requested cooperation in execution.

Muyul read the document.

A subordinate stood before him.

“Please grant the execution authorization.”

“Hold.”

“It is Hwagam’s request for cooperation.”

“Hold.”

“May I ask the reason.”

“Kangwi has not yet.”

The subordinate paused a moment.

“Shall I confirm with Kangwi.”

“There is no need to confirm.”

“How do you know.”

Muyul looked at the subordinate.

“When you do not know, you hold.”

The subordinate left.

Muyul sealed the document. He placed it in the cabinet of unexecuted sealed documents. There were many documents in this cabinet. Hwagam’s requests, Eidos’s requests, Altair’s analysis results. All on hold. Without reason. Without duration.

“Muyul.”

The words came from somewhere in the space.

Muyul closed the cabinet.

“Yes.”

“The Sector 7 report.”

Muyul picked up the report.

“I heard it yesterday.”

Muyul set the report down.

“Let Hwagam move first.”

“Yes.”

“Altair too.”

“Yes.”

“Eidos too.”

“Yes.”

“The ones who move can be seen.”

There was a silence.

Muyul stood before the cabinet. Kangwi could not be seen. He was somewhere in the space. No one had asked where.

What Hwagam disliked was that Eidos only watched. What Eidos disliked was that Hwagam intervened too much. Altair judged both inefficient.

Geumhui judged all three loud.

Muyul picked up the next document.

-----

*Order-02 — end*

-----

## Fragment-01 — “Direction”

*Placement: after Vol.5 Ch.42*
*POV: Dohee and the Observer*

-----

It was night.

The Observer asked.

“Why do you record.”

Dohee paused a moment.

“I don’t know.”

“Good.”

Dohee looked at the Observer.

“It isn’t good.”

“Why.”

“Because I couldn’t answer.”

“Knowing you couldn’t answer is the beginning.”

Dohee said no more.

The logbook was in the pack. She had read as far as the first page. The second page, not yet.

Not knowing. That was all Dohee could record now.

-----

*Fragment-01 — end*

-----

============================================================

# SCALAR: NODE ZERO

## BA-01 “Seoan’s Eye”

*POV: Seoan (Hwagam)*
*Event: Hwagam vs Geumhui — before Vol.6*
*Placement: retroactive insertion possible between Vol.6 and Vol.7*

-----

An order came.

It was Hwagam. The Hwagam headquarters was always bright. A place where the lights never went out. Orders came down day and night, and so the lights never went out either.

Enter the Geumhui records hall. The reason was not in the document. Seoan read it. If there was no reason, one had to ask.

“There is no reason.”

The superior said.

“You don’t need one.”

Seoan heard it.

“Execution must have grounds.”

“The order is the grounds.”

Seoan paused a moment.

“Understood.”

-----

The Geumhui records hall’s outskirts.

There were two. The Hwagam execution detail. Seoan was the senior. They confirmed the entry route. The records hall was sealed. From outside, the inside could not be seen.

“How do we get in.”

The junior asked.

“If we have to get in, a way appears.”

-----

There was a clash.

Someone came out from the Geumhui side. It was not Kangwi. It was someone Kangwi had sent. There was a document. A sealed document. Entry forbidden.

Seoan looked.

“Does the Hwagam order take precedence, or the Geumhui seal.”

“It is a Geumhui seal document.”

“It is a Hwagam order.”

-----

It was not fierce.

A thing hard to call a battle. It was a clash. The blocking and the trying-to-pass. Between the two, the thing happened.

It was Seoan’s left eye.

Seoan blocked. She could not block completely.

Metal grazed. The left field of vision reddened. Something hot ran down along the eye. The pain came one beat late.

Seoan did not close the eye. Did not stop either. Close it, and she could not see; unable to see, she could not block. With half a field of vision she blocked to the end. What Seoan learned that day was one thing. The body knew its own limits more exactly than orders did.

-----

They entered.

As far as the records hall’s outskirts. Not as far as the inside. The order was entry. Entry was done.

Returning, she checked the eye.

A scar. Not deep. The sight remained. But it was a scar.

She wrote the report.

*Entry complete. Interior unconfirmed. Hwagam–Geumhui boundary clash occurred. Injuries: 1.*

When she met Kangwi later, Kangwi looked at Seoan’s eye. Seoan looked at Kangwi too. Neither spoke.

-----

*BA-01 “Seoan’s Eye” — end*

============================================================

# Volume 6 — Convergence Arc

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.43 “The Records Hall”

-----

Someone was reading documents.

There was no sound. The Geumhui records hall was always quiet. The ceiling was high — how high could not be seen, for the light did not reach it. A structure that rose vertically. The walls were full of record cases. Left and right, above and below. The cases ran on like stairs, and the stairs ran on like cases. Which was record and which was passage, a person coming for the first time could not tell.

The archivists kept their places.

The archivists did not speak. They spoke only when something was needed. When nothing was needed, each did their own work. Sorting documents, ordering the cases, drawing out or replacing requested materials. The time of the records hall was different from the time outside — even when outside ran fast, here it was constant.

There was a person reading documents.

One table, one chair. Somewhere on the records hall’s middle level. The other archivists did not go near that person. As if they knew that place had to stay empty.

The document was thick. The reading was not fast. One page, one page. All of it was read. No marks were left. No pages folded. What was read was read. Without marks, it was remembered.

-----

One archivist approached carefully.

The younger one. He seemed not long assigned to the records hall. He came carrying a document, trying the whole way to soften his footsteps. He seemed to think no sound should be made near Kangwi.

A new document. An external report.

“Something has come up.”

He set it down. Stepped back.

The one reading the records picked up the new document. Opened it. A Hwagam report. Concerning the Sector 7 outskirts. Moving parties unidentified. Pursuit in progress.

“Hwagam says they still haven’t caught them.”

Another archivist said. In a very low voice.

The one reading set the document down.

“I know.”

-----

A second document came.

Eidos. Signal-tracking results. The unclassifiable signal had vanished from the Eidos observation net. Two position estimates. Eidos was expanding its observation range.

“Eidos is widening its range.”

“I know.”

-----

A third document.

Altair. Independent entry. Calculation results inconsistent. Pursuit premises under revision.

“Altair says it has revised its premises.”

“I know.”

The archivist stopped.

“Hwagam, Eidos, and Altair are all in the same zone. What will we do.”

It was quiet enough that the young archivist’s swallowing could be heard. The older archivist knew how this question ended, and did not stop his sorting hands.

The one reading looked at the archivist.

This was the moment. Receiving that gaze, the archivist felt in earnest that this person was looking at him. The expression was as usual. Calm. Not threatening. And the archivist knew from that gaze that the question was wrong.

“The question is wrong.”

“…My apologies.”

“It is not that we do not intervene. We are still recording.”

-----

The archivist withdrew.

The records hall went quiet again. Nothing had changed. The archivists continued each their own work.

That person picked up a document again. This time a different one. This time one that had not been requested. One drawn out directly. From somewhere in the records hall, from a position known without any marking.

Before drawing it out, he bound the document he had been reading with a black cord. A thin cord, and the motion was practiced. He bound the document — paused a moment — untied it again. And bound it again. The archivists sometimes saw the act. They did not ask why. Kangwi himself had never explained. Binding was closing; untying was confirming. The hand that confirms once more before closing. It was the motion thirty years had made.

One archivist saw it.

It was material concerning the Echo Sites. Not recent. Old.

Kangwi.

He opened the material.

-----

Echo Sites. The script patterns.

In the records were the time of discovery, the locations, the analysis results. In the analysis field — undecipherable, it said. Hwagam had attempted it. Eidos had observed it. Altair had calculated it. All undecipherable.

The last field.

*Geumhui review.*

Below it.

*Hold.*

Kangwi looked at the field.

Hold meant still recording. At Geumhui, hold was not abandonment. When more information accumulated, it would be drawn out again.

An archivist was beside him.

“A report concerning R-02 has also come up.”

A different archivist. The older one. Not fearless so much as — accustomed. The way of one who had worked near Kangwi a long time. Handing over the document, he waited a moment. As if he wanted to say something more.

Kangwi took the document.

The flooded transit network. Movement routes confirmed. Unidentified parties moving. And — markings.

“Markings, you say.”

“Discovered on the inner walls of the Sector 7 transit network. Unclassifiable.”

Kangwi looked at the field.

Markings. Unclassifiable. A form resembling Geumhui’s proprietary record format.

The archivist said:

“Hwagam classified them as evidence. Eidos ruled them unobservable. Altair processed them as a variable.”

“And Geumhui?”

The archivist paused a moment.

“Not yet processed.”

“The word yet is not right,” Kangwi said. “It is being recorded.”

-----

There was one more old item.

One Kangwi had drawn out himself. The innermost part of the records hall, the lowest level. A place even the archivists did not often go. The light was scarcer. The sound, scarcer still.

On the item’s cover was a classification.

Access grade: restricted.

Kangwi opened it.

The first line of the body.

*Sector 7.*

Next.

*Observer.*

The next field.

*Disposed.*

Kangwi looked at the field.

It was old. The making of this material was old. From before the present house system was established. Even then, someone had recorded this. And processed it as disposed.

Kangwi turned to the next page.

The next page was blank. There was no content. Not that there was no content — the content had been erased. There were traces. The place of something that had been there and been deleted.

“It was not disposed of.”

Kangwi said. It was not said to the archivist. Not said to the document either. He simply said it.

An archivist heard it from far off.

“Muyul.”

Kangwi called.

Somewhere in the space there was a presence. Muyul was inside the records hall. Since when, the archivist could not tell.

“Yes.”

“Reclassify this material as an active record.”

“It is material processed as disposed.”

“Disposal is an execution record. Execution requires grounds.”

There was a silence.

“Are there grounds.”

“There are none.”

Muyul said no more. He took the material. He asked no further questions.

-----

The records hall was quiet.

Kangwi returned to his place. Sat at the table. The document he had been reading was there. He picked it up again.

Hwagam executes. Eidos observes. Altair calculates.

Geumhui keeps.

Execution ends. Observation ends too. Calculation ends too. Only the record remains.

To keep was not to lose. Disposed did not mean gone. Ended did not mean closed. As long as the record existed, it could return. As long as the record existed, it could be seen again. That was Geumhui. That was the reason Kangwi held this seat.

There was an hour when the light changed. When morning crossed into evening. The light that had been white tilted slowly to orange. The color of the walls changed, and the shadows lengthened. ARIA liked that hour. No one asked why.

Observer. Sector 7. Echo Sites. R-02. Dohee. Hangyeol. ARIA. Claire. Min Siwoo.

Names Kangwi knew. Things that had repeated many times inside the records. Where they were now did not matter. Where they would appear again mattered. What was left in the records did not disappear. Someday it appeared again. It always had.

An archivist brought yet another document.

Kangwi did not take it.

“I am still reading.”

The archivist set it down and withdrew.

Kangwi closed the record.

Someone thinks it has disappeared. Someone thinks it has ended.

The record does not think so.

-----

*Ch.43 “The Records Hall” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.44 “Execution”

-----

Muyul left the records hall.

At the boundary where the corridor’s dark gave way to the light outside, he narrowed his eyes once. On days he had been long in the records hall, the outside light was always strong.

One Geumhui subordinate asked, following:

“Are you going yourself.”

“The record has moved.”

The subordinate stopped. Muyul did not stop.

-----

They moved.

Geumhui’s way of moving was unlike Hwagam’s. Hwagam moved leaving marks — zone markers, checkpoints, reporting routes. Muyul did not. When there was a destination, he moved. He left no marks along the way.

The subordinate asked, following:

“Where are we going.”

“An Echo Site.”

“Which number.”

“The fifth.”

The subordinate took out the terrain materials. Checked. It was a place not on the terrain map.

“It is not on the map.”

“I know.”

“How will you get there.”

Muyul said, without changing direction:

“There is a record.”

-----

When they reached the fifth site, no one was there anymore.

Muyul went inside the structure. The subordinate watched the outside. He did not come in. There had been no word to come in.

Inside the structure it was quiet. Muyul looked at the wall.

There was script. Scratched marks. Muyul did not calculate them or analyze them. Nor did he look long. Confirming did not take long.

Looking long was the Eidos way. Calculating was the Altair way. Muyul did neither. What Muyul did was one thing — confirming what was not there. If what should be there was not, it had been erased. If something had been erased, someone had erased it. That was all.

He looked at what was not there.

The place where it should have been. The place among the other characters where something should have been and was not. The surface was different — handled more recently than the other marks. It had been erased.

“Erased, then.”

The subordinate, perhaps having heard from outside, came in carefully.

“What was erased.”

“I don’t know.”

The subordinate paused a moment.

“But you know it was erased.”

“There are traces.”

The subordinate tried to understand what the words meant. Muyul did not seem likely to explain. He never had. The subordinate gave up and looked at the wall. To him, only nothing showed.

Muyul left the structure.

-----

Outside, a junior Geumhui execution squad was waiting.

Three of them. Lower in rank than Muyul. They had come to support the movement. The team leader reported.

“Surrounding search complete. A Hwagam pursuit unit is moving in the same direction. They appear to be tracking the moving parties.”

Muyul heard it.

“Do we track them as well.”

“Observation only.”

“No arrests.”

“There are no grounds.”

The team leader paused a moment.

“It concerns the Observer. It is in the records too. Could that not serve as grounds.”

“And.”

The team leader looked at Muyul. What the word and meant — execute if there are grounds, or so what of it — he could not tell.

“Does it serve as grounds for execution.”

“No.”

“Why not.”

Muyul looked at the team leader.

“The record tells us the Observer was here. It does not tell us what the Observer did. If we do not know what was done, there are no grounds for execution.”

The team leader started to write it down.

“You need not record it.”

The team leader stopped.

“Remember it.”

Muyul began moving again.

The team leader started to say something and stopped. In the end he said nothing. There was nothing to do but follow.

-----

At night, Muyul wrote the report.

There was a format. The Geumhui execution report form. There were fields — destination, movement route, on-site confirmations, execution status.

The execution status field.

*Hold. Grounds insufficient.*

Muyul filled the field. Moved to the next.

Registered items.

Observer. ARIA. Echo Sites. R-02. Dohee.

Muyul confirmed each item. They were existing ones. Kangwi knew them, and they were in the records hall.

The next.

Hangyeol.

Muyul paused a moment.

He added a new item. Name. Manner of action. Traces at the Echo Sites.

The next cell.

*Classification:*

Muyul looked at the cell.

He started to write variable — and stopped.

“Not a variable.”

He said it alone.

“Not an observation subject either.”

A moment.

“Not yet.”

He did not write it. He left the classification cell empty. The same way as a field without grounds for execution. Not filling was Muyul’s way. If it cannot be filled, do not fill it. If unknown, leave it as unknown.

He closed the report.

Tomorrow it would go up to Kangwi. Kangwi would already know. That Muyul had been here, and what he had seen on site. He would send it up anyway. Records do not exist for those who know. They exist so that those who do not know may someday know.

Geumhui keeps.

Muyul was the one who executed that.

-----

*Ch.44 “Execution” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.45 “Observation”

-----

The Eidos observatory stood on high ground.

The highest point of the terrain. Not a building but an installation — it had walls and a roof, but it was designed so that the view was blocked in no direction. There was observation equipment. There were screens. Numbers flowed.

The observers sat in their places.

Deeper inside the observatory was Tiko. He was receiving a report.

“It concerns Min Siwoo.”

The reporter said.

“His dwell time at the dead observation network is abnormal. He is not moving from the site. It seems he is not tracking the subject — he is looking at the place where the subject was.”

Tiko looked at the screen. Min Siwoo’s position data.

“Min Siwoo is being strange.”

“He is not strange.”

The reporter stopped.

“An abnormal dwell time has been recorded.”

“I said he is not strange.”

The reporter said no more.

-----

Two Eidos observers talked in the corridor after the report ended.

“Tiko. Do you drink coffee.”

“I don’t.”

“Why not.”

“I can’t sleep.”

“Coffee is what you drink to stay awake, though.”

“I know.”

The observer said no more. Tiko passed by.

-----

Later, another observer asked.

“Tiko. When do you rest.”

“I am resting.”

“You’re working right now.”

“I know.”

That observer, too, said no more.

-----

Tiko pulled up Min Siwoo’s logs.

Observation records. Sorted by date. He read from the most recent.

There was an entry.

*What does the subject see.*

The next entry.

*What does the subject see.*

Next.

*What does the subject see.*

It repeated. The same entry, twelve times. It was not observation content. It was an observation question. Min Siwoo was not observing the subject — he was trying to observe what the subject saw.

“Not good.”

Tiko said. In a room with no one in it.

-----

One Eidos observer came in.

“Have you checked the logs.”

“I have.”

“How will you handle it.”

“What has Min Siwoo done wrong.”

The observer paused a moment.

“By Eidos standards, he is departing from the subject observation range.”

“It is not departure,” Tiko said. “The range is narrowing.”

The observer seemed not to understand.

“You must look at where the subject is. You must not look at where the subject looks.”

“Why not.”

“The moment you look, it is no longer observation. It is participation.”

The observer heard it. After a long while he said:

“But wouldn’t knowing make it more accurate.”

“Accuracy is not the purpose.”

“Then what is.”

“Not being distorted is the purpose.”

Looking at what the subject looks at — why that was participation took time to understand. Looking the same direction as the subject. Looking at the same thing. That was the observer coming to stand in the subject’s position.

“And Min Siwoo, right now.”

“Is looking the same direction.”

The observer asked no more.

-----

Tiko moved to the site.

The outskirts of the dead observation network. Near the place Min Siwoo had dwelled. Tiko did not look at the site. He looked at the traces of the one who had looked at the site.

Min Siwoo’s traces read first.

He had stayed long. Had not moved. Had kept looking the same direction. The direction of the footprints was constant — they faced one way only. The direction where the Observer had been.

Then the Observer’s traces read.

The Observer was already far. Had moved. To somewhere not visible from here.

Tiko looked at the gap between the two sets of traces.

The Observer was receding. Min Siwoo was narrowing. Over the same subject, two people were going in different directions.

Tiko went to the place where Min Siwoo had stood long. The place where the footprints had pressed in. He looked the direction that had been looked at from there. The direction where the Observer would have been.

A few seconds passed.

There was something. Something that made one want to look. The sense that something was in that direction. Tiko, knowing it, drew his gaze back.

“That is the problem.”

He said it alone.

Min Siwoo would not have drawn his gaze back.

“Too late, then.”

Tiko said.

-----

After returning, he filled in the records.

Observer. Observing.
ARIA. Observing.
Geumhui. Observing.

Tiko wrote a name in the last entry.

Min Siwoo.

The next cell.

*Re-observation required.*

The classification cell.

*Distance loss. In progress.*

One more entry below it.

*Observer-subject boundary unstable.*

Tiko closed the record.

An Eidos observer keeps distance from the subject. That was the condition on which observation was possible. Too close, and it was not observation. The observer became part of the subject.

The direction Min Siwoo was going now was that way.

Tiko knew it.

He lowered the lens and looked the same direction for a moment with the naked eye. To the naked eye there was nothing. What exists only in the lens, and what exists to the naked eye too. They were eyes that had spent a lifetime looking at the things in between.

Whether he could still be stopped — could not be known.

-----

*Ch.45 “Observation” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.46 “Premises”

-----

One more premise was added.

Claire was calculating. Since the result had narrowed two days before with the added premise, she had kept adding. The more the premises grew, the more precise the calculation became. The margin of error shrank. The route candidates shrank.

But the conviction shrank.

Claire found the inversion strange. Precision and conviction were things that went together. If there was a point where they did not, then from that point something other than calculation was beginning.

Claire thought about it for a long time. The more premises are added, the more accurate the calculation. Then why does conviction shrink. If the calculation grew accurate, conviction should grow. By the Altair way, it did.

Now it was different.

Each time she added a premise, she had to ask whether the premise itself was right. With one premise, verification was simple. When the premises became ten — all ten had to be confirmed right. If even one was wrong, the whole calculation wavered.

Claire closed the screen. Briefly.

-----

There had been her trainee days.

The Altair calculation hall. The first place Claire entered. Julius’s training program. From the first day it was calculation. When a calculation ended, the next calculation.

Twenty-four hours passed and Julius said:

“Rest.”

Claire stopped mid-calculation.

“Why.”

“A broken calculator cannot calculate.”

“I am not a calculator.”

Julius looked at Claire.

“Not yet.”

It took Claire time to understand what the words meant. Not yet. Not a calculator now — but possibly becoming one, someday. It was not a good meaning. It was a warning.

She rested.

-----

From the same days, there was a thing Julius had said.

“Calculation is not a technique for finding answers.”

Claire had stopped then too.

“Then what is it.”

“A technique for reducing questions.”

“Is there a difference.”

“A large one,” Julius said. “Find the answer and it ends. Reduce the questions and there is a next.”

Claire had not understood it. At the time. If a calculation produced an answer, it was success. If questions remained, the calculation was insufficient.

She had not understood it then. She did not completely understand it now.

Now — Claire had added ten premises and the questions had not shrunk.

What Julius had said might have been this.

-----

She opened the screen again.

She sorted the premises.

*The subject does not select the optimal route.*

This one was already there.

*The subject selects the route that lowers the probability of discovery.*

This one too.

Claire added a new entry.

*The subject is leading someone.*

The result changed.

The route candidates shrank. Shrank greatly. One of the directions that had refused to narrow until now disappeared. What remained was close to one.

Claire looked at the entry.

*The subject is leading someone.*

It was a premise of the calculation. But it was not a number. It was an intent. She had entered the other’s intent as a premise.

At Altair, a premise had to be a verified fact. Intent could not be verified. Entering what cannot be verified as a premise — a method Claire had never been taught.

But a result had come out.

Claire stopped.

For the first time, she had entered not calculation but the other’s intent as a premise. And the calculation had narrowed. A wrong method, and a result came. A right method, and no result came.

Claire recorded it. Wrote it down.

*Can intent be a premise.*

It was a question. Not a calculation.

-----

She kept calculating.

She followed the remaining route. Terrain data, movement traces, time estimates. Everything pointed the same direction. Claire estimated the end of that direction.

There was a point.

It was not on the terrain map. Not in the official records. A place classified into no house’s territory.

But the calculation pointed there.

Claire marked the spot. It had no name. No classification. No place in Claire’s table.

But the routes headed there.

Dohee, Hangyeol, ARIA, the Observer — every movement read as centered on that spot. The things Claire had calculated for days converged on it.

A convergence point.

Claire wrote the entry.

*Variable count increasing. Convergence point found. Convergence point identity unknown. Who is being led unknown.*

Subjects that had never moved the same direction were pointing, for the first time, at a single spot. Dohee, Hangyeol, ARIA, the Observer. Every trace Claire had followed for days converged on the same place.

That a convergence point existed. That was all Claire could do for now.

-----

A communication connected.

It was Julius.

A space showed on the screen. The calculation hall. Julius was not in front of the screen — after a moment he appeared.

“You are late.”

Claire said.

“I was calculating.”

“Have you eaten.”

Julius paused a moment.

“…I was calculating.”

“And sleep.”

“I was calculating.”

“That is not an answer.”

“I know.”

Claire heard it.

“I have a report.”

“Speak.”

“I revised the premises. I used an unverified premise. A result came out.”

There was a pause on the far side of the connection.

“An unverified premise produced a result.”

“Yes.”

“What is the premise.”

“Intent.”

“Is intent verifiable.”

“No.”

“Then it is not calculation.”

Claire paused a moment.

“But a result came out.”

“A result coming out and being calculation are different things.”

There was a silence. Claire did not argue. She did not think Julius was wrong. He was right. By the Altair standard, what Claire had done was not calculation.

“Continue.”

Claire heard it.

He does not acknowledge it. Continue anyway. It was not that Julius had permitted it. He had tolerated her going on, wrong. Claire knew the difference.

Claire looked at the convergence point on the screen. A place without a name. A place without a classification.

The calculation said she had to go there.

-----

*Ch.46 “Premises” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.47 “The Gaze”

-----

Min Siwoo came to the dead observation network again.

There was no reason. No reason that should have been there. The Observer had already left this zone. The traces were old. There was no new data. By the Eidos way, there was no longer a reason to be here.

But he came.

The feet came first and the reason did not follow. For Min Siwoo, it was a first. Eidos observation departed from reasons. He had been taught that observation without reason was not observation. He was now doing something other than what he had been taught.

He went into the tower. Climbed the stairs. Stood before the window. The place where the Observer had looked from, long.

The Observer’s position he knew roughly. The direction too. It matched the direction of the convergence point Claire had calculated. He could just go that way.

But right now that did not matter.

Min Siwoo, knowing it, stood before the window. Not where the Observer was. What the Observer had seen from here — that was what he wanted to know. That question was larger. It was the reason he had come here while knowing the direction.

Min Siwoo did not record it.

-----

Two hours passed.

A notification came on the terminal. The Eidos automatic tracking system. Two hours earlier there had been a movement signal north of this zone. An Observer-class signal.

Two hours earlier.

The hour Min Siwoo had arrived here. While Min Siwoo was before the window — at the same hour, there had been a signal.

He had missed it.

Min Siwoo looked at the terminal. The signal from two hours before. Position, direction, speed estimate. All there. The automatic system had recorded it. Min Siwoo had not. Because he had been before the window, looking the direction the Observer had looked.

It was a first.

Min Siwoo missing a signal he should have observed. The reason for missing it being that he was following the subject’s gaze.

He stopped. For a long time.

-----

Tiko’s records updated.

The entry concerning Min Siwoo.

*Departure from observation subject continuing. Distance loss in progress. Observer-subject boundary unstable.*

Below it.

*He can still return.*

*No grounds.*

Tiko looked at the entry. The one who had written it was himself — and there was no conviction. That he could still return was an estimate. There was no data. In the Eidos records there was no case of an observer who lost the boundary and returned.

So he wrote it.

That the word still was not conviction but hope — Tiko did not record.

-----

He came down from the tower.

He followed the traces of which direction the Observer had moved after this tower. North of the tower. Below a low slope, a sunken place. It looked like a place of a brief stop.

There was something there.

A small thing. Between the stones. Not deliberately hidden — simply left behind. An old lens. An optical lens, already useless. It was cracked. In no state to be used for observation.

The Observer had carried it and left it.

Min Siwoo looked at it.

He had to write it in the report. Place of discovery, description of the object, state of preservation. The Eidos manner of field recording. Then photograph it and leave it in place. An observer does not alter the site.

Min Siwoo picked up the lens.

He had to put it back. Min Siwoo knew it. He tried to set it down. His hand did not move.

Holding it, Min Siwoo knew it was wrong. Knowing, he did not set it down. The Observer would have carried it. Would have tried to see with this lens. There would have been a reason for carrying it even now that it could not be used.

Min Siwoo wanted to know what that was.

So he held it.

-----

Moving, Min Siwoo thought.

If I see the same thing — could I understand together with them.

What the Observer saw, if Min Siwoo looked from the same place in the same direction. What the Observer held, if Min Siwoo held it. If I am closer, could I know more.

That this was not the Eidos way, Min Siwoo knew.

Eidos keeps distance from the subject. Lose the distance and it is not observation but participation. They were Tiko’s words. They were the Eidos doctrine too.

Min Siwoo thought the words were right.

And he thought that understanding could matter more than observation.

The two thoughts stood together. It was a contradiction. But he did not think it was wrong.

Min Siwoo did not record the contradiction.

-----

He found the place where the Observer would have last stood.

The side far from the tower. Open terrain. From here one could see more widely. The Observer would have looked from this place.

Min Siwoo stood in it.

There was something ahead.

A direction. A direction where something would be. It could not be seen. The distance was far. Claire’s convergence point lay that way. Where Hangyeol and ARIA were heading lay that way. What the Observer would have seen lay that way too.

Min Siwoo looked, long.

He raised the lens. The cracked one. He could not see through it. He raised it anyway. As the Observer had held it.

Far off there was something.

What it was could not be seen. Only that direction kept entering his eyes. Even when he moved his gaze elsewhere, it returned.

-----

He opened the records.

There had been a moment when what the Observer saw became more important than where the Observer was. Since when, he could not remember.

*What the Observer saw. Confirmation required.*

The next line.

*Subject observation. Hold.*

He put the lens in his pack. A thing not returned to its place. A thing not written in the records. Min Siwoo knew it was the first.

He began to move.

The direction was toward the convergence point.

-----

*Ch.47 “The Gaze” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.48 “Convergence”

-----

There was an end to R-02.

Dohee saw it first. The tunnel began to rise. A gradient formed. The water lessened. From ankle to instep, from instep to gone. The floor was dry tile.

That person, ahead, slowed.

It was an exit. An old one. It looked as if it had been sealed and someone had opened it. Not a door — a wall standing open. Beyond it there was light.

They came outside.

Dohee stopped.

The terrain was strange. A place seen for the first time — that did not feel like a first. Something was familiar. Dohee looked around.

There were marks on a wall. The same kind as seen in R-02. Beside them, marks made the same way but in a different form — the kind seen at the Echo Sites. Farther off, the ruins of a tower showed. Of the dead observation network’s line.

“It was strange from here on.”

Dohee said. It was not said to that person.

Everything seen until now was all here. The things that had been separate. The things she had not thought would connect.

That person looked ahead.

There was a further inside.

Dohee watched the way that person stopped. It was not the taking of a bearing. They looked at one place, long. For the first time — the first time as far as Dohee had seen — that person looked long. Not reaching a judgment, but doing the looking itself.

For Dohee it was a first. For that person, it seemed to be a first too.

-----

Hangyeol and ARIA came down the slope.

ARIA in front, Hangyeol behind. By now that was the default. ARIA knew the direction and Hangyeol made the direction safe.

Below the slope there was a place.

ARIA stopped.

There was a wall. There were structures. The same as the Echo Sites — and different. Larger. Not one. Several structures stood connected. Some had collapsed and some remained.

And on the walls there was script.

ARIA went close.

The three marks were there. The thing that had been present without fail from the first site until now. ARIA looked at it. She looked at the other characters around it too. The things that had differed site by site were all here. The first site’s were here. The second’s were here. The third, fourth, fifth. All of them.

“It started from here.”

ARIA said.

Hangyeol looked at her.

“How do you know.”

“I don’t,” ARIA said. “I just know. It went out from here. This is the original place.”

Why, she did not say. She could not explain. Knowing and being unable to explain — ARIA was growing used to it.

Hangyeol watched the surroundings. There was nothing dangerous. Not yet.

-----

Claire arrived.

She had moved without stopping the calculation. Calculated while moving. Kept narrowing the route to the convergence point. For the last several dozen meters there was conviction. Not 91 percent. 97. The number Julius had spoken of.

And she arrived.

She stopped.

The calculation and reality coincided. The convergence point was here. What Claire had calculated for days was right. That was success.

But it made no sense.

There were structures. There were marks. Traces of several kinds stood in one place. Claire looked at them. Entries that were separated in the calculation table — R-02’s, the Echo Sites’, the observation network’s. Things of different origins stood in the same place.

They should not be in the same place.

In Claire’s premises, these were independent of one another. Unconnected. And they were connected. She had found by calculation that a convergence point existed — and the convergence point’s contents would not be explained by calculation.

“It makes no sense.”

Claire said.

There was no one to hear it. Only Claire was there.

She took out the calculator. She tried to classify what was before her eyes, entry by entry. No classification came out. Things that should go in the same entry differed. Things that should go in different entries were the same.

For the first time, Claire did not make a table. And she did not record. Because she did not know what to write.

She only looked.

-----

From a distance, Min Siwoo watched.

The top of the slope. He did not go down. He wanted to go down. Knowing it, he did not.

People were arriving. Dohee was there. Hangyeol was there. ARIA was there. Someone else too — Claire. A combination seen for the first time. People who had come from different directions were arriving at the same place.

That person was there too.

The Observer. The one Min Siwoo had tracked so long.

Min Siwoo took out the lens. The cracked one. The one that could not be seen through. He raised it anyway and set the direction. Toward the Observer.

He could not see.

He just looked that direction.

Tiko would not have gone down. Min Siwoo deliberated a moment. Then he began going down.

-----

The inside of the structures.

It was like a facility. And it was not a facility. Like ruins. And not ruins either. There were high places and low places. Some parts looked very old and some looked not old. Whether it had been built or had grown, there was no telling.

Dohee looked at the inside.

There was script on the walls. An unknowable language. Different from the markings of R-02. Different from the Echo Sites’ too. All three kinds were here, and they were not mixed. Each had its own place. Not divided — as if each had been in its place from the start.

Hangyeol stood beside ARIA.

ARIA did not move. She looked at the inside. Looked at the structures. Looked at the walls. She stayed long.

Hangyeol looked at the outside before the inside of the structures. It was quiet. Too quiet. There was no way this many people arriving at this place had gone unnoticed.

And he said it. The voice was as usual. Its being as usual was what made the weight.

“We found it.”

-----

*Ch.48 “Convergence” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.49 “Intervention”

-----

Reports came up to the Geumhui records hall.

Several came up at once. The Hwagam report. The Eidos observation results. The Altair calculation update. And Muyul’s on-site confirmation record.

Kangwi read them in order.

The Echo Core direction. Multiple moving parties. A place Hwagam had failed to identify. Eidos was widening its observation range, and Altair was narrowing its calculation toward the convergence point.

Kangwi closed the documents.

One archivist said carefully:

“All the other houses are moving in the same direction.”

“I know.”

“What will Geumhui do.”

Kangwi paused a moment.

“There is still something unknown.”

The archivist waited.

“Even so, it is enough.”

-----

Kangwi untied the sealing cord.

The black cord. The document he had read and bound before. The Echo Site materials. The old ones. Kangwi opened it again.

Undecipherable. Unanalyzable. It was all written that way.

Kangwi did not look at that. He looked at the date above it. That it was old. That it was old and repeating now.

What repeats has a record. Where there is a record, it can return.

An archivist brought a new document.

“Muyul has completed the position confirmation.”

“I know.”

“Please issue the execution order.”

Kangwi looked at the archivist.

“Hold.”

The archivist stopped. Hold, again. Kangwi had never once ordered an execution directly. There had only been holds.

“The reason for the hold.”

“It is still being recorded.”

The archivist said no more.

Kangwi bound the sealing cord again. Binding it — he untied it. And bound it again. The archivist watched the act. He could not understand it. It had always been so.

Kangwi said:

“Muyul.”

-----

Muyul was moving.

Kangwi’s order came. It was short.

*Secure order.*

That was all. Two words. For Muyul, it was enough.

It was not the execution Hwagam did. Not the observation Eidos did. Not the calculation Altair did. What Muyul did was different. It was making boundaries. Dividing the space where people could move from the space where they could not.

He confirmed the approach routes.

There were three routes entering toward the Echo Core. Muyul deployed his subordinates in the three directions. The order was simple.

“Do not block.”

A subordinate asked.

“Then what.”

“Record.”

“Who comes in.”

“And who comes out.”

The subordinate wrote it down. Not blocking, only recording. An order Hwagam would not have understood, hearing it.

To Muyul it was obvious. Things that move have direction. Where there is direction, there is purpose. Know the purpose and grounds for execution form. Before grounds for execution form, one does not execute.

Geumhui maintains order. To maintain order, one must first know the state. To know the state, records are needed.

Muyul recorded from outside the boundary.

One subordinate asked:

“And if the order collapses.”

“Then we execute.”

The subordinate said no more.

-----

While the boundary was being built, they met a Hwagam advance unit.

It was not unexpected. Deploying the boundary, Muyul had known Hwagam was moving the same direction.

One of the Hwagam advance unit said:

“Step aside. Execution in progress.”

A Geumhui subordinate said:

“There are no grounds.”

“It is the Observer pursuit. There are grounds.”

“Has it been confirmed what the Observer did.”

Hwagam stopped.

“It is not what was done. The problem is where they are.”

“Where they are is not grounds. What was done is grounds.”

Hwagam came forward. The Geumhui subordinate did not step back. Hwagam tried to pass. Geumhui blocked.

It was a brief contact. Hwagam stepped back. Geumhui stepped back too. That was all. No weapons came out. There were no injuries.

Hwagam said:

“This becomes a problem later.”

“We will record it.”

Muyul was briefed on the situation.

“Continue.”

-----

Inside the Echo Core, Dohee looked at the wall.

It was different from before.

It was not that the wall’s characters had changed. Dohee had been looking long, and looking long — an order began to show. It was not random. Which came first and which came after. She could not know it, but that there was an order could be felt.

ARIA put her hand to the wall.

And stopped.

“It’s changed.”

Dohee looked at her.

“What has.”

“This part.” ARIA said, hand still on it. “It’s different from before. It hasn’t changed, but — it’s different.”

Dohee looked at the part. It looked the same. What had become different did not read.

“Different how.”

ARIA paused a moment.

“It responds.”

The moment the words ended, a small sound came from somewhere in the wall. A sound like stone moving. Everyone looked that way at once.

Nothing happened.

Hangyeol moved a step in that direction and stopped. There was nothing. ARIA, hand off the wall, looked at it. That person did not turn around.

Hangyeol kept watching the entrance.

Still nothing. Quiet. Hangyeol closed his hand. It was strange. Something seemed about to come. There were no grounds. But the body knew first.

That person looked toward the wall.

A long time. They looked as if they already knew. And then looked once more. It was confirming. They were confirming something.

For the first time, Dohee felt that that person was not certain. Knowing, and having to confirm. There were cases where knowing and confirming were different.

Dohee looked at them.

“When you say it responds.”

That person looked at her.

“What does it mean.”

That person looked at the wall, then at Dohee.

“Next, it comes.”

That was all.

What would come next — that person said no more. Dohee asked no more. Hangyeol kept watching the entrance.

-----

Kangwi spoke in the records hall.

“The ones who move can be seen.”

An archivist heard it.

“Then do we not move.”

Kangwi looked at the archivist.

“Not yet.”

And he said:

“Begin the boundary records.”

Muyul answered.

“Yes.”

This time it was not a hold. The black sealing cord lay over the records. Still bound. But Kangwi’s hand was on the cord. Not the hand that binds and unbinds — the hand that had decided to unbind. The different weight of the same hand, the archivists could tell even from far off.

-----

*Ch.49 “Intervention” — end*

-----

## Vol.6 — Ch.50 “The Recorder”

-----

The figures came up again.

Gadeung opened the report. The past three weeks of data. The tally of anomalous figures by zone. Each sat within the margin of error. By the manual’s standard, figures requiring no handling.

But summed, it was different.

Gadeung looked at it for a long time. He had seen it before. This pattern. Each normal, and the sum abnormal. Before 7-7-4, too, there had been this pattern. Then, Dohee had found it first.

7-7-4.

Gadeung pulled the file out again. The old one. The one Dohee had made. Not through official channels. It had been stored on a personal terminal, then later transferred to the Hwagam archive. There was a reason for the transfer — because Dohee, in the end, had been right.

And at first, it had been within the margin of error.

-----

The new appointee sent up a report.

“I’ve checked the Sector 7 outskirts figures. Within the margin of error.”

Gadeung looked at him.
